Second Aspect: Religions are Beliefs, Not Actions
Maturidi argues that even if sin were obedience, it would not constitute a religion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi defines religions as internal beliefs rather than external actions, and how this distinction prevents misidentifying obedience to Satan as a religion.

Core Idea
Religions are rooted in heartfelt beliefs, not in coerced actions.

Maturidi continues his argument against the hypothetical that obeying Satan could constitute a religion. He establishes a fundamental principle: religions (al-diyānāt) are matters of belief (i'tiqād), not of acquired actions. This distinction is crucial because beliefs cannot be forced, and they reside in the heart. Even if an action were obedience to someone other than God, it would not form a religion without inner conviction. Maturidi supports this with a reported view from Abu Hanifa emphasizing the role of intention.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Start with Maturidi's statement that religions are based on beliefs, not actions. Notice the reasoning: beliefs cannot be coerced.
This sets the premise.
2
Apply the premise to the hypothetical
Maturidi then applies this: even if sin were obedience, it would not become a religion because religion requires belief. He also notes that disbelief itself is a religion of a kind.
The conclusion follows from the premise.
3
Consider Abu Hanifa's response
Review the reported answer from Abu Hanifa emphasizing intention (qasd). The act is not committed with the intention to obey Satan, so it does not constitute disbelief.
Intention is key to identifying religious acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أن الديانات من اعتقادات لا أفعال تكتسب، إذ الاعتقادات لا يجرى عليها القهر والغلبة، ولا لأحد من الخلائق على اعتقاد آخر ومنعه سلطان؛ وهن أفعال القلوب خاصة. وربما كان للألسن بها تعلق من حيث لا يقدر على استعمال لسان غيره وكذلك قلبه، ويقدر على سائر الجوارح. وإذا كانت الديانات كما ذكرنا، والكفر والإيمان دين، لم يصر الذي ذكرت - لو كان طاعة ـ دينا . والكفر دين، فكيف وهو من الوجه الذي ذكرت ليس بطاعة. وقد روى عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله أنه أجاب لهذا السؤال أن الذي ذكرته حق القصد لا حق الوقوع على حال لا يقصد ذلك. وعلى ذلك أمور علقت بالقصد، وذلك يخرج على ما بينا من ترتيب الاعتقادات . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The second is that religions are based on beliefs, not acquired actions. Because beliefs are not subject to compulsion and domination, and no one has authority over another's belief or prevention of it. They are specifically actions of the hearts. And perhaps the tongues have attachment to them from the perspective that one cannot use another's tongue, nor his heart, while one can control other limbs. And since religions are as we mentioned, and disbelief and faith are a religion, then what you mentioned—even if it were obedience—would not become a religion. Disbelief is a religion, so how could it be, while from the aspect you mentioned it is not obedience? And it has been narrated from Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) that he answered this question: that what you mentioned is true of intention, not true of occurrence, when he does not intend that. And upon that are matters attached to intention, and that comes out according to what we have explained of the ordering of beliefs. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
Maturidi's second argument distinguishes religions (adyan) as matters of belief (i'tiqad), not of external actions. Beliefs cannot be coerced; they are internal acts of the heart. Even if a sinful action were considered obedience to Satan, it would still not constitute a religion because religion requires inner conviction. Abu Hanifa's reported answer reinforces that the issue is about intention (qasd): the act is not committed with the intention to obey Satan, so it does not become disbelief.

Key Terms
دين (religion)
اعتقاد (belief)
قصد (intention)
إيمان (faith)
كفر (disbelief)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that committing sinful acts out of obedience to someone other than God could constitute a separate religion.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that religion is fundamentally about internal beliefs and intentions, not merely external actions. An action without the corresponding belief does not form a religion.

Checkpoint
In Maturidi's second aspect, what do religions (الديانات) primarily consist of?
A) Actions of the limbs
B) Beliefs of the heart
C) Verbal declarations
D) Ritual practices

Key Takeaway
Religion is fundamentally a matter of heartfelt belief and intention, not external actions that can be coerced.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship, do you examine what your heart truly believes, or do you rely solely on the external movements? How can you ensure that your actions flow from sincere belief in Allah alone, as this lesson demands?
?
Have you ever felt pressured to conform to a belief or practice that did not resonate with your heart? How does Maturidi’s argument that religion is rooted in inner conviction, not coercion, challenge you to stand firm in truth even when pressured?
?
If disbelief itself is considered a religion, then your own belief in Allah is not merely a set of actions but a heartfelt conviction. What specific steps will you take this week to strengthen that internal belief so that your worship is truly a religion of submission, not just routine?

Degrees of Blameworthy Actions
Maturidi states a general principle about varying ugliness of threatened actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's foundational principle that actions threatened with punishment vary in degrees of ugliness, and that one must use intellect and consult revelation and community consensus before equating different types of sins.

Core Idea
Not all sins are equal – degrees of ugliness require careful discernment.

Maturidi sets down a foundational rule for understanding God's threats of punishment. Actions warned about in revelation are not all alike; they differ in moral weight (ugliness). The intellect can perceive these differences, but one must also check the consensus of the Muslim community and the full scope of revealed texts before grouping different types of blameworthy acts under a single judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall principle.
Notice that the passage begins with 'Then the principle regarding everything that is threatened...'
2
Identify the key terms
Look for the terms 'ugliness' (قبح), 'intellects' (عقول), 'revelation' (سمع), and 'community' (أمة). These are central to the argument.
3
Understand the condition for combining
The passage says one should not combine different blameworthy actions under one judgment unless they either examine the community's understanding or examine all relevant revelation. Ask yourself: why does Maturidi impose this condition?
This condition prevents hasty generalizations based on limited evidence.
4
Reflect on the role of intellect
Maturidi says the intellect is 'honored with knowledge of the difference of locations' of ugliness. Consider how reason and revelation work together in his methodology.
This reflects Maturidi's balanced approach between reason and transmitted knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم الأصل في كل شيء أوعد عليه أن حقيقة ذلك تقع من صاحبه على وجوه من القبح مما يعلم كل تفاوت ذلك في العقول. وكذلك كل اسم جاء به تسمية الفاعل. إن كان ذلك يقتضي مختلفًا من معان لا تعقل لا تكون هي من كل الوجوه على وزن واحد في القبح ولا فاعله في الذم . وذلك غائب عن السامع، فالزمه بعقله الذي أُكرم معرفة اختلاف مواقع ذلك أن لا يجمع بينها إلا أن يمتحن ما عليه الأمة من الأفهام فوجدها حصلت على ذلك، أو يمتحن جميع ما ورد في السمع فوجده محققا لذلك،
Al-Faqih Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: Then the principle regarding everything that is threatened [with punishment] is that its reality occurs from its doer in aspects of ugliness, the variation of which is known by intellects. Likewise, every name that has come for naming the agent. If that requires different meanings that are not rationally understood, they are not from all aspects on one level in ugliness, nor is the doer in blame. And that is hidden from the listener, so bind him by his intellect which has been honored with knowledge of the difference of locations of that, not to combine them unless he examines what the community of understandings is upon and finds it attaining that, or examines all that has come in revelation and finds it verifying that.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage introduces a general principle (الأصل) about actions that are threatened with punishment. Maturidi (referred to as al-Faqih Abu Mansur) states that such actions occur in various degrees of ugliness (قبح), and the intellect can recognize this variation. Similarly, names given to describe the doer of an action can imply different meanings that are not always rationally apparent. These names are not all on the same level of ugliness, nor is the doer equally blameworthy. Since this nuance is hidden from the listener, one must use his intellect—honored by God to discern such differences—to avoid lumping together different types of blameworthy acts unless he first checks the consensus of the Muslim community's understanding (ما عليه الأمة من الأفهام) and finds that they indeed combine them, or examines all revealed texts (السمع) and finds that they confirm that combination. The principle cautions against hasty judgments and emphasizes reliance on both reason and revelation.

Key Terms
أصل
القبح
العقول
السمع
الأمة
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sins are equally blameworthy and should be treated identically.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that sins vary in ugliness and blameworthiness; one must use intellect and consult revelation and community consensus before treating them as equal.

Checkpoint
According to the principle, what should a person do before combining different types of blameworthy actions under one judgment?
A) Rely solely on his own reasoning.
B) Follow only the revealed texts.
C) Examine the understanding of the community or the totality of revelation.
D) Always consider them equal.

Key Takeaway
Blameworthy actions differ in ugliness; one must use intellect and check community/revelation before equating them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone committing a sin you consider 'major', do you automatically assign the same level of blame to them without considering the context or their intentions? How can Maturidi’s principle change your reaction?
?
Have you ever confidently judged an action as forbidden or permissible based solely on your own reasoning, without checking what the Qur’an, Sunnah, or the early community actually said? What would it cost you to examine those sources before forming a verdict?
?
In your own spiritual self-accountability, do you treat all your shortcomings as equally blameworthy, or have you honestly assessed the varying degrees of ugliness? How might that shift your priorities in repentance and self-improvement?

The Believer's Heart and the Nature of Sin
Maturidi describes the believer's heart and its immunity to shirk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a believer's sin, even if grave, cannot be considered worship of other than God, and how the heart's conviction distinguishes the believer from the disbeliever.

Core Idea
A believer's sin remains a failing, not worship of other than God.

Maturidi begins by describing the spiritual state of a true believer: when the heart is filled with awareness of God's blessings and power, it naturally turns away from any other object of obedience or worship. This foundation helps us understand that even when a believer commits a sin, that act does not become obedience or worship of other than God, unlike the disbeliever whose actions are rooted in disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the believer's state
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning to 'ولا قوة إلا بالله'. Let the description of the believer's heart settle before analyzing.
Notice the two aspects of God's delight mentioned at the start.
2
Understand the believer's inner conviction
Maturidi describes that when the believer truly recognizes God's lordship, blessings, and power, his heart is protected from inclining to obedience or worship of another. Ask yourself: what keeps the believer from idolatry?
3
Contrast with the disbeliever
Note how the disbeliever's actions are attributed to Satan or the soul because of his false beliefs. This contrast highlights that the same action has different moral status based on belief.
The passage ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' as a reminder of divine support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنه لا أحد أبغض إليهم منه، ولا شيء أثقل على طباعهم وعقولهم مما فيه سروره بذلك على وجهين. أحدهما في طاعته وعبادته؛ فتجاوز الله عنهم ولذته، فضلاً عن العاجل بمنع اسم الطاعة والعبادة له؛ والثاني بإطماع المغفرة والتجاوز، بما آثر عداوة الشيطان في وقت عصيانه ورجاء رحمة رب العالمين المعروف بالكرم والجود الذي لم يزل يعودهم بإحسانه إليهم وإفضاله عليهم له الحمد على ذلك أوفره.

وبعد، فإن العبد إذا اعتقد طاعة الرب، وعرف العبودية، وأشعر قلبه عظيم نعمه عليه وآلائه لديه، ثم أراه عظيم سلطانه وقدرته بما ذكره حكمته في خلقه ونفاذ مشيئته فيه كف أن تميل إلى طاعة من لا يكون طاعته طاعته، وصانها عن توهم عبادة دونه. فلم يجز صرف فعله الواقع منه - بعد أن اطمأن قلبه على هذا وصار ذا آثر عنده الدنيا من والآخرة - لشهوة غلبته أو لرحمة يأملها أو لأمر دفعه إليها، إلى طاعة لغيره أو عبادة منه أحدا دونه، وما ذكرته هو لازم قلبه وقت فعله. وإنما يكون مثله من الكافر الذي اعتقد طاعة من دونه وعبادة من لا يستحقها، أن يُصرف ذلك إلى من به صار إلى ذلك من الشيطان أو النفس. ولا قوة إلا بالله.

قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم الأصل في كل شيء أوعد عليه أن حقيقة ذلك تقع من صاحبه على وجوه من القبح مما يعلم كل تفاوت ذلك في العقول. وكذلك كل اسم جاء به تسمية الفاعل. إن كان ذلك يقتضي مختلفًا من معان لا تعقل تكون هي من كل الوجوه على وزن واحد في القبح ولا فاعله في الذم. وذلك غائب عن السامع، فالزمه بعقله الذي أُكرم معرفة اختلاف مواقع ذلك أن لا يجمع بينها إلا أن يمتحن ما عليه الأمة من الأفهام فوجدها حصلت على ذلك، أو يمتحن جميع ما ورد في السمع فوجده محققا لذلك، أو يجعله يحتمل الإحاطة بكل فنون الحكمة فوجد ذلك يضيق عن التخصيص ويلزمه القول بالعموم. فأما أن يحصل على المخرج من العموم في القضاء وقد علم أن ذلك لو كان حقا في الحكمة أو واجبًا في التدبير ليجد أهل الإلحاد أوضح طعن في القرآن وأيسر سبيل إلى القول بأنه غير منزل من عند الرحمن؛ إذ به وصفه أنه ولو كان من عند غير الله لوجدوا فيه اختلافا كثيرًا (النساء: 82)، وقال: ولا يأتيه الباطل من بين يديه ولا من خلفه (فصلت: 42) الآية، وقال: إنا نحن نزلنا الذكر وإنا له لحافظون (الحجر: 9)، ثم وجد أكثر ما فيه من الحكم منصرِفًا إلى غير المخرج ومحصلاً على غير مجرى اللفظ من العموم والخصوص - وذلك على هذا القول صرف عن طريق الحكمة ومزيل حق التدبير. جل الله عن أن يلحق حجته هذا الوصف أو دليله هذا التناقض. ثم قد بين جل ثناؤه - لما أرسل من الأسماء المحمودة والمذمومة - المقابلات التي لديها يظهر لزوم حق صرف المطلق من ذلك، فقال: إن الأبرار لفي نعيم وإن الفجار لفي جحيم (الانفطار: 13-14)، ثم وصفهم فقال: كلا إن كتاب الفجار لفي سجين (المطففين: 7) إلى آخر السورة؛ فبين الفاجر المطلق المقصود بالوعيد وما منه من التكذيب، والبر تركه لما قد بينه في غير موضع علمه. ثم قال: أفمن كان مؤمنا كمن كان فاسقا لا يستوون (السجدة: 18). ثم بين من المراد بالمؤمن وما له من المآب، والفاسق وما إليه مرجعه مع بيان تكذيبه في ذلك باليوم، وقال فيما قال: كيف يهدي الله قوما كفروا بعد إيمانهم (آل عمران: 86) إلى قوله: والله لا يهدي القوم الظالمين، وقال: قالوا لم نك من المصلين (المدثر: 43) الآية، وقال فيمن لم يؤد الزكاة وهم بالآخرة هم كافرون (السجدة: 41) وفي أمر الربا ما ذكر من
And that no one is more hateful to them than Him, and nothing is heavier on their natures and intellects than what contains His delight in that in two respects: one in His obedience and worship; so God overlooks them and [grants] pleasure, let alone the immediate [reward] by preventing the name of obedience and worship to Him; and the second by giving hope of forgiveness and overlooking, because he preferred enmity of Satan at the time of his disobedience and hope of the mercy of the Lord of the worlds, known by generosity and bounty, who has never ceased to return to them with His beneficence and favor upon them; to Him is praise for that in its fullness.

And then, when the servant believes in obedience to the Lord, and recognizes servitude, and his heart feels the greatness of His blessings upon him and His favors to him, and then He shows him the greatness of His dominion and power by what He mentioned of His wisdom in His creation and the execution of His will in it, he refrains from inclining towards obedience to one whose obedience is not His obedience, and preserves it from imagining worship of other than Him. So it is not permissible to direct his action occurring from him – after his heart has become assured of this and he has become such that he prefers this world over the Hereafter – due to a desire that overcomes him, or a mercy he hopes for, or a matter that pushes him to it, to obedience to other than Him or worship of anyone other than Him; and what I have mentioned is the necessity of his heart at the time of his action. And the like of him from the disbeliever who believed in obedience to other than Him and worship of one who does not deserve it is only that that [action] be directed to the one through whom he came to that, whether Satan or the soul. And there is no power except with God.

Al-Faqih Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: Then the principle regarding everything that is threatened is that its reality occurs from its possessor in aspects of ugliness, by which every difference in that is known in intellects. And similarly every name by which the agent is named. If that requires different meanings that are not intelligible, they are not from all aspects on one level in ugliness, nor is its agent in blame. And that is absent from the listener, so he must, by his intellect which was honored with knowledge of the difference of the positions of that, not combine them unless he examines what the community of understandings is upon and finds that they have arrived at that, or examines all that has come in the revealed text and finds it confirming that, or he [God] makes it capable of encompassing all kinds of wisdom and finds that that is too narrow for specification and necessitates the statement of generality. As for obtaining the way out from generality in judgment – and he has known that if that were true in wisdom or obligatory in governance, the people of atheism would find the clearest criticism in the Quran and the easiest path to saying that it is not sent down from the All-Merciful; since by it He described that 'If it had been from other than God they would have found in it much discrepancy' (4:82), and He said: 'Falsehood cannot come to it from before it or from behind it' (41:42), and He said: 'Indeed We have sent down the Reminder and indeed We will preserve it' (15:9). Then he finds most of the wisdom in it turning away from the way out and being obtained on other than the course of the letter from generality and particularity – and that, according to this statement, is a turning away from the path of wisdom and a removal of the right of governance. Exalted is God above that His proof should be described with this description or His evidence with this contradiction. Then He, exalted be His praise, made clear – when He sent the praised and blamed names – the correlatives by which appears the necessity of the right of restricting the absolute from that, and He said: 'Indeed the righteous are in bliss, and indeed the wicked are in Hellfire' (82:13-14), then He described them and said: 'No! Indeed the record of the wicked is in Sijjin' (83:7) to the end of the surah; so He made clear the absolute wicked intended by the threat and what of denial comes from him, and the righteous He left [detailed] for what He had made clear in other places of His knowledge. Then He said: 'Then is he who was a believer like he who was a disobedient? They are not equal' (32:18). Then He made clear who is intended by the believer and what abode he has, and the disobedient and his final return, along with clarification of his denial of that on the Day [of Judgment]. And He said in what He said: 'How shall God guide a people who disbelieved after their belief' (3:86) to His saying: 'And God does not guide the wrongdoing people.' And He said: 'They will say: We were not among those who prayed' (74:43) and He said about those who did not pay zakah 'and they, of the Hereafter, are disbelievers' (41:7) and regarding the matter of usury, what was mentioned of
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument by Imam al-Maturidi concerning the principle of interpreting divine threats (al-wa'id) in the Quran. It is a methodological and polemical passage defending the Quran's consistency against those who claim its threats are absolute and contradictory.

The argument begins by discussing the spiritual state of the believer: when a servant truly believes in obedience to God and recognizes servitude, and when he contemplates God's blessings and power, his heart naturally turns away from any obedience or worship of other than God. Therefore, even if he commits a sin due to overpowering desire or hope for mercy, that sin cannot be attributed to obedience or worship of other than God; it remains a failing within the context of faith. In contrast, the disbeliever's actions are rightly attributed to the worship of false gods or Satan.

Then al-Maturidi lays down a principle: every action that is threatened (i.e., punished) has its reality in various degrees of ugliness, and these degrees are rationally discernible. Similarly, names given to agents (e.g., 'believer', 'disbeliever', 'sinner') do not all carry the same weight of blame. Since the listener may not know the precise distinctions, he must use his intellect to avoid lumping all threats together as absolute. Instead, he should either check what the Muslim community (al-umma) understands, or examine all revealed texts (al-sam') to see if they consistently affirm distinctions, or recognize that God's wisdom encompasses all arts of wisdom, which precludes narrowing down to specification and necessitates affirming generality (al-'umum).

Al-Maturidi then warns: if one insists on taking every threat as absolute and unconditional (al-'umum fi al-qada'), this would give atheists a clear ground to criticize the Quran, because the Quran itself claims to be consistent (4:82) and preserved from falsehood (41:42, 15:9). Yet such a literalist reading would lead to contradictions within the Quran, turning its wisdom into foolishness and its governance into disorder. God is exalted above such a defect.

To support his position, al-Maturidi cites Quranic verses that show God distinguishes between the righteous (al-abrar) and the wicked (al-fujjar) in ultimate destinies (82:13-14), and that the record of the wicked is in Sijjin (83:7ff). He notes that God does not always detail the righteous in the same passage but leaves it to other places. He then cites 32:18 to show that believers and disobedient are not equal, and 3:86 to show that apostasy is a distinct category. He also references 74:43 about those who neglected prayer, and 41:7 about those who withhold zakah and are disbelievers in the Hereafter. These examples demonstrate that the Quran itself applies threats and descriptions to particular categories of people, not absolutely to all.

Thus, al-Maturidi's argument is a defense of the Quran's coherence by insisting on a contextual and differentiated understanding of divine threats. He refutes both literalist absolutism and rationalist criticism by showing that the Quran's own structure of general and particular, and its pairing of names with correlative descriptions, necessitate a nuanced interpretation.

Key Terms
طاعة
عبادة
إيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what distinguishes a believer's sin from a disbeliever's sin?
A) The believer's sin is always minor.
B) The believer's heart is assured of God's lordship, so sin does not become worship of other than God.
C) The disbeliever repents quickly.
D) The believer's sin is forgiven automatically.

Key Takeaway
A believer's sin, however grave, does not constitute worship of other than God because of the heart's underlying conviction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever feel that your faith is nullified, that you have betrayed God so deeply that you are no longer a believer? How does Maturidi's teaching about the believer's heart being assured even during sin change the way you view your own failures? Will you now turn to God with hope rather than despair?
?
Think of a specific sin you committed recently. In the moment of that action, was your heart truly acknowledging God's lordship, or were you momentarily placing your desire above Him? What does this passage teach you about the state of your heart in that moment? What concrete steps will you take to make your heart's intention more consistent?
?
This passage says that the believer's heart has a 'necessity' (lazim) that prevents his action from being worship of other than God. Do you feel that necessity in your own heart? If not, what is lacking? How can you deepen your recognition of God's blessings and power so that your heart is truly assured?

Interpreting Divine Threats: General and Specific
Maturidi presents the principle of general/specific in threats and warns against absolutism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that divine threats are not absolute but apply to specific categories, and how this maintains the consistency and wisdom of the Quran.

Core Idea
Divine threats are not absolute; they apply to specific categories of people.

Maturidi now lays down a methodological principle: every threatened action has degrees of ugliness that are rationally discernible, and the names applied to agents carry different weights of blame. He warns that taking threats as absolute would open the Quran to criticism from atheists, but the Quran itself provides examples of distinction between the righteous and the wicked, affirming nuanced interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle and its rational basis
Read from 'قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله' to the first Quranic citation. Understand that each threatened act has varying degrees of ugliness, and the listener must use intellect to differentiate.
The principle is that threats are not uniform; they depend on context.
2
Note the warning against absolutism
Maturidi argues that if one insists on absolute generality, atheists would find grounds to criticize the Quran for inconsistency, contradicting its own claims of preservation.
Consider the verses 4:82, 41:42, 15:9 that assert the Quran's perfection.
3
Examine the Quranic examples
Maturidi cites several verses (82:13-14, 83:7, 32:18, 3:86, 74:43, 41:7) that distinguish between righteous and wicked, believer and disbeliever. Identify how each verse restricts the threat to specific categories.
These examples demonstrate that God Himself applies threats to particular groups, not absolutely to all.

وأنه لا أحد أبغض إليهم منه، ولا شيء أثقل على طباعهم وعقولهم مما فيه سروره بذلك على وجهين. أحدهما في طاعته وعبادته؛ فتجاوز الله عنهم ولذته، فضلاً عن العاجل بمنع اسم الطاعة والعبادة له؛ والثاني بإطماع المغفرة والتجاوز، بما آثر عداوة الشيطان في وقت عصيانه ورجاء رحمة رب العالمين المعروف بالكرم والجود الذي لم يزل يعودهم بإحسانه إليهم وإفضاله عليهم له الحمد على ذلك أوفره.

وبعد، فإن العبد إذا اعتقد طاعة الرب، وعرف العبودية، وأشعر قلبه عظيم نعمه عليه وآلائه لديه، ثم أراه عظيم سلطانه وقدرته بما ذكره حكمته في خلقه ونفاذ مشيئته فيه كف أن تميل إلى طاعة من لا يكون طاعته طاعته، وصانها عن توهم عبادة دونه. فلم يجز صرف فعله الواقع منه - بعد أن اطمأن قلبه على هذا وصار ذا آثر عنده الدنيا من والآخرة - لشهوة غلبته أو لرحمة يأملها أو لأمر دفعه إليها، إلى طاعة لغيره أو عبادة منه أحدا دونه، وما ذكرته هو لازم قلبه وقت فعله. وإنما يكون مثله من الكافر الذي اعتقد طاعة من دونه وعبادة من لا يستحقها، أن يُصرف ذلك إلى من به صار إلى ذلك من الشيطان أو النفس. ولا قوة إلا بالله.

قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم الأصل في كل شيء أوعد عليه أن حقيقة ذلك تقع من صاحبه على وجوه من القبح مما يعلم كل تفاوت ذلك في العقول. وكذلك كل اسم جاء به تسمية الفاعل. إن كان ذلك يقتضي مختلفًا من معان لا تعقل تكون هي من كل الوجوه على وزن واحد في القبح ولا فاعله في الذم. وذلك غائب عن السامع، فالزمه بعقله الذي أُكرم معرفة اختلاف مواقع ذلك أن لا يجمع بينها إلا أن يمتحن ما عليه الأمة من الأفهام فوجدها حصلت على ذلك، أو يمتحن جميع ما ورد في السمع فوجده محققا لذلك، أو يجعله يحتمل الإحاطة بكل فنون الحكمة فوجد ذلك يضيق عن التخصيص ويلزمه القول بالعموم. فأما أن يحصل على المخرج من العموم في القضاء وقد علم أن ذلك لو كان حقا في الحكمة أو واجبًا في التدبير ليجد أهل الإلحاد أوضح طعن في القرآن وأيسر سبيل إلى القول بأنه غير منزل من عند الرحمن؛ إذ به وصفه أنه ولو كان من عند غير الله لوجدوا فيه اختلافا كثيرًا (النساء: 82)، وقال: ولا يأتيه الباطل من بين يديه ولا من خلفه (فصلت: 42) الآية، وقال: إنا نحن نزلنا الذكر وإنا له لحافظون (الحجر: 9)، ثم وجد أكثر ما فيه من الحكم منصرِفًا إلى غير المخرج ومحصلاً على غير مجرى اللفظ من العموم والخصوص - وذلك على هذا القول صرف عن طريق الحكمة ومزيل حق التدبير. جل الله عن أن يلحق حجته هذا الوصف أو دليله هذا التناقض. ثم قد بين جل ثناؤه - لما أرسل من الأسماء المحمودة والمذمومة - المقابلات التي لديها يظهر لزوم حق صرف المطلق من ذلك، فقال: إن الأبرار لفي نعيم وإن الفجار لفي جحيم (الانفطار: 13-14)، ثم وصفهم فقال: كلا إن كتاب الفجار لفي سجين (المطففين: 7) إلى آخر السورة؛ فبين الفاجر المطلق المقصود بالوعيد وما منه من التكذيب، والبر تركه لما قد بينه في غير موضع علمه. ثم قال: أفمن كان مؤمنا كمن كان فاسقا لا يستوون (السجدة: 18). ثم بين من المراد بالمؤمن وما له من المآب، والفاسق وما إليه مرجعه مع بيان تكذيبه في ذلك باليوم، وقال فيما قال: كيف يهدي الله قوما كفروا بعد إيمانهم (آل عمران: 86) إلى قوله: والله لا يهدي القوم الظالمين، وقال: قالوا لم نك من المصلين (المدثر: 43) الآية، وقال فيمن لم يؤد الزكاة وهم بالآخرة هم كافرون (السجدة: 41) وفي أمر الربا ما ذكر من
And that no one is more hateful to them than Him, and nothing is heavier on their natures and intellects than what contains His delight in that in two respects: one in His obedience and worship; so God overlooks them and [grants] pleasure, let alone the immediate [reward] by preventing the name of obedience and worship to Him; and the second by giving hope of forgiveness and overlooking, because he preferred enmity of Satan at the time of his disobedience and hope of the mercy of the Lord of the worlds, known by generosity and bounty, who has never ceased to return to them with His beneficence and favor upon them; to Him is praise for that in its fullness.

And then, when the servant believes in obedience to the Lord, and recognizes servitude, and his heart feels the greatness of His blessings upon him and His favors to him, and then He shows him the greatness of His dominion and power by what He mentioned of His wisdom in His creation and the execution of His will in it, he refrains from inclining towards obedience to one whose obedience is not His obedience, and preserves it from imagining worship of other than Him. So it is not permissible to direct his action occurring from him – after his heart has become assured of this and he has become such that he prefers this world over the Hereafter – due to a desire that overcomes him, or a mercy he hopes for, or a matter that pushes him to it, to obedience to other than Him or worship of anyone other than Him; and what I have mentioned is the necessity of his heart at the time of his action. And the like of him from the disbeliever who believed in obedience to other than Him and worship of one who does not deserve it is only that that [action] be directed to the one through whom he came to that, whether Satan or the soul. And there is no power except with God.

Al-Faqih Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: Then the principle regarding everything that is threatened is that its reality occurs from its possessor in aspects of ugliness, by which every difference in that is known in intellects. And similarly every name by which the agent is named. If that requires different meanings that are not intelligible, they are not from all aspects on one level in ugliness, nor is its agent in blame. And that is absent from the listener, so he must, by his intellect which was honored with knowledge of the difference of the positions of that, not combine them unless he examines what the community of understandings is upon and finds that they have arrived at that, or examines all that has come in the revealed text and finds it confirming that, or he [God] makes it capable of encompassing all kinds of wisdom and finds that that is too narrow for specification and necessitates the statement of generality. As for obtaining the way out from generality in judgment – and he has known that if that were true in wisdom or obligatory in governance, the people of atheism would find the clearest criticism in the Quran and the easiest path to saying that it is not sent down from the All-Merciful; since by it He described that 'If it had been from other than God they would have found in it much discrepancy' (4:82), and He said: 'Falsehood cannot come to it from before it or from behind it' (41:42), and He said: 'Indeed We have sent down the Reminder and indeed We will preserve it' (15:9). Then he finds most of the wisdom in it turning away from the way out and being obtained on other than the course of the letter from generality and particularity – and that, according to this statement, is a turning away from the path of wisdom and a removal of the right of governance. Exalted is God above that His proof should be described with this description or His evidence with this contradiction. Then He, exalted be His praise, made clear – when He sent the praised and blamed names – the correlatives by which appears the necessity of the right of restricting the absolute from that, and He said: 'Indeed the righteous are in bliss, and indeed the wicked are in Hellfire' (82:13-14), then He described them and said: 'No! Indeed the record of the wicked is in Sijjin' (83:7) to the end of the surah; so He made clear the absolute wicked intended by the threat and what of denial comes from him, and the righteous He left [detailed] for what He had made clear in other places of His knowledge. Then He said: 'Then is he who was a believer like he who was a disobedient? They are not equal' (32:18). Then He made clear who is intended by the believer and what abode he has, and the disobedient and his final return, along with clarification of his denial of that on the Day [of Judgment]. And He said in what He said: 'How shall God guide a people who disbelieved after their belief' (3:86) to His saying: 'And God does not guide the wrongdoing people.' And He said: 'They will say: We were not among those who prayed' (74:43) and He said about those who did not pay zakah 'and they, of the Hereafter, are disbelievers' (41:7) and regarding the matter of usury, what was mentioned of
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument by Imam al-Maturidi concerning the principle of interpreting divine threats (al-wa'id) in the Quran. It is a methodological and polemical passage defending the Quran's consistency against those who claim its threats are absolute and contradictory.

The argument begins by discussing the spiritual state of the believer: when a servant truly believes in obedience to God and recognizes servitude, and when he contemplates God's blessings and power, his heart naturally turns away from any obedience or worship of other than God. Therefore, even if he commits a sin due to overpowering desire or hope for mercy, that sin cannot be attributed to obedience or worship of other than God; it remains a failing within the context of faith. In contrast, the disbeliever's actions are rightly attributed to the worship of false gods or Satan.

Then al-Maturidi lays down a principle: every action that is threatened (i.e., punished) has its reality in various degrees of ugliness, and these degrees are rationally discernible. Similarly, names given to agents (e.g., 'believer', 'disbeliever', 'sinner') do not all carry the same weight of blame. Since the listener may not know the precise distinctions, he must use his intellect to avoid lumping all threats together as absolute. Instead, he should either check what the Muslim community (al-umma) understands, or examine all revealed texts (al-sam') to see if they consistently affirm distinctions, or recognize that God's wisdom encompasses all arts of wisdom, which precludes narrowing down to specification and necessitates affirming generality (al-'umum).

Al-Maturidi then warns: if one insists on taking every threat as absolute and unconditional (al-'umum fi al-qada'), this would give atheists a clear ground to criticize the Quran, because the Quran itself claims to be consistent (4:82) and preserved from falsehood (41:42, 15:9). Yet such a literalist reading would lead to contradictions within the Quran, turning its wisdom into foolishness and its governance into disorder. God is exalted above such a defect.

To support his position, al-Maturidi cites Quranic verses that show God distinguishes between the righteous (al-abrar) and the wicked (al-fujjar) in ultimate destinies (82:13-14), and that the record of the wicked is in Sijjin (83:7ff). He notes that God does not always detail the righteous in the same passage but leaves it to other places. He then cites 32:18 to show that believers and disobedient are not equal, and 3:86 to show that apostasy is a distinct category. He also references 74:43 about those who neglected prayer, and 41:7 about those who withhold zakah and are disbelievers in the Hereafter. These examples demonstrate that the Quran itself applies threats and descriptions to particular categories of people, not absolutely to all.

Thus, al-Maturidi's argument is a defense of the Quran's coherence by insisting on a contextual and differentiated understanding of divine threats. He refutes both literalist absolutism and rationalist criticism by showing that the Quran's own structure of general and particular, and its pairing of names with correlative descriptions, necessitate a nuanced interpretation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine threats in the Quran are absolute and apply equally to all sinners.
Correction: Maturidi argues that threats have degrees and are context-dependent; the Quran itself distinguishes between categories like righteous and wicked, believer and disbeliever.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what would be the consequence of taking every divine threat as absolute and unconditional?
A) It would strengthen the Quran's claim of consistency.
B) It would provide atheists with a clear criticism against the Quran.
C) It is the position of the righteous ancestors (salaf).
D) It would harmonize with God's wisdom.

Key Takeaway
Divine threats must be interpreted with consideration of general and specific contexts to uphold the Quran's consistency and wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses of threat in the Quran, do you automatically assume they apply to you? How does Maturidi's principle challenge you to re-examine your assumptions about God's mercy and justice? Will you now pause to consider the specific context before fearing or hoping?
?
Think of a sin you struggle with. If you heard a threat like 'those who do X will be punished,' do you immediately despair or dismiss it? How can Maturidi's teaching help you take the threat seriously without losing hope in God's mercy? What specific verse or example from this lesson will you hold onto when fear overwhelms you?
?
The Quran claims to be free of contradiction. Are you sometimes guilty of reading verses in isolation and drawing conclusions that contradict other verses? How can the principle of general and specific protect you from misinterpreting God's word? What practical step will you take to study the Quran more holistically from now on?

Method of Learning

The Middle Path: Maturidi and Ash'ari's Shared Methodology
States the shared middle-path methodology and the link between methodology and doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi and Ash'ari share a middle-path methodology between literalist and rationalist extremes, and that their doctrine is the application of this methodology.

Core Idea
The middle path is their methodology; doctrine is its application.

This lesson introduces the foundational methodology of the two great Sunni theologians, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. Both adopted a balanced approach between those who take scripture literally and those who rely solely on reason. Their agreement on this method is the basis for their shared doctrinal positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the middle path
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the clear statement of their shared approach.
2
Identify the two extremes
The passage contrasts two groups: the literalists (Hashwiyya, anthropomorphists, etc.) and the rationalists (Mu'tazila). Understand what each group represents.
Literalists take scripture at face value, rationalists prioritize reason over revelation.
3
Understand the relationship between methodology and doctrine
The passage explicitly states: 'doctrine is nothing but the application of methodology.' Reflect on how this connects their method to their creed.
4
Reflect on why this balance is important
Consider how this middle path avoids extremes and provides a coherent Sunni theological foundation.

اتفاق الماتريدي والأشعري في المنهج وأصول المذهب يسلك الماتريدي - كما يسلك الأشعري - منهجاً وسطاً بين الحرفيين والعقليين، بين الحرفيين من الحشوية والمشبهة والمكيفة المحددة والمجسمة وبين العقليين من المعتزلة . كما يتخذ الماتريدي والأشعري موقناً متوسطاً معتدلاً و من غلاة الرفض. فيتوسط شيخا السنة بين هذه الفرق ، ويلتقيان في المنهج يلتقيان في المذهب ؛ فليس المذهب إلا تطبيقا للمنهج . يلتقيان في إثبات صفات الله وفي كلامه الأزلي وفي جواز رؤيته وفي بيان عرشه واستوائه ، وفي أفعال عباده ، وفي أمر مرتكب الكبيرة منهم ، وفي شفاعة رسوله . وتلك أهم المسائل التي وقع فيها الخلاف بين فرق المسلمين ، بل إنها أهم موضوعات علم الكلام. يتوسط الأشعري في الصفات فيذكر ابن عساكر أنه نظر في كتب المعتزلة والجهمية والرافضة وأنهم عطلوا وأبطلوا فقالوا : لا علم لله ولا قدرة ولا سمع ولا بضر ولا حياة ولا بقاء ولا إرادة . وقالت الحشوية والمجسمة والمكيفة المحددة : إن الله كالعلوم وقدرة كالقدر وسمعاً كالأسماع وبصراً كالأبصار . فسلك رضي الله عنه طريقة بينهما فقال : إن الله سبحانه وتعالى علماً لا كالعلوم وقدرة لا كالقدر وسمعاً لا كالأسماع وبصرا لا كالأبصار
Agreement of Maturidi and Ash'ari in methodology and principles of doctrine. Maturidi - like Ash'ari - follows a middle path between the literalists and the rationalists, between the literalists from the Hashwiyya, the Anthropomorphists, those who apply modality and limitation, and the Corporealists, and between the rationalists from the Mu'tazila. Likewise, Maturidi and Ash'ari take a moderate, balanced position and from the extremists of rejection. Thus the two masters of the Sunna take a middle way between these sects, and they agree in methodology and agree in doctrine; for doctrine is nothing but the application of methodology. They agree on affirming the attributes of God, His eternal speech, the permissibility of seeing Him, explaining His Throne and His istiwa', the actions of His servants, the matter of the grave sinner among them, and the intercession of His Messenger. These are the most important issues on which differences occurred among the sects of Muslims; indeed, they are the most important topics of kalam. Ash'ari takes a middle position on the attributes, as Ibn 'Asakir mentions that he examined the books of the Mu'tazila, Jahmiyya, and Rawafid, and that they annulled and nullified [the attributes], saying: There is no knowledge for God, no power, no hearing, no sight, no life, no endurance, no will. And the Hashwiyya, Corporealists, and those who apply modality and limitation said: God [has] knowledge like [other] knowledges, power like power, hearing like hearings, sight like sights. So he, may God be pleased with him, followed a way between them, saying: God, exalted is He, [has] knowledge not like knowledges, power not like powers, hearing not like hearings, sight not like sights.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage is a combined classification and narrative of the agreement between Maturidi and Ash'ari. It begins by stating their shared methodology: a middle path between two extremes—the literalists (Hashwiyya, anthropomorphists, etc.) and the rationalists (Mu'tazila). Then it asserts that this methodology is the foundation of their doctrine, which is the application of that method. It lists the key theological issues where they agree: God's attributes, eternal speech, beatific vision, throne and istiwa', human actions, the grave sinner, and intercession—these being the central topics of kalam. Finally, it elaborates on the attributes issue, quoting Ibn 'Asakir's account of Ash'ari's middle position: rejecting both the Mu'tazilite negation and the anthropomorphist comparison, affirming attributes as 'not like' created attributes.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidis and Ash'aris are fundamentally opposed in methodology.
Correction: This passage shows they share a common middle-path methodology and that their doctrines are based on it.

Checkpoint
What is the relationship between methodology and doctrine for Maturidi and Ash'ari according to the passage?
A) They are separate and independent.
B) Doctrine is the application of methodology.
C) Methodology is derived from doctrine.
D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi and Ash'ari share a middle-path methodology that forms the basis of their doctrinal agreement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes, do you find yourself either over-rationalizing them or falling into anthropomorphic images? Where is your heart in this spectrum? Today, commit to seeking the middle path of Ahl al-Sunna by studying a reliable source on Tawhid.
?
This lesson shows that doctrine flows from methodology. What is your method when you encounter a difficult verse about Allah? Do you first consult revelation and the scholars, or do you rely on your own reasoning? Are you willing to adopt the balanced approach of Maturidi and Ash'ari? Take a step: write down a specific attribute of Allah and how you will avoid both extremes in understanding it.
?
The middle path between extremes is not easy—it requires humility and submission. Are you willing to let go of any extreme views you may have held? Can you embrace the moderate position of the Salaf even when it challenges your intellect? Pray for guidance to follow the path of the two masters.

Agreed Doctrines: Key Issues Where Maturidi and Ash'ari Unite
Enumerates seven agreed theological issues and elaborates on the middle position on attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the key theological issues on which Maturidi and Ash'ari agree, and explain their shared middle position on divine attributes.

Core Idea
They unite on attributes, speech, vision, throne, actions, sinners, and intercession.

After establishing their shared methodology, the passage now enumerates the specific doctrinal issues where Maturidi and Ash'ari stand together. These issues were highly contested among early Muslim sects. The lesson then delves into their middle position on divine attributes, illustrating how they avoid both negation and anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of agreed issues
Read the Arabic passage that enumerates the seven issues. Note each one: attributes, eternal speech, beatific vision, throne and istiwa', human actions, grave sinner, intercession.
These are central topics in kalam.
2
Recognize these as central kalam topics
The passage explicitly states these are the most important issues of kalam. Reflect on why they are so significant.
3
Study the example of divine attributes
Read the elaboration on Ash'ari's position on attributes. Contrast the erroneous views of Mu'tazila (negation) and Hashwiyya (comparison) with Ash'ari's middle position.
Ash'ari says 'knowledge not like knowledges', affirming transcendence.
4
Notice the method: affirmation without tashbih
This exemplifies the middle path method: affirming attributes while negating any similarity to creation.

اتفاق الماتريدي والأشعري في المنهج وأصول المذهب يسلك الماتريدي - كما يسلك الأشعري - منهجاً وسطاً بين الحرفيين والعقليين، بين الحرفيين من الحشوية والمشبهة والمكيفة المحددة والمجسمة وبين العقليين من المعتزلة . كما يتخذ الماتريدي والأشعري موقناً متوسطاً معتدلاً و من غلاة الرفض. فيتوسط شيخا السنة بين هذه الفرق ، ويلتقيان في المنهج يلتقيان في المذهب ؛ فليس المذهب إلا تطبيقا للمنهج . يلتقيان في إثبات صفات الله وفي كلامه الأزلي وفي جواز رؤيته وفي بيان عرشه واستوائه ، وفي أفعال عباده ، وفي أمر مرتكب الكبيرة منهم ، وفي شفاعة رسوله . وتلك أهم المسائل التي وقع فيها الخلاف بين فرق المسلمين ، بل إنها أهم موضوعات علم الكلام. يتوسط الأشعري في الصفات فيذكر ابن عساكر أنه نظر في كتب المعتزلة والجهمية والرافضة وأنهم عطلوا وأبطلوا فقالوا : لا علم لله ولا قدرة ولا سمع ولا بضر ولا حياة ولا بقاء ولا إرادة . وقالت الحشوية والمجسمة والمكيفة المحددة : إن الله كالعلوم وقدرة كالقدر وسمعاً كالأسماع وبصراً كالأبصار . فسلك رضي الله عنه طريقة بينهما فقال : إن الله سبحانه وتعالى علماً لا كالعلوم وقدرة لا كالقدر وسمعاً لا كالأسماع وبصرا لا كالأبصار
Agreement of Maturidi and Ash'ari in methodology and principles of doctrine. Maturidi - like Ash'ari - follows a middle path between the literalists and the rationalists, between the literalists from the Hashwiyya, the Anthropomorphists, those who apply modality and limitation, and the Corporealists, and between the rationalists from the Mu'tazila. Likewise, Maturidi and Ash'ari take a moderate, balanced position and from the extremists of rejection. Thus the two masters of the Sunna take a middle way between these sects, and they agree in methodology and agree in doctrine; for doctrine is nothing but the application of methodology. They agree on affirming the attributes of God, His eternal speech, the permissibility of seeing Him, explaining His Throne and His istiwa', the actions of His servants, the matter of the grave sinner among them, and the intercession of His Messenger. These are the most important issues on which differences occurred among the sects of Muslims; indeed, they are the most important topics of kalam. Ash'ari takes a middle position on the attributes, as Ibn 'Asakir mentions that he examined the books of the Mu'tazila, Jahmiyya, and Rawafid, and that they annulled and nullified [the attributes], saying: There is no knowledge for God, no power, no hearing, no sight, no life, no endurance, no will. And the Hashwiyya, Corporealists, and those who apply modality and limitation said: God [has] knowledge like [other] knowledges, power like power, hearing like hearings, sight like sights. So he, may God be pleased with him, followed a way between them, saying: God, exalted is He, [has] knowledge not like knowledges, power not like powers, hearing not like hearings, sight not like sights.
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage is a combined classification and narrative of the agreement between Maturidi and Ash'ari. It begins by stating their shared methodology: a middle path between two extremes—the literalists (Hashwiyya, anthropomorphists, etc.) and the rationalists (Mu'tazila). Then it asserts that this methodology is the foundation of their doctrine, which is the application of that method. It lists the key theological issues where they agree: God's attributes, eternal speech, beatific vision, throne and istiwa', human actions, the grave sinner, and intercession—these being the central topics of kalam. Finally, it elaborates on the attributes issue, quoting Ibn 'Asakir's account of Ash'ari's middle position: rejecting both the Mu'tazilite negation and the anthropomorphist comparison, affirming attributes as 'not like' created attributes.

The Middle Path: Maturidi and Ash'ari's Shared Methodology and Doctrines
Describes the middle path and lists agreed doctrines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi and Ash'ari share a balanced approach between extremes and agree on key kalam doctrines.

Core Idea
Maturidi and Ash'ari are united in methodology and core beliefs.

This passage from a classical source highlights the remarkable agreement between the two great Sunni theologians, al-Maturidi and al-Ash'ari. It shows that they both follow a middle path, avoiding the extremes of anthropomorphism and excessive rationalism. The passage then lists the main theological issues where they concur, providing a foundation for understanding Sunni orthodoxy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two extremes
Read the passage and note which groups are considered literalist (Hashwiyya, Mushabbiha, etc.) and which are rationalist (Mu'tazila). Understand that Maturidi and Ash'ari take a middle path between them.
Also note their moderate stance against extremists among the Rafida.
2
List the agreed doctrines
The passage enumerates seven specific doctrines on which Maturidi and Ash'ari agree. Write them down in your own words.
These are considered the most important topics of kalam.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this agreement matters for Sunni identity. The author states that the school (madhhab) is an application of the methodology.
This passage counters the misconception that Maturidis and Ash'aris are fundamentally different.

This passage classifies the methodological and doctrinal agreement between Maturidi and Ash'ari. Both scholars also take a moderate position against the extremists of the rejecters (probably the Rafida). Two Approaches to Phrasing Divine Attributes
Al-Nasafi contrasts two formulations for stating God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the Ahl al-Hadith formulation 'alim bi-'ilm' and the majority Maturidi formulation 'alim wa lahu 'ilm', and why the latter avoids implying that knowledge is an instrument.

Core Idea
Phrasing matters: a small change can prevent misunderstanding God's attributes.

Al-Nasafi presents two ways theologians have phrased the relationship between God and His attributes. The first, used by Ahl al-Hadith, directly says 'knowing by knowledge.' The second, favored by most Maturidi scholars, cautiously says 'knowing and He has knowledge' to avoid implying that knowledge is a tool. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on the two formulations
Read the first part of the passage from al-Nasafi, which presents two approaches. Note the exact phrases.
2
Identify the precautionary reason
The majority refrained from 'alim bi-'ilm' because it might suggest knowledge is an instrument. Find the Arabic phrase that explains this.
Key phrase: 'احترازا عما توهم أن العلم آلة وأداة'.
3
Compare the two formulations
Notice the difference: 'alim bi-'ilm' vs. 'alim wa lahu 'ilm'. The first uses 'bi-' (by) which could imply instrumentality; the second uses 'wa lahu' (and He has) which avoids that.
Consider why 'having' knowledge is safer than 'by' knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول النسفي : ثم اعلم أن عبارة متكلمى أهل الحديث في هذه المسألة أن يقال : إن الله تعالى عالم بعلم ، وكذا فيما وراء ذلك من الصفات ، وأكثر مشايخنا امتنعوا عن هذه العبارة احترازا عما توهم أن العلم آلة وأداة ، فيقولون : الله تعالى عالم وله علم ، وكذا فيما وراء ذلك الصفات ، والشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي رحمه الله يقول : إن الله تعالى عالم بذاته حي بذاته قادر بذاته ولا يريد به نفى الصفات ؛ لأنه أثبت الصفات في مصنفاته وآتى بالدلائل لإثباتها ودفع شبهاتهم على وجه لا محيص للخصوم عن ذلك ، غير أنه أراد بذلك دفع وهم المغايرة، وأن ذاته يستحيل أن لا يكون عالماً
Al-Nasafi says: Then know that the expression of the theologians of Ahl al-Hadith in this matter is that it is said: God Almighty is knowing by knowledge, and likewise for the rest of the attributes. And most of our teachers refrained from this expression as a precaution against the misunderstanding that knowledge is an instrument and a tool, so they say: God Almighty is knowing and He has knowledge, and likewise for the rest of the attributes. And Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, may God have mercy on him, says: God Almighty is knowing by His essence, living by His essence, powerful by His essence, and He does not intend thereby the negation of the attributes; because he affirmed the attributes in his writings and brought proofs to establish them and refute their doubts in a manner from which opponents cannot escape. However, he intended by that to remove the misconception of otherness, and that His essence is impossible to not be knowing.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage is a methodological discussion from al-Nasafi about the proper way to phrase statements regarding divine attributes. It contrasts three approaches. The first is the expression of the mutakallimun of Ahl al-Hadith: 'God is knowing by knowledge' ('alim bi-'ilm), and similarly for other attributes. The second is the approach of most Maturidi teachers: they avoid saying 'by knowledge' because it might imply that knowledge is an instrument (ala wa ada), and instead say 'God is knowing and He has knowledge' ('alim wa lahu 'ilm). The third is the approach of Imam al-Maturidi himself: he says 'God is knowing by His essence' (alim bi-dhatihi), which he does not mean to deny the attributes, as he affirmed them in his works. Rather, he intended to remove the misconception of otherness (taghayyur) and to emphasize that it is impossible for God's essence to not be knowing.

Key Terms
عالم بعلم
عالم وله علم
آلة وأداة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That saying 'God is knowing by knowledge' is always safe and carries no problematic implications.
Correction: The Maturidi teachers caution that it may imply knowledge is an instrument, so they prefer 'knowing and He has knowledge'.

Checkpoint
Why did most Maturidi teachers avoid saying 'God is knowing by knowledge' ('alim bi-'ilm)?
A) It implies knowledge is created.
B) It might suggest knowledge is an instrument.
C) They deny that God has knowledge.
D) They only accept the phrase 'God is knowing by His essence'.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi scholars prefer 'God is knowing and He has knowledge' over 'God is knowing by knowledge' to avoid implying that knowledge is an instrument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God's attributes, do you ever use phrases that might subtly resemble how we describe human tools? How can you guard your speech to preserve the transcendence of Allah?
?
Are you willing to change a habitual phrase like 'Allah knows by His knowledge' to 'Allah knows and He has knowledge' if it protects you from even a slight misbelief? What holds you back from adopting more precise wording?
?
This lesson shows that a single preposition ('bi-') can carry a dangerous implication. What other common expressions about God might you need to rethink after realizing how easily language can mislead?

Al-Maturidi's Essence-Based Attribution
Al-Nasafi explains al-Maturidi's essence-based attribution and its purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Maturidi said 'God is knowing by His essence' and how this phrase does not deny the attributes but removes the notion of otherness.

Core Idea
Al-Maturidi's 'by His essence' affirms attributes by rejecting any hint of separation.

Imam al-Maturidi takes a distinct approach: he says 'God is knowing by His essence' (alim bi-dhatihi). At first glance, this might seem to deny the attributes. But al-Nasafi clarifies that al-Maturidi did not intend negation; rather, he affirmed the attributes in his writings. His goal was to remove the misconception of otherness (taghayyur) and to emphasize that God's essence is inherently knowing, without needing a separate attribute that could be mistaken as something other than His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Maturidi's statement
Read the part of the passage where al-Nasafi quotes al-Maturidi: 'God is knowing by His essence, living by His essence, powerful by His essence.'
2
Understand al-Maturidi's intention
Al-Nasafi says al-Maturidi does not intend to negate the attributes. Find why: because he affirmed them in his writings and provided proofs.
Key phrase: 'ولا يريد به نفى الصفات' (he does not intend by it the negation of the attributes).
3
Identify the misconception he refutes
Al-Maturidi wanted to remove the 'wahm al-mughayara' — the illusion of otherness. What does that mean? It means the idea that the attribute is something other than the essence.
Think: If we say 'God is knowing by knowledge,' could that imply knowledge is a separate entity? Al-Maturidi's phrase avoids that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول النسفي : ثم اعلم أن عبارة متكلمى أهل الحديث في هذه المسألة أن يقال : إن الله تعالى عالم بعلم ، وكذا فيما وراء ذلك من الصفات ، وأكثر مشايخنا امتنعوا عن هذه العبارة احترازا عما توهم أن العلم آلة وأداة ، فيقولون : الله تعالى عالم وله علم ، وكذا فيما وراء ذلك الصفات ، والشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي رحمه الله يقول : إن الله تعالى عالم بذاته حي بذاته قادر بذاته ولا يريد به نفى الصفات ؛ لأنه أثبت الصفات في مصنفاته وآتى بالدلائل لإثباتها ودفع شبهاتهم على وجه لا محيص للخصوم عن ذلك ، غير أنه أراد بذلك دفع وهم المغايرة، وأن ذاته يستحيل أن لا يكون عالماً

?

Refutation of Taqlid and the Ash'ari Position
The author describes Maturidi's opening chapter against taqlid and Ash'ari position on takfir of the muqallid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus against taqlid and the Ash'ari view that faith must be based on reasoning, including the role of doubt as a purifying step.

Core Idea
Doubt in Ash'ari thought is a tool to clear the mind, not a goal.

In this passage, we see Maturidi's alignment with Sunni and Mu'tazilite consensus in rejecting blind following. However, al-Ash'ari and his school go further, declaring mere taqlid as disbelief. They also introduce a striking requirement: a person must pass through a stage of doubt before attaining true faith. This lesson unpacks that view and its purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice that Maturidi starts with a chapter invalidating taqlid, and then the text moves to the Ash'ari view.
The passage is from an analysis of Maturidi's work, so it comments on his opening chapter.
2
Identify the consensus on taqlid
The passage states that Maturidi's position is in line with the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah and the Mu'tazila that taqlid is corrupt and invalid. This sets a common ground among major theological schools.
3
Understand the Ash'ari position on takfir and the doubt requirement
Al-Ash'ari and the Ash'aris declared the muqallid a disbeliever. They stipulated that true faith requires reasoning (istidlal), and a person's Islam only becomes valid after puberty when they become a doubter (shak) rather than a mere believer (muṣaddiq).
This is a stricter position than the general consensus.
4
Reflect on the purpose of doubt as purification
The passage clarifies that this doubt is not an end in itself. Its purpose is to purify the soul from inherited ideas, preparing one to accept truth through reflection (nazar) and reasoning. The Muslim should be trained in this method at puberty.
This addresses the common misunderstanding that doubt is praised for its own sake.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في 1 - تحليل محتوى كتاب التوحيد يستهل الماتريدي كتاب التوحيد بفصل في إبطال التقليد . وهو في ذلك لا يخرج على الإجماع المنعقد بين أهل السنة والمعتزلة على فساد التقليد وبطلانه لقد ذهب الأشعري والأشعرية إلى حد تكفير المقلد ؛ إذ اشترطوا في صحة الإيمان أنه لا بد أن يكون قائماً على الاستدلال ، بل إنهم يرون لا يصبح إسلام أحد حتى يكون بعد بلوغه شاكاً غير مصدق، على أن الشك هنا ليس مقصوداً لذاته ، وإنما هو شك الغرض منه تطهير النفس من الأفكار الموروثة حتى تستعد لقبول الحق بالنظر والاستدلال الذي ينبغي تدريب المسلم عليها فور أن يراهق
In 1 - Analysis of the content of the Book of Tawhid: Al-Maturidi begins the Book of Tawhid with a chapter on invalidating taqlid (blind following). In that, he does not depart from the consensus held between Ahl al-Sunnah and the Mu'tazila on the corruption and invalidity of taqlid. Al-Ash'ari and the Ash'aris went to the extent of declaring the muqallid (blind follower) a disbeliever; for they stipulated in the validity of faith that it must be based on reasoning (istidlal), rather they hold that the Islam of a person does not become valid until after he reaches puberty as a doubter (shak) not a believer, although doubt here is not intended for its own sake, but it is a doubt whose purpose is to purify the soul from inherited ideas so that it becomes ready to accept the truth through reflection (nazar) and reasoning (istidlal) which the Muslim should be trained in as soon as he approaches puberty.
Source pages p. 567

Commentary
This passage begins the analysis of Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid. It states that Maturidi starts his book with a chapter refuting taqlid (blind adherence), which aligns with the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah and Mu'tazila on the invalidity of taqlid. It then explains that al-Ash'ari and the Ash'aris went further, declaring the muqallid a disbeliever (takfir al-muqallid). They stipulate that true faith must be based on reasoning (istidlal). In fact, they hold that a person's Islam is not valid until, after reaching puberty, he becomes a doubter (shak) rather than a believer (muṣaddiq). However, the author clarifies that this doubt is not an end in itself; it is a purposeful doubt aimed at purifying the soul from inherited beliefs, preparing it to accept truth through reflection (nazar) and reasoning (istidlal). The Muslim should be trained in this method as soon as he approaches adolescence. This passage highlights the strict Ash'ari position that faith must be rationally grounded, contrasting with the more general consensus on the invalidity of taqlid.

Key Terms
التقليد
الاستدلال
الشك
تطهير النفس
المقلد
تكفير المقلد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the Ash'ari requirement of doubt means they praise doubt and uncertainty as a permanent state.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that doubt is not intended for its own sake. It is a temporary means to purge inherited beliefs and prepare the soul for rational inquiry, leading to conviction through reasoning.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the doubt (shak) required by Ash'aris before faith according to this passage?
A) To make faith uncertain.
B) To reject all knowledge.
C) To purify the soul from inherited ideas and prepare for rational inquiry.
D) To align with Mu'tazila doctrine.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari requirement of doubt before faith is not for skepticism but to purge inherited beliefs and enable reasoned conviction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever examined the reasons for your own beliefs about Allah, or have you simply accepted what you were taught? What would it mean for you to engage in the kind of doubt Maturidi and the Ash'aris prescribe—not to lose faith, but to purify your heart? Could your faith survive that kind of scrutiny? What are you afraid of finding?
?
The Ash'aris say a person's Islam is not valid until after puberty as a doubter. Does your faith rest on inherited certainty or on personal conviction? What would it take for you to move from taqlid to tahqiq (verified faith)?
?
Are you training yourself in reflection and reasoning (nazar and istidlal) as the passage says Muslims should be trained as soon as they approach puberty? If not, why not? What is holding you back from seeking solid intellectual foundations for your faith?

The Unity of Ahl al-Sunnah on Essential Creed
Ibn al-Subki affirms unity of creed and lists three groups of Ahl al-Sunnah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all three groups of Ahl al-Sunnah share a single creed regarding what is necessary, possible, and impossible for God, despite differing in their methodological approaches.

Core Idea
One creed, three paths: Ahl al-Sunnah agree on essentials.

Ibn al-Subki begins his classification by affirming a profound unity among Ahl al-Sunnah. Before listing the three groups, he emphasizes that they all concur on the fundamental creed concerning God's attributes and actions. This lesson will introduce you to that shared foundation and the three schools that uphold it.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read Ibn al-Subki's affirmation that all Ahl al-Sunnah agree on one creed regarding wajib, jaiz, mustahil.
Notice the terms 'yajib', 'yajuz', 'yastahil' — the three rational judgments.
2
Identify the three groups
The passage then classifies Ahl al-Sunnah into three groups by induction: Ahl al-Hadith, Ash'ariyya, and Hanafiyya (Maturidis). Note the founders named.
The Ash'ariyya and Hanafiyya are grouped under 'Ahl al-Nazar'.
3
Reflect on unity vs. diversity
Appreciate that despite different methods (scriptural vs. rational), the creed itself is one. This is a key point in Sunni theology.
This refutes claims that these groups are separate sects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول ابن السبكي في شرح عقيدة ابن الحاجب : اعلم أن أهل السنة والجماعة كلهم قد اتفقوا على معتقد واحد فيما يجب ويجوز ويستحيل وإن اختلفوا في الطرق والمبادئ الموصلة لذلك . وبالجملة فهم بالاستقراء ثلاث طوائف : الأولى أهل الحديث ، ومعتمد مباديهم الأدلة السمعية – الكتاب ، والسنة ، والاجماع . الثانية أهل النظر الأشعرية والحنفية ، وشيخ الأشعرية أبو الحسن الأشعري ، وشيخ العقلي وهم الحنفية أبو منصور الماتريدي. وهم متفقون في المبادئ العقلية في كل مطلب يتوقف السمع عليه ، وفي المبادئ السمعية فيما يدرك العقل جوازه فقط ، والعقلية والسمعية غيرها ، واتفقوا في جميع المطالب الاعتقادية إلا في مسائل
Ibn al-Subki says in his commentary on the Creed of Ibn al-Hajib: 'Know that Ahl al-Sunnah wal-Jama'ah all have agreed upon one creed (mu'taqad) regarding what is necessary (yajib), possible (yajuz), and impossible (yastahil), even if they differ in the paths and principles (turuq wa mabadi) leading to that. In sum, they are, by induction, three groups: the first, Ahl al-Hadith, and the basis of their principles is the scriptural evidences (al-adilla al-sam'iyya) — the Book, the Sunnah, and Consensus (ijma'). The second, the people of rational consideration (ahl al-nazar), the Ash'ariyya and the Hanafiyya; the shaykh of the Ash'ariyya is Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, and the shaykh of the rationalists (al-'uqala) who are the Hanafiyya is Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. They agree upon the rational principles (al-mabadi' al-'aqliyya) in every matter that depends on revelation (sam'), and upon the scriptural principles in what the intellect only deems permissible, and the rational and scriptural other than that. And they agreed in all matters of belief except in some issues.'
Source pages p. 567

Commentary
This passage is a classification of Ahl al-Sunnah by Ibn al-Subki, quoting from his commentary on Ibn al-Hajib's creed. He begins by affirming that Ahl al-Sunnah wal-Jama'ah share a single creed concerning the three rational judgments: what is necessary (wajib), possible (ja'iz), and impossible (mustahil) for God. However, they differ in the paths and principles leading to that knowledge. He then identifies three schools: (1) Ahl al-Hadith, who rely solely on scriptural evidences (sam'iyyat) — the Qur'an, Sunnah, and Ijma'. (2) The Ash'ariyya and Hanafiyya, who use rational consideration (nazar). He names Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari as the founder of the Ash'aris and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi as the founder of the Hanafiyya rationalists. Ibn al-Subki explains that these two rationalist schools agree on rational principles in matters that depend on revelation, and on scriptural principles in matters where reason only deems possibility. They agreed on all doctrinal issues except a few minor ones.

Key Terms
أهل السنة والجماعة
معتقد
أدلة سمعية
أهل النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think Ash'aris and Maturidis disagree on fundamental creed.
Correction: Ibn al-Subki states they agree on the creed itself, differing only in method and minor issues.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Subki, what do the three groups of Ahl al-Sunnah agree upon?
A) The methods of reasoning (turuq)
B) The scriptural evidences (adilla sam'iyya)
C) The creed regarding what is necessary, possible, and impossible
D) The leadership of Abu Hanifa

Key Takeaway
All Ahl al-Sunnah share one creed, though they follow different paths to it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a fellow Muslim from the Ash'ari or Maturidi tradition, do you view them as having the same essential faith as you, or do you let methodological differences create distance in your heart? Will you actively remind yourself of their shared creed from this lesson?
?
Have you ever favored one method (e.g., pure scripturalism or pure rationalism) to the point of dismissing the other? What does Ibn al-Subki's inclusion of all three groups compel you to change in your attitude toward those who approach faith differently?
?
If the unity of Ahl al-Sunnah on essentials is real, why do you allow secondary disagreements to fester into division in your community? What can you do today to express that underlying creedal unity in how you treat others?

Agreement and Minor Differences Among Ahl al-Sunnah
Ibn al-Subki describes agreement on principles and near-unanimity on belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'aris and Maturidis agree on rational and scriptural principles, and that their differences are limited to a few issues.

Core Idea
Ash'aris and Maturidis share methodology and agree on all major beliefs.

Ibn al-Subki continues by explaining the relationship between the two rationalist schools. He shows that despite being distinct groups, they agree on fundamental methodological principles and on all doctrinal matters except a handful of issues. This lesson will clarify that unity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreement on rational principles
The passage states that Ash'aris and Maturidis agree on rational principles for matters dependent on revelation. Reflect on the role of reason in Islamic theology.
This shows reason is not opposed to revelation.
2
Identify the agreement on scriptural principles
They also agree on scriptural principles where reason only deems possibility. Note the boundary between reason and revelation.
This is a nuanced position.
3
Note the concluding affirmation
They agreed on all matters of belief except for a few issues. This underscores essential unity.
The 'few issues' are often discussed but do not affect core creed.
