Maturidi's Opening Argument Against Taqlid
Maturidi opens Kitab al-Tawhid by arguing taqlid is inexcusable without proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's foundational argument that blind following (taqlid) cannot justify religious convictions unless anchored by rational proof and demonstration.

Core Idea
Blind following is no excuse—only rational proof and demonstration establish truth.

In the opening of Kitab al-Tawhid, Imam al-Maturidi confronts a universal human predicament: people of different religions each claim to be right, yet all rely on imitation of predecessors. He argues that this shared reliance on taqlid undermines its validity, because the same excuse could be used by anyone. However, he leaves open the possibility that true guidance comes through those who possess rational proof and demonstration. This passage sets the stage for his entire theological method, establishing that faith must be grounded in evidence, not mere inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's own logical flow come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi starts with an observation about human disagreement.
2
Identify the universal observation
Maturidi notes that people of different sects all agree that their own view is true and others false, yet each follows predecessors. Identify this observation and why it is important.
This observation is the premise for his conclusion about taqlid.
3
Understand the conclusion about taqlid
Maturidi concludes that taqlid cannot be an excuse because the opposite opinion can claim the same justification, and taqlid only offers numbers, not truth. Reflect on the logic.
4
Note the exception
Maturidi states that if the one being followed has a rational proof (hujjat 'aql) and demonstration (burhan), then following him is valid. Identify this exception.
This exception is key to Maturidi's method.
5
Reflect on the obligation
Maturidi concludes that the one with proof is the true one (muhaqq), and every individual is obligated to know the truth in their religion. Consider the implications.
This sets a high standard for religious knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول الماتريدي مفتتحاً كتاب التوحيد : أما بعد ، فإنا وجدنا الناس مختلفي المذاهب في النحل في الدين ، متفقين على اختلافهم في الدين على كلمة واحدة : أن الذي هو عليه حق ، والذي عليه غيره باطل ، على اتفاق جملتهم في أن كلاً له سلف يقلد ، فثبت أن التقليد ليس مما يعذر صاحبه لإصابة مثله ضده على أنه ليس فيه سوى كثرة العدد ، اللهم إلا أن يكون لأحد ممن ينتهي القول إليه حجة عقل يعلم بها صدقه فيما يدعي ، وبرهان يقهر المنصفين على إصابته الحق ، فمن إليه مرجعه في الدين بما يوجب تحقيقة عنه فهو المحق، وعلى كل واحد معرفة الحق فيما يدين هو به
Maturidi says, opening the Book of Tawhid: 'To proceed, we have found people differing in sects (al-madhaahib) in religion, agreeing upon their disagreement in religion on one word: that what they themselves are upon is truth (haqq), and what others are upon is falsehood (baatil), by the agreement of all of them that everyone has predecessors whom they blindly follow (yuqallid). So it is established that blind following (taqlid) is not something that excuses its practitioner, because the like of it is opposite to it, and there is nothing in it save multiplicity of number. Unless there be for someone to whom the statement is ultimately referred a rational proof (hujjat 'aql) by which he knows his truthfulness in what he claims, and a demonstration (burhaan) that overwhelms the fair-minded that he has hit the truth. Then whoever is the one to whom reference is made in religion by that which necessitates verification concerning it, he is the one who is correct (muhaqq), and upon every individual is the knowledge of the truth in what he practices as religion.'
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage is a definitional argument against taqlid (blind following) from Maturidi's opening of Kitab al-Tawhid. Maturidi begins by observing a universal phenomenon: people belong to different religious sects, yet each is certain that their own position is true and others' false. All agree that everyone has predecessors they imitate. From this, Maturidi concludes that taqlid is not a valid excuse—because the opposite opinion can claim the same justification, and taqlid offers only numerical strength, not truth. He then states the sole exception: if the person being followed has a rational proof (hujjat 'aql) and a demonstration (burhaan) that establish his truth, then following him is justified. The one who has such proof is the one truly correct (muhaqq), and every individual is obligated to know the truth in their own religion.

Key Terms
تقليد
حجة عقل
برهان
محق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Maturidi rejects all forms of following others in religious matters.
Correction: Maturidi allows following those who possess rational proof and demonstration, but rejects blind imitation based solely on tradition or numbers.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi identify as the only thing that can excuse someone from taqlid?
A) Agreement with the majority
B) Following the predecessors
C) Having a rational proof and demonstration
D) Being a scholar

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that taqlid is not a valid basis for religious truth; only rational proof and demonstration can justify one's convictions, and every person is obligated to seek such knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You follow a particular madhab or scholar. Have you ever verified that they possess a rational proof for their positions? If not, are you guilty of the taqlid that Maturidi says is inexcusable? What step will you take today to seek that proof?
?
When you say your beliefs are true and others are false, do you have a demonstration that would overwhelm a fair-minded person? If you cannot provide one, how can you be sure you are not just repeating what your predecessors said? Will you commit to learning the evidence for at least one core belief this week?
?
Maturidi says upon every individual is the knowledge of the truth in what they practice as religion. Is there a belief you hold that you cannot defend with rational proof? If so, how long will you continue to practice it without understanding? What will you do to change that starting today?

When Must One Use Inference? Comparing Scholarly Views
Classifies positions on when istidlal becomes obligatory and compares Maturidi with Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different positions on when the obligation of istidlal (rational inference) begins, and the notable agreement between Imam Maturidi and the Mu'tazila on the rejection of taqlid.

Core Idea
Maturidi and Mu'tazila agree: blind following is invalid, only expression differs.

This passage from Ibn Hazm's classification surveys a spectrum of views on when a person becomes obligated to use rational inference to know God. It reports al-Tabari's strict view that even children must be trained, the Ash'ari view that it begins at puberty, and then makes a striking observation: despite sectarian differences, Imam Maturidi's position on taqlid is identical to that of the Mu'tazila. Let's explore the details and significance of this classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the flow from Ibn Hazm's report to the comparative conclusion.
Pay attention to the names and the terms 'istidlal' and 'taqlid'.
2
Identify al-Tabari's position
Al-Tabari holds that upon reaching puberty or even earlier, a person must use inference to know God with all His attributes; otherwise they are considered disbelievers. He also requires training children from age seven.
This is the strictest view.
3
Contrast with the Ash'ari position
The Ash'aris (except al-Simnani) say inference is not obligatory until after puberty. This is a more lenient stance.
Note the exception of al-Simnani.
4
Notice the Maturidi-Mu'tazila convergence
The passage states that the Mu'tazila's statements on invalidating taqlid are no different from what Maturidi wrote in Kitab al-Tawhid. Qadi Abd al-Jabbar, a leading Mu'tazili, said nothing different; only the wording differs.
This shows a shared rationalist methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البلوغ أو قبل ذلك إذا استطاع إليها سبيلاً . يقول ابن حزم : ذهب محمد بن جرير الطبري والأشعرية كلها حاشا السمناني إلى أنه لا يكون مسلماً إلا وإلا فليس مسلماً . وقال الطبري : من بلغ الاحتلام أو الإشعار استدل من أسمائه من الرجال والنساء أو بلغ المحيض من النساء ولم يعرف الله عز وجل بجميع وصفاته من طريق الاستدلال فهو كافر حلال الدم والمال ، وقال : إنه إذا بلغ الغلام أو الجارية سبع سنين وجب تعليمها وتدريبها على الاستدلال على ذلك ، وقالت الأشعرية : لا يلزمها الاستدلال على ذلك إلا بعد البلوغ . أما المعتزلة فلا تخرج أقوالهم في إبطال التقليد عما يقوله الماتريدي في كتاب التوحيد . فلم يقل القاضي عبد الجبار المعتزلي المتوفى عام ٤١٥ هـ / ١٠٢٤ م أي بعد وفاة الماتريدي باثنين وثمانين عاماً شيئاً سوى ما قاله الماتريدي. وليس بينهما اختلاف إلا في العبارة فقط ، أما سياق الأدلة فواحد عند الرجلين
Or before that if he is able. Ibn Hazm says: Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari and all the Ash'aris except al-Simnani held that one is not a Muslim unless ... or else he is not a Muslim. And al-Tabari said: Whoever reaches puberty or discretion and [has the ability] to infer from His names, whether male or female, or a woman reaches menstruation and does not know God Almighty with all His attributes by way of inference, then he is a disbeliever, his blood and wealth are permissible. And he said: When a boy or girl reaches seven years, it is obligatory to teach them and train them in inference on that. And the Ash'aris said: Inference is not obligatory for them except after puberty. As for the Mu'tazila, their statements on invalidating taqlid do not go beyond what Maturidi says in Kitab al-Tawhid. So Qadi Abd al-Jabbar the Mu'tazili, died 415 AH / 1024 CE, i.e., eighty-two years after Maturidi's death, said nothing except what Maturidi said. And there is no difference between them except in expression; the context of the evidence is one for the two men.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage classifies the positions of early scholars on when the obligation of istidlal (inference) for knowing God begins. It begins by quoting Ibn Hazm, who reports that al-Tabari and all the Ash'aris except al-Simnani held that a person is not a Muslim unless ... etc. Then it details al-Tabari's view that upon reaching puberty or the age of discretion, one must infer God from His names, otherwise they are a disbeliever. It also reports that al-Tabari said that at age seven, children must be taught and trained in inference. The Ash'aris, by contrast, said inference is not obligatory until after puberty. Then it turns to the Mu'tazila, stating that their statements on invalidating taqlid do not differ from what Maturidi says in Kitab al-Tawhid. It notes that Qadi Abd al-Jabbar, despite being Mu'tazili, said nothing different from Maturidi, and there is no difference between them except in phrasing; the context of the evidence is the same for both.

Key Terms
استدلال
تقليد
بلوغ
ماتريدي
قاضي عبد الجبار
طبري
أشعرية
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think all scholars agree that the obligation of istidlal begins at puberty.
Correction: This passage shows a spectrum: al-Tabari requires it even earlier (training from age seven, obligation at puberty), the Ash'aris delay it to puberty, and Maturidi/Mu'tazila reject taqlid without specifying a fixed age, emphasizing rational inference.

Checkpoint
According to al-Tabari, at what age should children be taught and trained in istidlal?
A) At puberty
B) At seven years
C) At ten years
D) At the age of discretion

Key Takeaway
Early scholars differed on the timing of the obligation of istidlal, but Maturidi and the Mu'tazila share a common rejection of taqlid and emphasize rational inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that God exists, is your conviction built on a chain of evidence you can articulate, or on the authority of those who taught you? If you cannot defend your faith rationally, does that concern you in light of this lesson?
?
Al-Tabari considered it so essential that he taught children from age seven to infer God's existence. Are you preparing the next generation to own their faith through reasoning, or merely to inherit it? What would you change starting today?
?
The Maturidis and Mu'tazila agreed on the substance of inference yet differed in expression. How often do you mistake differences in wording for differences in belief, and how might that affect your unity with other Muslims?

Maturidi's Opening: The Dilemma of Taqlid
Maturidi presents his initial observation and argument against taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's observation that all sects claim truth while relying on blind following, and his conclusion that taqlid is not a valid excuse.

Core Idea
Taqlid is self-defeating because it can be used by all sides.

Maturidi begins his Kitab al-Tawhid by pointing out a striking fact about religious disagreement: everyone claims to be right, yet everyone relies on blindly following their predecessors. He uses this to show that taqlid cannot be a valid way to truth, because it offers no criterion to distinguish right from wrong. In this lesson, we examine his opening argument and its implication that only rational proof can establish truth.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's opening observation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi sets up the problem: people differ, each claims truth, but all rely on past authority.
2
Identify the paradox
All sects agree that only their own view is true, yet they all use taqlid. This shows taqlid provides no way to select the correct view.
3
Note Maturidi's conclusion
Taqlid is not a valid excuse because the opponent can say the same. Only 'multitude of number' is not proof.
4
Recognize the exception
Maturidi says that only a rational proof (hujja 'aql) and demonstration (burhan) can establish truth. This is the only way to break the cycle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول الماتريدي مفتتحاً كتاب التوحيد : أما بعد ، فإنا وجدنا الناس مختلفي المذاهب في النحل في الدين ، متفقين على اختلافهم في الدين على كلمة واحدة : أن الذي هو عليه حق ، والذي عليه غيره باطل ، على اتفاق جملتهم في أن كلاً له سلف يقلد ، فثبت أن التقليد ليس مما يعذر صاحبه لإصابة مثله ضده على أنه ليس فيه سوى كثرة العدد ، اللهم إلا أن يكون لأحد ممن ينتهي القول إليه حجة عقل يعلم بها صدقه فيما يدعي ، وبرهان يقهر المنصفين على إصابته الحق ، فمن إليه مرجعه في الدين بما يوجب تحقيقة عنه فهو المحق، وعلى كل واحد معرفة الحق فيما يدين هو به . ويقول القاضي عبد الجبار في الفصل الذي عقده في كتاب المغني في بيان فساد التقليد : إعلم ، أن القول به يؤدي إلى جحد الضرورة ؛ لأن تقليد من يقول بقدم الأجسام ليس بأولى من تقليد من يقول بحدوثها . فيجب إما أن يعتقد حدوثها وقدمها ، وذلك محال ، أو يخرج عن كلا الاعتقادين ، وهو محال أيضاً. وكذلك القول في سائر المذاهب .... وليس له أن يقول : أقلد للأكثر في ذلك ، وذلك لأن المحق قد يكون واحدا ، والمبطل قد يكثر جمعه ، فيجب أن يعرف الحق بغير هذه الطريقة . فهل يدل هذا الاتفاق بين الرجلين على أثر الماتريدي على شيخ الاعتزال ؟ نحن لا نستبعد أن يكون القاضي عبد الجبار قد تأثر في ذلك بالماتريدي . إننا القاضي عبد الجبار نشأ على السنة على مذهب الأشعري قبل المعتزلة . ونعلم أن الأشعري وإن كان قد ذم التقليد إلا أنه لم يذكر هذه الأدلة التي نجدها عند الماتريدي وعند القاضي عبد الجبار من بعده . وإذا كان التقليد باطلاً مرفوضاً من العقل فإنه مذموم أيضاً من الشرع . فالله سبحانه وتعالى قد ذم التقليد والمقلدين في كتابه الكريم ، قال تعالى : "إنا وَجَدْنَا آبَاءَنَا عَلَى أُمَّةٍ وَإِنَّا عَلَى آثَارِهِمْ مُقْتَدُونَ" وقال تعالى : "أَوَلَوْ كَانَ آبَاؤُهُمْ لَا يَعْقِلُونَ شَيْئًا وَلَا يَهْتَدُونَ" وقال تعالى : "وَقَالُوا رَبَّنَا إِنَّا أَطَعْنَا سَادَتَنَا وَكُبَرَاءَنَا فَأَضَلُّونَا السَّبِيلا" وغير ذلك كثير في التنزيل.
Maturidi says, opening Kitab al-Tawhid: 'To proceed, we found people differing in sects in religion, agreeing in their disagreement on religion on one word: that what he is upon is true, and what others are upon is false, with their total agreement that each has predecessors he follows blindly. So it is established that taqlid (blind following) is not something that excuses its practitioner because the like of it is against him, for it contains only multitude of number. Unless one who is referred to in speech has an intellectual proof by which his truth in what he claims is known, and a demonstration that subdues the fair-minded to his possessing the truth. So whoever is referred to in religion by that which necessitates its verification is the right one, and upon everyone is the knowledge of the truth in what he practices as religion.' And Qadi Abd al-Jabbar says in the chapter he devoted in the book al-Mughni on explaining the corruption of taqlid: 'Know that adhering to it leads to denial of the necessary (knowledge); because following one who says bodies are eternal is not more appropriate than following one who says they are originated. So either one must believe both their eternity and origination, and that is impossible, or abandon both beliefs, and that is also impossible. And likewise for all other doctrines. ... And he cannot say: I follow the majority in that, because the truthful may be one, and the false may be many in number. So the truth must be known by other than this method.' So does this agreement between the two men indicate the influence of Maturidi upon the shaykh of the Mu'tazila? We do not rule out that Qadi Abd al-Jabbar was influenced in that by Maturidi. We know that Qadi Abd al-Jabbar grew up upon the Sunnah upon the Ash'ari school before [joining] the Mu'tazila. And we know that al-Ash'ari, even though he condemned taqlid, did not mention these proofs that we find with Maturidi and with Qadi Abd al-Jabbar after him. And if taqlid is invalid and rejected by reason, it is also condemned by law. For Allah Almighty has condemned taqlid and those who practice it in His Noble Book. He said: 'Indeed we found our fathers upon a path [religion] and indeed we are following in their footsteps.' And He said: 'Even if their fathers did not understand anything nor were guided?' And He said: 'And they said: Our Lord, we obeyed our masters and our great ones, so they led us astray from the way.' And many other such [verses] in the Revelation.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage presents the core argument against taqlid from Maturidi's opening of Kitab al-Tawhid. Maturidi observes that people differ in religious sects yet each claims to be right and the other wrong, but all rely on blind following of predecessors. He concludes that taqlid is not a valid excuse because the same could be said for the opponent, and it only provides numerical majority. He then states that only a rational proof that compels fairness can establish truth. Then the passage quotes Qadi Abd al-Jabbar's similar argument from al-Mughni: that following one view over another arbitrarily leads to contradiction; either accept both views (impossible) or reject both (also impossible), so one must know truth by a different method. The passage then discusses the possibility of Maturidi's influence on Qadi Abd al-Jabbar, noting that al-Ash'ari also condemned taqlid but did not present these specific arguments. Finally, it cites Quranic verses that condemn taqlid of ancestors and leaders, showing that taqlid is rejected both rationally and scripturally.

Key Terms
تقليد
حجة عقل
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taqlid is acceptable if one follows the majority or a respected scholar.
Correction: Maturidi argues that truth is not determined by numbers or authority; only rational proof and demonstration can establish truth, making taqlid of any kind insufficient.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the only thing that can excuse one from the charge of falsehood in religious claims?
A) Following the majority
B) Having a rational proof
C) Being from a large group
D) Relying on predecessors

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's opening establishes that taqlid is inherently flawed because it provides no way to distinguish truth from falsehood; only rational proof and demonstration can establish truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend your beliefs to someone of another religion, do you rely on arguments you have inherited or on reasoning you have verified for yourself? What would it cost you to examine your faith with the same scrutiny you demand of others?
?
If you discovered that a belief you hold was based solely on what you were taught, without any rational proof, would you be willing to abandon it? How would you go about finding the truth?
?
Maturidi says everyone is obliged to know the truth in what they practice. What is the one belief you are most afraid to question, and why? What would it mean to subject it to an honest intellectual proof?

Qadi Abd al-Jabbar's Logical Rejection of Taqlid
Qadi Abd al-Jabbar provides a concise rational argument refuting taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Qadi Abd al-Jabbar's argument that taqlid leads to logical contradictions, making it an untenable method for knowing truth.

Core Idea
Taqlid forces you to accept contradictory beliefs or abandon all belief — both impossible.

Qadi Abd al-Jabbar, a leading Mu'tazili theologian, presents a crisp logical argument against taqlid in his work al-Mughni. He shows that if you blindly follow one opinion without evidence, you have no principled way to decide between contradictory claims. This lesson unpacks his argument and its devastating conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read Qadi's argument carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that he says taqlid leads to 'juhud al-darura' (denial of necessary knowledge).
2
Understand the example
He gives the example of bodies being eternal vs. originated. If you blindly follow one view, you have no reason to prefer it over the other.
3
Identify the two impossible outcomes
Either you must believe both views (contradiction) or reject both (also impossible because one must be true).
4
Notice the rejection of majority argument
Qadi rejects the idea that following the majority is a solution, because truth may be with the minority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول الماتريدي مفتتحاً كتاب التوحيد : أما بعد ، فإنا وجدنا الناس مختلفي المذاهب في النحل في الدين ، متفقين على اختلافهم في الدين على كلمة واحدة : أن الذي هو عليه حق ، والذي عليه غيره باطل ، على اتفاق جملتهم في أن كلاً له سلف يقلد ، فثبت أن التقليد ليس مما يعذر صاحبه لإصابة مثله ضده على أنه ليس فيه سوى كثرة العدد ، اللهم إلا أن يكون لأحد ممن ينتهي القول إليه حجة عقل يعلم بها صدقه فيما يدعي ، وبرهان يقهر المنصفين على إصابته الحق ، فمن إليه مرجعه في الدين بما يوجب تحقيقة عنه فهو المحق، وعلى كل واحد معرفة الحق فيما يدين هو به . ويقول القاضي عبد الجبار في الفصل الذي عقده في كتاب المغني في بيان فساد التقليد : إعلم ، أن القول به يؤدي إلى جحد الضرورة ؛ لأن تقليد من يقول بقدم الأجسام ليس بأولى من تقليد من يقول بحدوثها . فيجب إما أن يعتقد حدوثها وقدمها ، وذلك محال ، أو يخرج عن كلا الاعتقادين ، وهو محال أيضاً. وكذلك القول في سائر المذاهب .... وليس له أن يقول : أقلد للأكثر في ذلك ، وذلك لأن المحق قد يكون واحدا ، والمبطل قد يكثر جمعه ، فيجب أن يعرف الحق بغير هذه الطريقة . فهل يدل هذا الاتفاق بين الرجلين على أثر الماتريدي على شيخ الاعتزال ؟ نحن لا نستبعد أن يكون القاضي عبد الجبار قد تأثر في ذلك بالماتريدي . إننا القاضي عبد الجبار نشأ على السنة على مذهب الأشعري قبل المعتزلة . ونعلم أن الأشعري وإن كان قد ذم التقليد إلا أنه لم يذكر هذه الأدلة التي نجدها عند الماتريدي وعند القاضي عبد الجبار من بعده . وإذا كان التقليد باطلاً مرفوضاً من العقل فإنه مذموم أيضاً من الشرع . فالله سبحانه وتعالى قد ذم التقليد والمقلدين في كتابه الكريم ، قال تعالى : "إنا وَجَدْنَا آبَاءَنَا عَلَى أُمَّةٍ وَإِنَّا عَلَى آثَارِهِمْ مُقْتَدُونَ" وقال تعالى : "أَوَلَوْ كَانَ آبَاؤُهُمْ لَا يَعْقِلُونَ شَيْئًا وَلَا يَهْتَدُونَ" وقال تعالى : "وَقَالُوا رَبَّنَا إِنَّا أَطَعْنَا سَادَتَنَا وَكُبَرَاءَنَا فَأَضَلُّونَا السَّبِيلا" وغير ذلك كثير في التنزيل.

Checkpoint
What logical consequence does Qadi Abd al-Jabbar derive from blindly following a claim about the eternity of bodies?

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about God or religion that you have never critically examined—perhaps one you inherited from family or community. Qadi argues that such blind acceptance (taqlid) can lead to logical absurdity. Are you willing to honestly investigate that belief using evidence and reason, even if it means challenging what you’ve always assumed? What concrete step will you take this week to begin that investigation?
?
When you encounter two contradictory views among scholars or Muslims, do you tend to side with the majority or the more popular opinion? Qadi says 'the truthful may be one, and the false may be many in number.' How does that statement unsettle your default approach? Will you commit to seeking truth by examining evidence, not by counting followers?
?
Qadi states that taqlid leads to 'denial of necessary knowledge'—rejecting what is rationally obvious. Have you ever suppressed a rational doubt or refused to follow a logical argument because it conflicted with what you were taught? What is holding you back from fully submitting to the truth, wherever it leads? How can you change that today?

Quranic Condemnation and Historical Connections
Cites Quranic verses against taqlid and discusses potential influence between theologians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran condemns taqlid of ancestors and leaders, and how this aligns with the rational arguments of Maturidi and Qadi Abd al-Jabbar.

Core Idea
The Quran condemns blind following as a rejection of reason and guidance.

After presenting rational arguments against taqlid, the passage concludes by citing Quranic verses that explicitly condemn the practice of blindly following ancestors and leaders. This lesson examines those verses and also considers the historical possibility that Qadi Abd al-Jabbar was influenced by Maturidi's earlier argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses cited
Read the Arabic and translation of each verse. Note the context: they criticize those who follow ancestors without thought.
2
Understand how each verse condemns taqlid
Verse 43:23 shows people justifying their practice by saying 'we found our fathers on a path'. Verse 2:170 questions whether they would follow even if their fathers lacked reason. Verse 33:67 shows the regret of followers on Judgment Day.
3
Note the author's historical observation
The author suggests that Qadi Abd al-Jabbar may have been influenced by Maturidi, since al-Ash'ari did not present these specific rational arguments.
4
Reflect on the convergence of reason and scripture
Both rational arguments and Quranic verses independently reject taqlid, reinforcing that faith should be based on knowledge and evidence.

يقول الماتريدي مفتتحاً كتاب التوحيد : أما بعد ، فإنا وجدنا الناس مختلفي المذاهب في النحل في الدين ، متفقين على اختلافهم في الدين على كلمة واحدة : أن الذي هو عليه حق ، والذي عليه غيره باطل ، على اتفاق جملتهم في أن كلاً له سلف يقلد ، فثبت أن التقليد ليس مما يعذر صاحبه لإصابة مثله ضده على أنه ليس فيه سوى كثرة العدد ، اللهم إلا أن يكون لأحد ممن ينتهي القول إليه حجة عقل يعلم بها صدقه فيما يدعي ، وبرهان يقهر المنصفين على إصابته الحق ، فمن إليه مرجعه في الدين بما يوجب تحقيقة عنه فهو المحق، وعلى كل واحد معرفة الحق فيما يدين هو به . ويقول القاضي عبد الجبار في الفصل الذي عقده في كتاب المغني في بيان فساد التقليد : إعلم ، أن القول به يؤدي إلى جحد الضرورة ؛ لأن تقليد من يقول بقدم الأجسام ليس بأولى من تقليد من يقول بحدوثها . فيجب إما أن يعتقد حدوثها وقدمها ، وذلك محال ، أو يخرج عن كلا الاعتقادين ، وهو محال أيضاً. وكذلك القول في سائر المذاهب .... وليس له أن يقول : أقلد للأكثر في ذلك ، وذلك لأن المحق قد يكون واحدا ، والمبطل قد يكثر جمعه ، فيجب أن يعرف الحق بغير هذه الطريقة . فهل يدل هذا الاتفاق بين الرجلين على أثر الماتريدي على شيخ الاعتزال ؟ نحن لا نستبعد أن يكون القاضي عبد الجبار قد تأثر في ذلك بالماتريدي . إننا القاضي عبد الجبار نشأ على السنة على مذهب الأشعري قبل المعتزلة . ونعلم أن الأشعري وإن كان قد ذم التقليد إلا أنه لم يذكر هذه الأدلة التي نجدها عند الماتريدي وعند القاضي عبد الجبار من بعده . وإذا كان التقليد باطلاً مرفوضاً من العقل فإنه مذموم أيضاً من الشرع . فالله سبحانه وتعالى قد ذم التقليد والمقلدين في كتابه الكريم ، قال تعالى : "إنا وَجَدْنَا آبَاءَنَا عَلَى أُمَّةٍ وَإِنَّا عَلَى آثَارِهِمْ مُقْتَدُونَ" وقال تعالى : "أَوَلَوْ كَانَ آبَاؤُهُمْ لَا يَعْقِلُونَ شَيْئًا وَلَا يَهْتَدُونَ" وقال تعالى : "وَقَالُوا رَبَّنَا إِنَّا أَطَعْنَا سَادَتَنَا وَكُبَرَاءَنَا فَأَضَلُّونَا السَّبِيلا" وغير ذلك كثير في التنزيل.
Maturidi says, opening Kitab al-Tawhid: 'To proceed, we found people differing in sects in religion, agreeing in their disagreement on religion on one word: that what he is upon is true, and what others are upon is false, with their total agreement that each has predecessors he follows blindly. So it is established that taqlid (blind following) is not something that excuses its practitioner because the like of it is against him, for it contains only multitude of number. Unless one who is referred to in speech has an intellectual proof by which his truth in what he claims is known, and a demonstration that subdues the fair-minded to his possessing the truth. So whoever is referred to in religion by that which necessitates its verification is the right one, and upon everyone is the knowledge of the truth in what he practices as religion.' And Qadi Abd al-Jabbar says in the chapter he devoted in the book al-Mughni on explaining the corruption of taqlid: 'Know that adhering to it leads to denial of the necessary (knowledge); because following one who says bodies are eternal is not more appropriate than following one who says they are originated. So either one must believe both their eternity and origination, and that is impossible, or abandon both beliefs, and that is also impossible. And likewise for all other doctrines. ... And he cannot say: I follow the majority in that, because the truthful may be one, and the false may be many in number. So the truth must be known by other than this method.' So does this agreement between the two men indicate the influence of Maturidi upon the shaykh of the Mu'tazila? We do not rule out that Qadi Abd al-Jabbar was influenced in that by Maturidi. We know that Qadi Abd al-Jabbar grew up upon the Sunnah upon the Ash'ari school before [joining] the Mu'tazila. And we know that al-Ash'ari, even though he condemned taqlid, did not mention these proofs that we find with Maturidi and with Qadi Abd al-Jabbar after him. And if taqlid is invalid and rejected by reason, it is also condemned by law. For Allah Almighty has condemned taqlid and those who practice it in His Noble Book. He said: 'Indeed we found our fathers upon a path [religion] and indeed we are following in their footsteps.' And He said: 'Even if their fathers did not understand anything nor were guided?' And He said: 'And they said: Our Lord, we obeyed our masters and our great ones, so they led us astray from the way.' And many other such [verses] in the Revelation.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage presents the core argument against taqlid from Maturidi's opening of Kitab al-Tawhid. Maturidi observes that people differ in religious sects yet each claims to be right and the other wrong, but all rely on blind following of predecessors. He concludes that taqlid is not a valid excuse because the same could be said for the opponent, and it only provides numerical majority. He then states that only a rational proof that compels fairness can establish truth. Then the passage quotes Qadi Abd al-Jabbar's similar argument from al-Mughni: that following one view over another arbitrarily leads to contradiction; either accept both views (impossible) or reject both (also impossible), so one must know truth by a different method. The passage then discusses the possibility of Maturidi's influence on Qadi Abd al-Jabbar, noting that al-Ash'ari also condemned taqlid but did not present these specific arguments. Finally, it cites Quranic verses that condemn taqlid of ancestors and leaders, showing that taqlid is rejected both rationally and scripturally.

Key Terms
تقليد
آيات
سلف
اتباع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran teaches to follow one's ancestors without question.
Correction: The Quran explicitly criticizes blind following of ancestors and leaders, as shown in these verses.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse states that people will say on Judgment Day, 'Our Lord, we obeyed our masters and our great ones, so they led us astray'?
A) 2:170
B) 43:23
C) 33:67
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Both reason and scripture converge in condemning taqlid, compelling believers to seek knowledge based on evidence and rational proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there beliefs or practices in your faith that you have inherited from your family or community without ever verifying them against the Quran and reason? Where might you be blindly following tradition without evidence?
?
The Qur’an warns of followers blaming their leaders on Judgement Day. Reflect: Are there people you look up to whose teachings you accept without scrutiny? What would you say if you stood before Allah and could not justify your beliefs by revelation and reason?
?
This lesson teaches that both reason and revelation reject taqlid. Have you ever made a conscious effort to understand the proofs behind your core beliefs, or do you rely on the reputation of scholars? What specific belief will you seek to verify with evidence starting today?

Sensory Knowledge as a Valid Source
Maturidi argues for the validity of sensory knowledge and refutes its denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that sensory knowledge is a self-evident source of certainty and how he refutes those who deny it.

Core Idea
Sensory knowledge is self-evident; its denial is mere stubbornness.

Imam Maturidi begins by affirming that sensory perception provides us with direct knowledge of pleasure, pain, existence, and perishing. He then addresses those who deny sensory knowledge, characterizing them as stubborn and unreasonable. In this passage, he offers a powerful practical proof: if you inflict pain on such a person, their own reaction will betray their denial, proving that sensory knowledge is irrefutable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument.
2
Identify the scope of sensory knowledge
Notice that Maturidi lists what we know through the senses: pleasure, pain, existence, perishing.
3
Understand the refutation strategy
See how Maturidi proposes to confront the denier: by inflicting pain and then mirroring their denial back at them.
This exposes the inconsistency in the denier's position.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why sensory evidence is considered a valid source of knowledge in Islamic theology.
This connects to the broader methodology of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالحس نعلم ما به نلذ ونتألم وما به نبقى ونفنى، وكل من ينكر المعرفة الحسية فهو مكابر متعنت لا تصح مناظرته؛ لأنه ينكر ما يعاينه بنفسه. ويكفي أن نؤلمه بالألم الشديد من ضرب أو قطع حتى يعترف بأنه يتألم، فنقابله حينئذ بتعنت مثل تعنته، منكرين عليه ألمه ومعرفته بألمله حتى يجزع ويضجر وينتهك ستره
By the senses we know that by which we experience pleasure and pain, and that by which we continue to exist and perish. And whoever denies sensory knowledge is a stubborn obstinate person whose debate is not valid, because he denies what he himself witnesses. It is sufficient that we inflict severe pain upon him by beating or cutting until he admits that he is in pain. Then we confront him with a stubbornness like his own, denying him his pain and his knowledge of his pain until he becomes distressed and annoyed and his covering is torn.
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the validity of sensory knowledge. Maturidi argues that sensory knowledge is self-evident; denying it is mere stubbornness. He gives a practical argument: if you cause someone pain, they will acknowledge it, proving that sensory perception is real. The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum: the denier's own reaction disproves his stance. The passage follows the logical order: first asserting the domain of sensory knowledge (pleasure, pain, existence, perishing), then characterizing the denier as stubborn, then offering the empirical test of inflicting pain, and finally suggesting a retort of equal stubbornness to expose the contradiction.

Key Terms
الحس
المعرفة الحسية
مكابر
تعنت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that kalam relies only on rational proofs and that sensory evidence is not considered valid in Islamic theology.
Correction: Imam Maturidi explicitly defends sensory knowledge as a valid source, using a practical argument that even a denier's own reaction proves its veracity.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi propose to refute someone who denies sensory knowledge?
A) By citing scripture
B) By logical deduction from first principles
C) By inflicting pain on them and observing their reaction
D) By appealing to consensus

Key Takeaway
Sensory knowledge is a self-evident truth; its denial is logically self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you acted as a mukabir, denying something evident to your senses—like the signs of Allah in creation—because of pride or stubbornness? What is one area today where you need to stop resisting and admit what you already sense to be true?
?
Maturidi says even severe pain can bring a denier to admission. Has Allah ever used a trial or hardship to break your stubbornness and force you to acknowledge Him? How did that change you, and what are you still resisting?
?
Your senses are a gift from Allah to recognize His signs. Which sensory evidence of His existence (e.g., the taste of rain, the warmth of the sun, the complexity of your own hand) have you been overlooking? Commit to savoring one such sign today and turning it into a moment of gratitude.

Reports as a Source of Certain Knowledge
Maturidi classifies reports and defends prophetic reports as the most certain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that reports, especially prophetic reports accompanied by miracles, provide certain knowledge and why denying them is irrational.

Core Idea
Prophetic reports, backed by miracles, give the most certain knowledge.

Maturidi begins by acknowledging that many things we know—like names, history, and distant places—come from reports. He then argues that prophetic reports are the most truthful because they are supported by clear miracles. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows how it grounds religious knowledge in evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: from general reports to prophetic reports.
The Arabic has OCR issues: 'الخير' should be 'الخبر' and 'يينا' likely 'بينا'.
2
Identify types of report-based knowledge
List the categories Maturidi mentions: names, genealogies, past events, distant lands, benefits and harms—anything we cannot witness directly.
3
Understand why prophetic reports are clearest
Maturidi says there is no report more clearly truthful than that of the messengers. Ask: What evidence does he cite?
Look for the word 'الآيات' (miracles).
4
Examine the role of miracles
Maturidi states that miracles demonstrate the truthfulness of prophets. This is what makes their reports certain, unlike ordinary reports.
The text says 'الآيات الموضحة صدقهم'.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by saying that anyone who denies this is stubborn and obstinate (تعنت ومكابرة). Why does Maturidi say this?
Because the evidence is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الخير أسمائنا ونسبنا وأسماء الأشياء جميعاً، كما نعرف بالخير الحوادث الماضية والبلاد النائية والمنافع والمضار وكل ما لا تتسع حياتنا لمعاينته بأنفسنا وأخبار الرسل ولا خبر أظهر صدقاً. الآيات الموضحة صدقهم؛ إذ لا يوجد خبر يطمئن إليه القلب يينا من المعارف التي يصير منكر ذلك متعنتاً بضرورة العقل - أوضح صدقاً أخبار الرسل صلوات الله عليهم، فمن أنكر ذلك فهو أحق من يقضي عليه بالتعنت والمكابرة
As for reports: our names, genealogies, and the names of all things [are known through reports], just as we know through reports past events, distant lands, benefits and harms, and everything that our lives do not allow us to witness ourselves. And the reports of the messengers—there is no report more clearly truthful. The miracles [الآيات] demonstrating their truthfulness; for there is no report that the heart finds reassuring [؟] among the sciences that the denier of that becomes stubborn by necessity of reason—more clearly truthful are the reports of the messengers, peace be upon them. Whoever denies that is more deserving of being judged stubborn and obstinate.
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage classifies reports (khabar) as a source of knowledge. Maturidi explains that we know many things through reports, including names, genealogies, historical events, distant lands, benefits and harms—anything we cannot witness directly. He then distinguishes prophetic reports as the most certain, because they are accompanied by miracles (al-ayat) that demonstrate their truthfulness. The argument concludes that denying such reports is extreme stubbornness and obstinacy. The passage follows the order: first describing the scope of report-based knowledge, then elevating prophetic reports as the clearest, then citing miracles as evidence, and finally asserting the irrationality of denial.

أخبار الرسل
الآيات
تعنت
مكابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophetic reports are accepted blindly without evidence.
Correction: Maturidi grounds prophetic reports in evidentiary miracles, making them rationally certain.

Checkpoint

B) The miracles that demonstrate their truthfulness
C) The consensus of scholars
D) Their consistency with reason

Key Takeaway
Prophetic reports, validated by miracles, are the most certain form of report-based knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you read a hadith that challenges your habits or assumptions, does your heart find it reassuring because of its divine backing, or do you feel stubborn resistance? How can you cultivate the certainty that Maturidi describes?
?
If the evidence for prophethood is as clear as the passage claims, where does your doubt—if any—come from? Are you willing to repent from stubbornness and submit fully to the prophetic reports?

Reflection vs. Blind Imitation: The Intellect as a Light
Maturidi exhorts to reflection and condemns blind imitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic imperative to use reflection (al-nazar) and the condemnation of blind imitation (al-taqlid) as a nullification of the intellect's purpose.

Core Idea
Your intellect is a candle; do not extinguish it by blind imitation.

Imam Maturidi emphasizes that Allah Himself commands us to reflect and take heed. He then warns that blind imitation robs the intellect of its very purpose, using a vivid metaphor: a candle given to light one's way should not be extinguished. This lesson unpacks the call to reflection and the danger of mindless following.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Then read the English translation. Notice the key terms: النظر (reflection), التقليد (blind imitation), التفكر (pondering).
The passage is a concise exhortation with a striking metaphor.
2
Identify the two commands
Find the three actions Allah urges: حثنا على النظر (urged reflection), رغبنا فيه (desired it), الزمنا الاعتبار (obligated heed). Understand that reflection is not optional.
3
Understand the condemnation of taqlid
The author declares taqlid as إبطال منفعة العقل (nullification of the intellect's benefit). Reflect on why blind imitation negates the purpose of the intellect.
The intellect was created for pondering and contemplation.
4
Analyze the candle metaphor
The candle symbolizes the intellect. To extinguish it and walk in darkness is described as قبيح (ugly). Consider how following without evidence is like voluntarily stumbling in the dark.
This metaphor reinforces the moral weight of using one's intellect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك فإن الله سبحانه وتعالى قد حثنا على النظر ورغبنا فيه والزمنا الاعتبار وما التقليد إلا إبطال منفعة العقل لأنه إنما خلق وأمرنا بالتفكر والتدبر للتأمل والتدبر، وقبيح بمن أعطى شمعة يستضى بها أن يطفئها ويمشي في الظلمة.
Likewise, Allah Almighty has urged us to reflection (al-nazar) and desired it for us and obligated us to take heed, and blind imitation (al-taqlid) is nothing but the nullification of the benefit of the intellect, because He only created and commanded us to ponder and contemplate, and it is ugly for one who was given a candle to light his way to extinguish it and walk in darkness.
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage is an exhortation. The author begins by stating that Allah Himself has urged and desired reflection (nazar) for us, and made it obligatory to take heed (i'tibar). He then declares that blind imitation (taqlid) effectively nullifies the very benefit for which the intellect was given. The reasoning is that Allah created the intellect and commanded us to use it for pondering and contemplation. The author drives the point home with a vivid metaphor: it is ugly for a person who has been given a candle to light his way to extinguish it and walk in darkness. The candle represents the intellect; extinguishing it represents abandoning reflection in favor of blind imitation.

Key Terms

التقليد
التفكر
الاعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A candle that lights the way

C) A person who refuses to see
D) A command to ponder

Key Takeaway
Reflection is a divine command and blind imitation extinguishes the light of the intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When was the last time you sincerely pondered (tafakkur) a belief you hold, rather than accepting it because it was taught to you?
?

Maturidi's Three Sources of Knowledge
Maturidi enumerates the three sources of knowledge and defends them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's epistemology: he identifies three necessary sources of knowledge (sense, report, reason) and explains why each is indispensable, with special attention to sensory knowledge and the validation of prophetic reports.

Core Idea
Knowledge comes from sense, report, and reason — each source covers what the others cannot.

Imam Maturidi lays out his theory of knowledge, starting with the claim that human beings access truth through three distinct channels: sensory perception, transmitted reports, and reason. These three are not alternatives but complementary necessities. In this passage, Maturidi explains why each source is indispensable and how to evaluate the types of report, especially prophetic reports. He also provides a striking argument against those who would deny sensory knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three-source claim
Notice that Maturidi presents sense, report, and reason as three paths to knowledge; each gives access to a class of knowledge unavailable otherwise.
This is the foundation of his epistemology.
2
Examine the defense of sensory knowledge
See how Maturidi argues against denial of sense-perception by pointing to the self-refuting nature of such denial and the practical test of inflicting pain.
This shows the absurdity of rejecting sense experience.
3
Distinguish between types of report
Learn the two categories: mutawatir (requires scrutiny) and prophetic reports (validated by miracles, the most clearly truthful).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعرض الماتريدي في الكتاب نظرية في المعرفة، ويناقش قيمة معارفنا ومعيار الحق في المعارف التي تصل إلينا عن طريق الحس والخبر والعقل عنده سبلنا إلى العلم بحقائق الأشياء، ولا نستطيع أن نستغني في المعرفة عن هذه المصادر؛ لأن لكل مصدر طائفة من المعارف لا سبيل إلى الوصول إليها إلا عن طريق هذا المصدر. بالحس نعلم ما به نلتذ ونتألم وما به نبقى ونفنى، وكل من ينكر المعرفة الحسية فهو مكابر متعنت لا تصح مناظرته؛ لأنه ينكر ما يعاينه بنفسه. ويكفي أن نؤلمه بالألم الشديد من ضرب أو قطع حتى يعترف بأنه يتألم، فنقابله حينئذ بتعنت مثل تعنته، منكرين عليه ألمه ومعرفته بألمه حتى يجزع ويضجر وينهتك ستره. أو شهادة الغير بلغة العصر الحديث - فهو وسيلتنا إلى معرفة أسمائنا ونسبنا وأسماء الأشياء جميعاً، كما نعرف بالخبر الحوادث الماضية والبلاد النائية والمنافع والمضار وكل ما لا تتسع حياتنا لمعاينته بأنفسنا. ويميز الماتريدي بين نوعين من الخبر: أولهما خبر المتواتر، وينبغي النظر فيه وتمحيصه حتى يثبت صدقه. وثانيها أخبار الرسل، ولا خبر أظهر صدقاً من خبرهم بما معهم من الآيات الموضحة صدقهم؛ إذ لا يوجد خبر يطمئن إليه القلب أظهر صدقاً من أخبار الرسل صلوات الله عليهم، فمن أنكر ذلك فهو أحق من يقضي عليه بالتعنت والمكابرة.
And al-Maturidi presents in the book a theory of knowledge, and discusses the value of our cognitions and the criterion of truth in the cognitions that reach us through sense (al-hiss), report (al-khabar), and reason (al-'aql); in his view they are our paths to knowledge of the realities of things, and we cannot dispense with these sources in knowledge, because each source has a class of cognitions that can only be reached through that source. By sense we know what we take pleasure in and suffer pain from, and what we remain and perish by, and whoever denies sensory knowledge is a stubborn obdurate person whose disputation is not valid, because he denies what he himself perceives. And it suffices that we inflict severe pain on him by beating or cutting until he admits that he is suffering, then we meet him with stubbornness like his stubbornness, denying his pain and his knowledge of his pain until he becomes distressed and annoyed and his veil is torn. Or the testimony of another in modern language - it is our means to know our names, lineages, and the names of all things, just as we know by report past events, distant lands, benefits and harms, and everything that our life does not allow us to perceive ourselves. And al-Maturidi distinguishes between two types of report: first, the mutawatir report, which must be examined and scrutinized until its truthfulness is established. Second, the reports of the messengers, and there is no report more clearly truthful than their report along with the signs clarifying their truthfulness; for there is no report that reassures the heart more clearly truthful than the reports of the messengers, peace be upon them, and whoever denies that is most deserving of being judged as stubborn and obdurate.
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of Maturidi's epistemology. The author states that Maturidi presents a theory of knowledge, discussing the value and criterion of truth for cognitions reaching us via three sources: sense (hiss), report (khabar), and reason (aql). Each source provides a unique class of knowledge that cannot be obtained otherwise. The author then elaborates on sensory knowledge: through sense we know pleasure, pain, survival, and perishing. Denial of sensory knowledge is stubbornness (mukabara) and such a person cannot be rationally debated because he denies what he himself experiences. The author suggests a practical refutation: inflict pain on the denier until he admits his suffering, then treat him with the same stubbornness. Next, the author discusses report (khabar) as a means to know things beyond our direct perception, like names, histories, distant places, etc. Maturidi distinguishes two types of report: mutawatir (mass-transmitted) which requires scrutiny, and reports of prophets which are validated by accompanying miracles (ayat). The author asserts that prophetic reports are the most clearly truthful, and denial of them deserves the charge of stubbornness and obstinacy.

Key Terms
الحس
الخبر
العقل
المتواتر
التعنت
المكابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason alone is sufficient for all knowledge.
Correction: Maturidi insists that sense and report are also necessary, as each source provides knowledge inaccessible through others.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence for someone who denies sensory knowledge?
A) He is considered a heretic
B) His disputation is invalid
C) He must be punished
D) He should be ignored

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that sense, report, and reason are all necessary and complementary for human knowledge, and prophetic reports are the most certain type of report.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you ignored the evidence of your own senses because accepting it would have required you to change a comfortable habit or belief? How does Maturidi's proof by pain expose the dishonesty of such denial in your own life?
?
You rely on reports from others every day, but do you give the reports of the prophets the same weight as a friend's news? What would it look like for you to treat a prophetic command as 'most clearly truthful' and act on it with immediate certainty?
?
Reflecting on your own pursuit of knowledge, which of the three sources—sense, report, or reason—do you find yourself most prone to neglect? If you are honest, how does that neglect affect your relationship with Allah and His messengers?

The Necessity of Reflection as the Arbiter
Imam Maturidi argues for the necessity of reflection as the arbiter in knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reflection (nazar) is necessary as the arbiter of sense and report, and how it leads to knowledge of the Creator and distinction between the Eternal and the originated.

Core Idea
Reflection is the indispensable arbiter that judges sense, report, and revelation.

Imam Maturidi opens this section by establishing the foundational role of reflection (nazar) in acquiring certain knowledge. He argues that nazar is not merely recommended but necessary, because it serves as the ultimate arbiter in matters that sense and report alone cannot settle. This passage sets the stage for understanding why intellectual reflection is central to Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the author argues for the necessity of nazar by listing its functions.
Pay attention to the phrase 'الحاكم الذي تحتكم إليه'.
2
Identify the functions of nazar
List the different roles nazar plays according to the passage: judging sense, judging report, distinguishing miracles from deceptions, revealing wisdom, and knowing the Eternal.
3
Reflect on the necessity argument
Consider why all these roles make nazar indispensable. How would we know these things without reflection?
This connects to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما النظر فيلزم القول به؛ لأنه الحاكم الذي تحتكم إليه في علم الحس والخبر وذلك فيما يبعد عن الحواس أو يلطف، وفيما يرد من الخبر أنه من نوع ما يحتمل الغلط أو لا، ثم آيات الرسل وتمويهات السحرة وغيرهم في التمييز بينها. وبالنظر نعرف ما في الخلق من الحكمة وما فيه من الدلالة على من أنشأه، كما نعرف به القديم ونميزه عن الحادث، وكل ذلك مما لا سبيل إلى العلم به إلا بالنظر... باستعمال العقول بالنظر في الأشياء.
As for reflection (al-nazar), it is necessary to affirm it, because it is the arbiter to which one refers in the knowledge of sense and report, regarding what is distant from the senses or subtle, and regarding what comes from report as to whether it is of the type that may contain error or not, and then the signs of the messengers and the deceptions of sorcerers and others in distinguishing between them. And by reflection we know the wisdom in creation and what it contains of indication of the One who originated it, just as we know by it the Eternal and distinguish Him from the originated, and all of that cannot be known except through reflection... by using intellects through reflection on things.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the necessity of reflection (nazar). The author states that nazar must be affirmed because it serves as the arbiter (hakim) to which we appeal in matters of sense and report. Specifically, nazar judges when something is beyond the reach of the senses or too subtle, and it evaluates reports to determine whether they might contain error. Furthermore, nazar is needed to distinguish between the miracles (ayat) of prophets and the deceptions (tamwih) of sorcerers and others. Through nazar, we come to know the wisdom in creation and the signs pointing to the Creator; through it we know the Eternal (al-qadim) and distinguish Him from the originated (al-hadith). All of this knowledge is only attainable through reflection, using intellects by reflecting on things.

Misconception: Some consider reflection (nazar) to be mere speculation or optional in matters of faith.
Correction: Imam Maturidi asserts that nazar is necessary (يلزم القول به) and is the arbiter for sense and report, leading to certain knowledge of the Creator and His attributes.

Checkpoint

A) It is optional for theologians
B) It is the arbiter that judges sense and report
C) It is only for evaluating reports
D) It is a substitute for sense

Key Takeaway
Reflection (nazar) is an indispensable intellectual tool that enables us to judge sensory and reported information, distinguish truth from falsehood, and attain knowledge of the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says nazar distinguishes miracles from deceptions. Where in your life might you be mistaking deception (from your own desires or from external sources) for truth? What step will you take to apply reflection to that area?
?
You routinely rely on reports—from scholars, media, friends. But the passage says reflection is necessary to evaluate whether a report may contain error. Which report have you accepted recently that you have not personally scrutinized? Will you examine it now?

The Necessity of Rational Speculation
The commentator argues for the necessity of rational speculation as the ultimate judge in knowledge acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational speculation (al-nazar) is necessary as the ultimate arbiter for knowledge beyond sense perception and transmitted reports.

Core Idea
Rational speculation is the ultimate judge for knowledge beyond the senses.

Imam Maturidi (or his commentator) opens by asserting the necessity of rational speculation. He explains that speculation serves as the arbiter that we rely on when dealing with matters beyond direct sensory perception or reports that may be prone to error. This passage establishes the foundational role of reason in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold.
Notice the dense style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify key terms
Recognize the Arabic terms: النظر (speculation), الحكم (arbiter), الحس (sense), الخبر (report), القديم (the Eternal), الحادث (the temporal). These are central.
These terms recur throughout Islamic theology.
3
Understand the argument structure
The passage presents speculation as necessary because it is the arbiter in four domains: sense and report (especially subtle matters), distinguishing miracles from sorcery, knowing wisdom in creation, and knowing the Eternal from the temporal.
Each domain shows a limitation of sense/report alone.
4
Reflect on the necessity
Ask yourself: Why can't sense or report alone provide certainty in these matters? The passage answers: because some matters are beyond senses (subtle) and reports may contain error.
This highlights the epistemological role of reason.
5
Connect to broader theology
Realize that this lesson underpins the entire methodology of kalam: reason is indispensable for knowing God, distinguishing truth from falsehood, and understanding creation.
This is a foundational principle for Maturidi theology.

Source Text

He created it, as well as the indication by which we know the eternal and distinguish it from the temporal, and all of that can only be known through speculation .... by using intellects in speculating about things.

It states that speculation is the ultimate arbiter (al-hakim) in matters that are beyond sense perception or report, such as subtle issues or distinguishing between true miracles and false deceptions. It also helps us understand the wisdom in creation and serves as a means to know the Eternal (al-qadim) and differentiate it from the temporal (al-hadith). The passage emphasizes that there is no way to attain such knowledge except through speculation using intellects.

The Opening: Refutation of Taqlid and the Obligation of Proof
Maturidi opens with praise, then states his thesis refuting taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's core argument that taqlid (blind imitation) is not a valid foundation for religious belief and that true religious knowledge requires proof (dalil).

Core Idea
Truth cannot be determined by following the crowd; it requires evidence.

Imam al-Maturidi begins his monumental work on Islamic theology by diagnosing the fundamental error of his contemporaries: they follow their ancestors without critical examination. He observes that every sect claims truth based on imitation, yet they disagree with each other—showing that imitation alone cannot distinguish truth from falsehood. This opening sets the methodological foundation for the entire book: religion must be known through proof, not blind acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage and thesis statement
Begin by reading the basmala and the thesis 'The refutation of taqlid and the obligation of knowing religion by proof.' Notice how Maturidi immediately announces his purpose.
The thesis is the lens through which to understand the rest of the passage.
2
Analyze Maturidi's observation about sectarian diversity
Maturidi notes that people are divided into sects, each claiming truth. Identify the key phrase: 'every one of them sees that what he himself holds is true and what others hold is false.' Reflect on why this observation is important.
3
Understand the argument against taqlid
Maturidi argues that since each sect imitates its own predecessors, and their claims contradict each other, taqlid cannot be a valid excuse. Find the sentence that concludes this point and trace the logic.
The key is that imitating opposite predecessors leads to contradictory beliefs, so imitation alone cannot guarantee truth.
4
Examine the exception and the obligation
Maturidi makes an exception for a ‘proof’ (hujja/ burhan) – someone who provides evidence that compels acceptance. Then he states that every person must know the truth in their religion. Ask yourself: who qualifies as a proof, and what does this mean for personal responsibility?
5
Review the entire argument structure
Go back and see how the passage moves from observation to conclusion, then to exception and final injunction. Notice the shift from descriptive to prescriptive.
This structure is typical of Maturidi’s systematic approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم
الحمد لله الذي كل حمد حمد به من دونه فراجع بالحقيقة إليه، حمدًا يوافى نعمه ويكافئ من يده ويبلغ رضاه، ونسأله أن يصلي على من ختم به الرسالة وعلى إخوانه من المرسلين وعلى أوليائه أجمعين، ونستعصم به من الزلل ونرغب إليه في أن يكرمنا من القول والعمل.
إبطال التقليد ووجوب معرفة الدين بالدليل
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله: أما بعد، فإنا وجدنا الناس مختلفي المذاهب في النحل في الدين، متفقين على اختلافهم في الدين على كلمة واحدة: أن كل واحد منهم يرى أن الذي عليه حق، والذي عليه غيره باطل، على اتفاق جملتهم الذي هو له سلف يُقلد، فثبت أن التقليد ليس مما يُعذر صاحبه لإصابة مثله منهم ضده، على أنه ليس فيه سوى كثرة العدد، اللهم إلا أن يكون الأحد ممن ينتهى القول إليه حجة عمل يعلم بها صدقه فيما يدعى، وبرهان يقهر المنصفين على إصابته الحق، فمن إليه مرجعه في الدين بما يوجب تحقيقه عنه فهو المحق، وعلى كل واحد منهم معرفة الحق فيما يدين به.
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to Allah, every praise praised by anyone besides Him returns in reality to Him, a praise that matches His graces and rewards His bounty and attains His pleasure. And we ask Him to bless the one with whom He sealed the message, and his brethren from the messengers, and all His saints. And we seek protection in Him from error and we desire from Him that He honor us with speech and action. The refutation of taqlid and the obligation of knowing religion by proof. The shaykh Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: To proceed, we have found people differing in sects in religion, agreeing on their differences in religion on one word: that every one of them sees that what he himself holds is true and what others hold is false, based on the agreement of their whole group which they follow as a predecessor to imitate. So it is established that taqlid is not something for which its practitioner is excused, because the likes of him are found on the opposite side; indeed, there is nothing in it except multitude of number. Unless that one is someone to whom final appeal is made, a proof whose action makes known his truthfulness in what he claims, and a demonstration that compels the fair-minded to his being correct. So whoever has his reference in religion to that which necessitates its verification from him, he is the one who is right. And upon every one of them is the knowledge of the truth in what he professes.
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage is the opening of Imam al-Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid. It begins with the basmala and a hamdalah praising Allah, then immediately announces the thesis: 'إبطال التقليد ووجوب معرفة الدين بالدليل' — the refutation of taqlid (blind imitation) and the obligation of knowing religion by proof. This sets the methodology of the work. Maturidi then presents an observation: people are divided into many sects and each sect believes its own position is true and others' false. They all agree on this, but the basis of their conviction is that they follow their predecessors (سلف) in blind imitation (تقليد). He argues that this situation proves taqlid is not a valid excuse, because just as one group follows its predecessors, another group does the same with an opposite view. There is no decisive criterion in taqlid except the number of followers, which is not a valid proof. Then he makes an exception: unless the one being followed is a 'حجة' (proof) and a 'برهان' (demonstration) — that is, a prophet or a source of knowledge that can demonstrate its truth through evidence that forces the fair-minded to accept it. Whoever relies on such a proof is truly correct. And every person individually is obligated to know the truth in what he professes. Thus, the passage establishes that religious knowledge must be based on evidence, not mere imitation.

Key Terms
تقليد (taqlid)
دليل (dalil)
حجة (hujja)
برهان (burhan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the exception of 'someone who is a proof' applies to any scholar or religious leader, so following such a figure is also taqlid but acceptable.
Correction: Maturidi means only a divinely appointed proof (like a prophet) whose truthfulness is established by clear evidence (miracles), not just any fallible human. The exception is not a license for blind following of scholars; rather, it points to the necessity of evidence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi in this passage, why does the existence of multiple conflicting sects who all practice taqlid prove that taqlid is not a valid excuse?
A) Because taqlid is forbidden in the Quran.
B) Because each sect's claim is based on following predecessors, and contradictory claims cannot both be true if based on mere imitation.
C) Because only the majority can be correct, and taqlid leads to sectarianism.
D) Because the predecessors themselves did not practice taqlid.

Key Takeaway
Religious belief cannot be based on blind imitation of ancestors; it must be grounded in clear proof and evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief about God, are you basing it on evidence that would compel a fair-minded person, or simply because you grew up with it? What would change if you took Maturidi's words seriously?
?
Maturidi says every person is obligated to know the truth in what they profess. Have you ever examined your own faith with the same scrutiny you apply to other areas of your life? If not, what is holding you back from that investigation?
?
You likely respect certain scholars. Could you articulate the proof that shows they are worthy of being followed in the way Maturidi describes—a hujja with a burhan—or are you relying on their reputation or your community's practice? Be honest with yourself.

The Invalidity of Taqlid (Blind Following)
Maturidi refutes taqlid by showing it leads to contradictory justifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi argues that blind following (taqlid) cannot be a valid basis for religious truth, because it leads to logical contradiction.

Core Idea
If taqlid were valid, all opposing sects would be equally correct – an impossibility.

Imam Maturidi begins by observing that people of different religions and sects all claim their own view is true and others false, yet each group follows its own predecessors blindly. He then points out that if this method were acceptable, both sides would have equal justification, which is contradictory. Therefore, mere following cannot establish truth, nor can numerical majority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the first part of the passage from 'أما بعد' to 'سوى كثرة العدد'. Notice the observation and the conclusion.
The author uses a logical argument from contradiction.
2
Identify the observation
The author notes that people of different creeds each believe their own view is true and others false, yet they all rely on following earlier authorities.
3
Understand the argument
If taqlid were a valid method, then each opposing group would be equally justified in their belief, which is impossible since truth cannot be contradictory. Therefore, taqlid is not an excuse.
4
Consider the rejection of majority
The author adds that taqlid offers nothing but numerical majority, which is not a criterion for truth.
This preempts the argument that following the majority is sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم الحمد لله الذي كل حمد حمد به من دونه فراجع بالحقيقة إليه، حمدا يوافى نعمه ويكافئ من يده ويبلغ رضاه، ونسأله أن يصلى على من ختم به الرسالة وعلى إخوانه من المرسلين وعلى أوليائه أجمعين، ونستعصم به من الزلل ونرغب إليه فيا يكرمنا من القول والعمل
إبطال التقليد ووجوب معرفة الدين بالدليل
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله: أما بعد، فإنا وجدنا الناس مختلفي المذاهب في النحل في الدين، متفقين على اختلافهم في الدين على كلمة واحدة: أن كلا عليه حق، والذي عليه غيره باطل، على اتفاق جملتهم الذي هو له سلف يُقلد، فثبت أن التقليد ليس مما يُعذر صاحبه لإصابة مثله منهم ضده، على أنه ليس فيه سوى كثرة العدد، اللهم إلا أن يكون الأحد ممن ينتهى القول إليه حجة عمل يعلم [بها] صدقه فيما يدعى، وبرهان يقهر المنصفين على إصابته الحق، فمن إليه مرجعه فى الدين بما يوجب تحقيقه عنه فهو المحق، وعلى كل واحد منهم معرفة الحق فيما يدين به، كأن الذي دان به هو مع [أدلة صاله؟] وشهادة الحق له - قد حضرهم؛ إذ منتهى حجج كل منهم التسليم له لو ظفر بها، وقد ظهرت لمن ذكرت، ولا يجوز ظهور مثلها لعدة في الدين؛ لما يتناقض حجج وأظهر تمويه أسباب الشبه في غيره، ولا قوة إلا بالله العظيم.
In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to God, to Whom every praise that is praised by others ultimately returns in reality – praise that matches His blessings, suffices from His hand, and attains His pleasure. And we ask Him to bless the one with whom He sealed the message, and his brothers among the messengers, and all His saints. And we seek His protection from error and desire from Him that He honor us in speech and deed.
Refutation of taqlid (blind following) and the obligation of knowing religion by proof.
The Shaykh Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: To proceed: We found people of different schools and creeds in religion, agreeing despite their differences on one word: that each one's own view is true and what others hold is false, by the consensus of their entire group, which has a predecessor they follow. So it is established that taqlid is not an excuse for its practitioner, because an opponent likewise has a similar (predecessor) against him, and there is nothing in it but numerical majority. Unless the one to whom the saying is referred is a proof whose actions show his truthfulness in what he claims, and a demonstration that compels the impartial to his correctness. So the one to whom recourse is made in religion by what necessitates verification from him is the truth-holder, and it is upon each of them to know the truth in what he professes, as if what he professes is [supported by] evidence and testimony of the truth for him – indeed it has attended them; for the end of each of their proofs is to submit to him if they were to obtain it, and it has appeared to whomever I mentioned, and it is not permissible that similar (proofs) appear to many in religion, because their proofs would contradict and show the concealment of causes of doubt in others. And there is no power except with God, the Almighty.
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of taqlid (blind following) and a statement of the obligation to know religion by proof. The author, Imam Maturidi, begins by noting that people of different sects all claim their own view is true and others false, yet they follow earlier authorities blindly. If taqlid were valid, opposing groups would both be justified, leading to contradiction. Therefore, taqlid is not an acceptable excuse. The only exception is a person who possesses a proof of his truthfulness and a demonstration that compels others. In that case, he is the one truly correct. Each person must know the truth in his religion, as if he has direct evidence. The author then argues that if multiple groups had equal proofs, they would contradict each other, which is impossible. Thus, only one can have the true proof.

Key Terms
تقليد
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Following the majority or a scholar without question is always valid in religion.
Correction: Maturidi shows that blind following leads to contradictions; truth requires evidence, not mere following.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Maturidi, why is taqlid (blind following) not a valid excuse in religion?
A) Because it is prohibited in the Quran.
B) Because it would mean that opposing groups both have truth, which is contradictory.
C) Because most people do not practice taqlid.
D) Because only the Prophet can be followed blindly.

Key Takeaway
Blind following (taqlid) is invalid because it would allow contradictory truths, so religious knowledge must be based on proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your life do you simply follow what you were raised to believe without having examined the evidence yourself? If God asked you tomorrow, 'Why did you believe that?' would you have an answer beyond 'My parents/teacher said so'?
?
You have just read that taqlid is no excuse before God. Are you willing to honestly re-examine even your most cherished beliefs, knowing that you might find them lacking? What is holding you back from seeking proof today?
?
Consider a time you were certain someone else was wrong in their faith. Did that certainty come from knowing their position deeply or from simply following your own school? This passage demands that you not rely on majority or tradition—so what concrete step will you take this week to verify one of your core beliefs against the Qur'an and authentic sunnah?
