From Reports to Prophetic Reports: The Role of Signs and Investigation
Maturidi argues that the necessity of reports in general entails the necessity of accepting prophetic reports, which are supported by signs and require investigation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rational necessity of accepting reports leads to the acceptance of prophetic reports, and why those reports require investigation despite their superiority.

Core Idea
Prophetic reports are the most certain form of report because they come with clear signs.

Maturidi now applies the general acceptance of reports to the specific case of prophetic messages. Since reports are necessary, and prophetic reports come with confirming signs (āyāt), they are even more deserving of acceptance. However, he also notes that the transmission of these reports involves human channels, so rational investigation (naẓar) is needed to verify them.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument from necessity to prophecy
Read the Arabic where Maturidi connects the general necessity of reports to the specific necessity of prophetic reports. Notice the phrase 'lazima qabūl akhbār al-rusul' (necessary to accept reports of messengers).
This is a crucial inference.
2
Identify the role of signs
Maturidi mentions that other reports can be doubted, but prophetic reports are accompanied by clear signs (āyāt) that remove doubt. Find this in the text.
These signs are miracles (mu'jizāt) in the broader context.
3
Understand the need for investigation
Despite their superiority, prophetic reports reach us through human transmitters who are not infallible. This is why we must examine them rationally.
This is the basis for the science of uṣūl al-dīn.
4
Reflect on the overall argument chain
Consider how the previous lessons (denial absurd, reports necessary) lead directly to accepting prophetic messages, but with a rational check.
Maturidi is establishing a balanced epistemology.

?

When was the last time you truly investigated a hadith you act upon? Will you commit to examining one hadith this week with its chain and meaning?
?
The Prophet's reports are the best because of signs, yet you may follow teachings from unverified sources. Do you even know the signs that prove the Prophet's truthfulness? If not, how can you be sure the reports you follow are from him?
?
Maturidi says the denier of prophetic reports is obstinate. But what about you—are you obstinate in your refusal to investigate? Is there a belief you hold about Islam that you have never critically examined? What would it cost you to look into it today?

Refuting Deniers of Reports and Sense Perception
Maturidi refutes deniers of khabar and 'iyan by showing their self-contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes those who deny the validity of reports (khabar) and sense perception ('iyan) by showing that their own actions contradict their denial.

Core Idea
Denying reports or observation is self-defeating: your own response proves you accept them.

Imam Maturidi presents a clever dialectical method to refute someone who rejects the fundamental sources of knowledge: reports and direct observation. Rather than arguing from scripture or abstract logic, he uses the denier's own behavior against him. The passage shows that any response to a question or any reaction to physical harm implicitly acknowledges the validity of the very sources being denied.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the two parallel parts: one about reports and one about observation.
The passage uses a question-and-response method.
2
Identify the two types of deniers
The passage explicitly addresses two deniers: one who denies reports (al-khabar) and one who denies direct observation (al-'iyan). Notice how the refutation is similar in structure.
3
Analyze the refutation for the denier of reports
Maturidi says to ask the denier a question. If he responds, his response itself is a kind of report, so he accepts the validity of reports. If he refuses to respond, you are safe.
The key is that any answer relies on hearing and speech.
4
Analyze the refutation for the denier of observation
For the denier of observation, similar logic: ask a question; if he responds, he knows but is stubborn. If he refuses, striking him leads him to complain, which requires naming your action (a report), thus contradicting his denial.
This shows that even physical reaction implies acceptance of reports.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم لا يوصل بهما إلى إدراك المحاسن والمساوئ التي لا يعمل العقول على الإحاطة [بها] إلا باستعمال الألسن بالتكلم بها وإدناء السمع إليها . وحق مناظرة هذا وإن كانت مناظرته سفها – فيمازج أيضا ، فنقول له عند إنكاره الخبر : ما تقول ؟ فإن عاد إليه فاعلم أنه قبل خبرك حيث عاد إلى قوله وهو استعادتك الخبر ، وإن لم يعد إليه كفيت شره وحمدت الله وضحكت ، منه ومثله لمن ينكر العيان ، تقول له : ما تقول ؟ فإن عاد إليه ظهر لك أنه يعلمه ، ولكنه يتعنت ، وإن لم يعد إليه ، كفيت شره ، وشكرت الله تعالى على ما ألهمك ، أو تضربه وتؤلم ، فإنه لا يقدر أن يضجر أو يقابلك بالعتاب لما لا يحتمل ذلك إلا بتسمية فعلك ، وذلك يعرف بالخير ، وقد أنكره ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Then they are not reached through them to the perception of good and evil qualities that minds cannot encompass except by using tongues to speak them and bringing hearing close to them. The right of debating this person, even if his debate is foolish – he also mixes – so we say to him when he denies the report: What do you say? If he returns to it, know that he has accepted your report because he returned to his statement and that is your repetition of the report to him. And if he does not return to it, you are spared his evil, you praise Allah and laugh. And similarly for one who denies direct observation: you say to him: What do you say? If he returns to it, it appears to you that he knows it, but he is stubborn. And if he does not return to it, you are spared his evil, and you thank Allah Almighty for what He inspired you. Or you strike him and cause him pain, for he cannot become annoyed or respond with reproach because that cannot happen except by naming your action, and that is known through a report, whereas he has denied it. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 607

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's refutation of those who deny the validity of reports (khabar) and direct observation ('iyan). It is a dialectical refutation. He first states that certain good and evil qualities cannot be perceived by minds alone but require the use of tongues and hearing. Then for the denier of reports, he proposes asking him a question: if he responds, that very response constitutes acceptance of your report (his answer depends on hearing your words). If he refuses to respond, you are safe from his harm. Similarly for the denier of observation: asking him a question reveals his knowledge, or striking him leads him to complain, which implicitly acknowledges the report of your action. This demonstrates that denying either leads to performative contradiction. The passage emphasizes the necessity of these basic modes of knowledge.

Key Terms
الخبر
العيان
المحاسن والمساوئ
المناظرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rejecting reports or observation is a valid rational stance.
Correction: Any attempt to argue against these sources relies on them, leading to self-contradiction.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi refute someone who denies the validity of reports?
A) He cites a Quranic verse.
B) He asks a question and observes whether the person responds.
C) He provides a logical proof from causality.
D) He ignores him as stubborn.

Key Takeaway
Denying reports or direct observation is self-refuting because any interaction with the denier implicitly relies on these sources.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you last rejected a truthful report because it was uncomfortable, what was your justification? How does this lesson force you to admit that your rejection itself relied on reports?
?
Think of a habit you have that depends on ignoring your senses (e.g., eating unhealthy food despite tasting it). How does Maturidi's argument apply to your willful blindness?
?
If Maturidi questioned you about your beliefs, would your answers reveal hidden contradictions? What would it take for you to submit to the truth this lesson teaches?

Why Speculative Reasoning is a Necessity in Islam
Maturidi enumerates reasons necessitating speculative reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific theological problems that require speculative reasoning (nazar) according to Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Speculative reasoning is the essential tool for navigating what lies beyond direct sense perception.

Imam Maturidi begins his argument for the necessity of speculative reasoning by listing concrete scenarios where unaided senses or mere tradition fall short: discerning good from evil in subtle matters, verifying reports that admit confusion, and distinguishing prophetic miracles from sorcery. This passage establishes that reason is not an innovation but an indispensable means to certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Carefully read the Arabic text and its English translation. Notice how Maturidi structures his argument with 'one of them is… then…'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify each reason for nazar
List the three or four distinct reasons Maturidi gives for why speculative reasoning is necessary. Pay attention to the phrases 'في علم الحسن والخير', 'فيما يرد من الخير', and 'آيات الرسل وتمويهات السحرة'.
3
Reflect on the necessity
Ask yourself: In each case, why would sense perception or blind acceptance be insufficient? How does nazar resolve the problem?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل في لزوم القول بعلم النظر وجوه: أحدهما الاضطرار إليه في علم الحسن والخير، وذلك فيما يبعد من الحواس أو يلطف، وفيما يرد من الخير أنه في نوع ما يحتمل الخلط أو لا، ثم آيات الرسل وتمويهات السحرة وغيرهم في التمييز بينها، وفي تعرف الآيات بما يتأمل فيها (من) قوى البشر وأحوال الآتى بها
Then the basis for the necessity of speaking through speculative knowledge are several aspects: one of them is the necessity of it in the knowledge of good and evil, and that concerns what is distant from the senses or subtle, and regarding what comes from report about whether it is of a type that admits of confusion or not. Then the miracles of the messengers and the deceptions of sorcerers and others in distinguishing between them, and in recognizing the miracles through what is reflected upon concerning the powers of humans and the states of the one who brings them.
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage begins a discussion on the necessity of speculative reasoning (nazar). Maturidi lists several reasons why nazar is indispensable. First, it is needed to know good and evil (al-hasan wa al-khair) when matters are beyond the senses or subtle. Second, it is needed to determine whether a report belongs to a category that admits of confusion. Third, it is essential for distinguishing between the miracles of prophets and the deceptions of sorcerers, and for understanding the nature of miracles by reflecting on human powers and the condition of the miracle-worker. This sets the foundation for the role of rational inquiry in theology.

Key Terms
النظر
الحسن والخير
الآيات
السحرة
التمويهات

C) To establish the authority of consensus.
D) To interpret the Quran literally.

Key Takeaway
Speculative reasoning is indispensable for resolving uncertainties in matters of good and evil, verifying reports, and distinguishing divine miracles from sorcery.

Mutawatir and Ahad Reports
Maturidi defines mutawatir and ahad reports and their epistemic status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between mutawatir and ahad reports and their respective epistemic statuses as defined by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Mutawatir yields certainty; ahad reports require ijtihad.

Imam Maturidi begins by establishing the highest category of transmitted reports: mutawatir, which through its multiplicity of transmitters yields certain knowledge. He then contrasts this with reports that do not reach that level (ahad), which necessitate careful reasoning (ijtihad) to determine acceptance. This passage lays out the foundational methodology for evaluating religious reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of Maturidi's argument.
Pay attention to how he transitions from mutawatir to ahad.
2
Identify the two categories
Notice how Maturidi first describes mutawatir reports (those that necessitate knowledge and establish infallibility) and then ahad reports (those that do not reach that degree).
3
Understand the epistemic claims
For mutawatir, certainty and the group's infallibility is established. For ahad, the outcome is not certainty but probability, requiring ijtihad.
Contrast 'علم' (knowledge) with 'غالب الظن' (predominant probability).
4
Reflect on the practical outcome
Maturidi emphasizes that ahad reports are not to be rejected outright; rather, one must examine narrators and weigh probabilities.
He warns against both complete rejection and uncritical acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذبا قط فهو الذي من انتهى إليه مثله لزمه حق شهود القول ممن اتضح البرهان عصمته ، وذلك وصف خير المتواتر ، إن كلا منهم وإن لم يقم دليل على عصمته فإن الخبر منهم إذا بلغ ذلك الحد ظهر صدقه ، وثبتت عصمة مثله على الكتب، وإن أمكن خلاف ذلك في كل على الإشارة . وهكذا القول فما طريقه الاجتهاد ، وإن احتمل خطأ كل على الإنفراد والغلط ، فإنهم لم يتفقوا بمن يوفقهم لذلك ليظهر حقه ؛ إذ الآراء لا تؤدى إليه بعد اختلاف الأهواء وتفرق لذات ذى الرأى دون لطف العزيز الحميد الذي يملك إظهار حقه وعصمة خلقه فما شاء ، ولا قوة إلا بالله الجيني و خبر آخر ا لا يبلغ هذا القدر في ايجاب العلم والشهادة بأنه الحق عن نبي الرحمة ، فيجب العمل به والترك بالاجتهاد والنظر في أحوال الرواة ، والظاهر مما ظهر حقه ، وجوازه فى السمع الذى قد أحيط . ثم يعمل بما يغلب عليه الوجه ٣ وإن احتمل الغلط ، إذ ربما يعمل به في علم الحس الذي هو أرفع طرق يضعف الحواس ويبعد المحسوس ولطفه . على أن ترك العمل . جميعا، لا يرجع فيه إلى الإحاطة ، وإلى أيهما مال كان في ذلك إعراض عن الخبر ، فلذلك لزم القول فيه بالاجتهاد بالوجهين ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Never a lie, rather one whose like has reached [the level of] his [the Prophet's] station, the right of witnessing the statement from one whose proof has become clear necessitates his infallibility. And that is the description of the best of mutawatir. For each of them, even if no proof is established for his infallibility, when the report from them reaches that limit, its truth appears, and the infallibility of one like him is established regarding the books, even if the opposite is possible in every [single] indication. And such is the statement regarding that whose way is ijtihad, even if error is possible from each individually, for they did not agree upon one who would bring them together for that [purpose] so that his truth would appear; since opinions do not lead to it after the divergence of desires and the dispersion of the pleasures of the one of opinion without the grace of the Mighty, the Praiseworthy, who possesses the power to manifest His truth and the infallibility of His creation as He wills, and there is no power except with Allah. And another report that does not reach this degree in necessitating knowledge and testimony that it is the truth from the Prophet of mercy, so acting upon it and leaving it are obligatory through ijtihad and examination of the conditions of the narrators, and the apparent from what has become clear of its truth, and its permissibility in the hearing that has been encompassed. Then one acts upon what is predominant in the aspect [of probability], even if error is possible, for sometimes one acts upon it in the knowledge of sense perception, which is the highest of ways, weakening the senses and distancing the sensed and its subtlety. However, abandoning acting upon it altogether does not refer to encompassing, and to whichever of the two one inclines, there is in that an aversion to the report, therefore it was necessary to state about it through ijtihad in the two aspects, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage defines the epistemic categories of transmitted reports (khabar). It begins by describing the highest level: mutawatir reports, which are transmitted by such a large number that their truth is certain, even if each individual reporter's infallibility is not proven. The author argues that when a report reaches this level, its truth becomes manifest, and the infallibility of the group is established. He then contrasts this with reports that do not reach this degree—ahad reports—which do not yield certainty but require ijtihad (independent reasoning) to determine whether to accept or act upon them. The process involves scrutinizing the narrators' conditions, comparing with what is clearly established, and using probability (ghalabat al-zann) to decide. He warns against complete rejection or unquestioning acceptance, as both extremes entail neglect of the report. Thus, ijtihad is necessary in such cases.

Key Terms
متواتر
خبر
اجتهاد
عصمة
غالب الظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think ahad reports are useless or must be rejected.
Correction: Maturidi shows that ahad reports require ijtihad, not rejection. One must weigh probability and act accordingly.

Checkpoint
What is the epistemic status of a mutawatir report according to Maturidi?
A) It is probable but not certain
B) It yields certainty and establishes infallibility of the transmitters
C) It is rejected unless corroborated
D) It requires ijtihad to determine its truth

Key Takeaway
Mutawatir reports yield certainty, while ahad reports require ijtihad to determine their reliability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive news or information that is uncertain, do you quickly accept or reject it, or do you pause to examine it with ijtihad? How does this lesson challenge your default response?
?
Have you ever dismissed a religious teaching because it seemed less than certain? What would ijtihad look like in that situation, and are you willing to engage in it?
?
In your daily life, do you lean more towards seeking certainty or acting on probability? What does your approach reveal about your trust in Allah’s guidance through the methods He has prescribed?

First Proof: Divine Command to Reflect
Maturidi cites Quranic commands to reflect as proof for nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quranic commands to reflect on creation as the foundational proof for the necessity of speculative reasoning (nazar).

Core Idea
God commands reflection, making nazar an obligatory duty.

Maturidi opens his theological argument by grounding the necessity of intellectual investigation in divine scripture itself. He does not begin with abstract logic but with the Quran's own repeated command to observe, ponder, and derive lessons from creation. This passage establishes that nazar is not merely permissible but divinely mandated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
2
Identify the cited verses
The passage lists several Quranic verses that command reflection. List them and note their common theme.
There are four main references.
3
Understand the argument
Maturidi argues that because God commands reflection, it becomes obligatory. Understand the logical link: divine command implies duty.
This is a scriptural proof, not rational.
4
Reflect on the implication
If God commands reflection, then avoiding intellectual investigation is disobeying a divine order. Consider why this is a powerful starting point for kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لتظهر الحق بنوره والباطل بظلمته . وعلى ذلك دل الله بالذي ثبت بالأدلة مع المعجزة أنه منه ، من نحو القرآن الذي عجز الإنس والجن أن يأتوا بمثله الأمر به بقوله : سَرِيهِمْ آيَاتِنَا من الآفاق إلى آخر السورة ، وقوله : أَفَلَا يَنظُرُونَ إِلَى الإِبل ، وقوله : إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالأَرْضِ ، وقوله : وَفِي أَنفُسِكُمْ أَفَلَا تُبْصِرُونَ ، وغير ذلك مما رغب فى النظر وألزم الإعتبار وأمر بالتفكر والتدبر ، وأخبر أن ذلك يوقفهم على الحق ويبين لهم الطريق ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
So that truth appears with its light and falsehood with its darkness. And upon that God guided by what is established through proofs along with the miracle that it is from Him, such as the Quran which humans and jinn are incapable of bringing its like, commanding with His saying: 'We will show them Our signs in the horizons' to the end of the surah, and His saying: 'Do they not look at the camels', and His saying: 'Indeed, in the creation of the heavens and the earth', and His saying: 'And in yourselves, do you not see?', and other than that which encourages reflection and necessitates consideration and commands pondering and deliberation, and informed that that will make them realize the truth and show them the way, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the necessity of speculative reasoning (nazar): the divine command itself. Maturidi argues that God has guided people through clear proofs, especially miracles like the Quran, which no one can imitate. He then cites several Quranic verses that explicitly command reflection on the universe (e.g., horizons, camels, creation of heavens and earth, and the self). These verses show that God encourages nazar, necessitates consideration (i'tibar), and commands pondering (tafakkur) and deliberation (tadabbur). The conclusion is that such reflection leads to recognizing truth and the right path, thereby establishing that nazar is essential.

الآيات

التدبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think nazar (speculative reasoning) is unnecessary if one already has faith or has received revelation.
Correction: Maturidi demonstrates that God Himself commands reflection on creation, making nazar a divinely mandated duty, not an optional extra.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Quran itself commands us to reflect on creation, making speculative reasoning (nazar) a divinely obligated act.

Only Reason Can Refute Reason
Maturidi argues that speculative reasoning cannot be refuted except by itself and is necessary for knowledge of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that speculative reasoning (nazar) is indispensable and cannot be refuted without using it.

Core Idea
Speculative reasoning is self-validating: any attempt to reject it uses it.

Maturidi presents a powerful argument that demonstrates the necessity of rational inquiry. He shows that even those who reject reason must rely on reason to make their case. This passage lays the foundation for why knowledge of God and creation can only be attained through speculative reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow of the argument.
2
Identify the self-refutation argument
Maturidi claims that there is no proof against nazar except nazar itself. Understand why this shows nazar is necessary.
The skeptic cannot argue without using reason.
3
Consider the implications for knowledge of creation
The passage then explains that knowledge about creation's wisdom, origin, and attributes is impossible without nazar. Reflect on why this matters for theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما ليس لمن يتكر النظر على دفعه دليل سوى النظر ، فدل ذلك على لزوم النظر ما به دفعه ، ما لا بد من معرفة ما فى الخلق من الحكمة إذ لا يجوز فعل مثله عبثا وما فيه من الدلالة على من أنشأه أو على كونه بنفسه أو حدث أو قدم ، وكل ذلك مما لا سبيل إلى العلم به إلا بالنظر .
There is no proof for the one who repeatedly rejects speculative reasoning except speculative reasoning itself, so that indicates the necessity of speculative reasoning by which he rejects it. It is indispensable to know the wisdom in creation, for it is not permissible to do something like it in vain, and what is in it of indication of who originated it, or of its being by itself, or its being originated or eternal, and all that is something that there is no way to know except through speculative reasoning.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage presents a powerful self-refutation argument. Maturidi states that anyone who attempts to reject or deny speculative reasoning (nazar) has no tool to do so except nazar itself. This proves that nazar is necessary even to argue against it. He then explains the content that nazar must address: the wisdom in creation, which cannot be purposeless, and the need to know whether the universe points to a creator, is self-caused, originated, or eternal. All such knowledge is inaccessible without nazar.

Key Terms
النظر
الدليل
الحكمة
الخَلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can reject the validity of rational inquiry entirely.
Correction: Maturidi shows that any rejection of reason itself uses reason, so reason cannot be escaped.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

The Necessity of Reason for Human Stewardship
Maturidi argues that human stewardship necessitates reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that human stewardship of creation necessitates the use of speculative reason to discern good from evil.

Core Idea
Reason is essential for fulfilling our role as stewards of creation.

Maturidi presents a third proof for the necessity of reason, grounded in the human condition. He observes that humans are uniquely entrusted with managing creation and are tested through it. This role demands that we seek what is most beneficial and avoid harm, a task impossible without employing our intellects through rational inquiry. Let us examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the argument's flow come through before breaking it down.
Notice how Maturidi uses a single compound sentence to state his case.
2
Identify the premise about human stewardship
Maturidi states that humans are specially endowed with managing creatures and being tested in that. He also mentions seeking the most beneficial (al-aslah) and choosing good. This is the foundation of the argument.
What does it mean to be entrusted with management? Consider the concept of khilafah.
3
Find the conclusion about reason
The second part of the passage says there is no way to achieve that knowledge except by using intellects through speculative reasoning (nazar). This is the logical outcome: reason is necessary.
Speculative reasoning is not optional; it is the only route.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if reason is the only means to fulfill stewardship, what does that say about the role of intellect in Islam? How does this contrast with views that downplay reason?
This connects to broader debates in kalam about rationality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن البشر خص يملك تدبير الخلائق والمحنة فيها وطلب الأصلح لهم في العقول واختيار المحاسن في ذلك واتقاء مضادة ذلك ، ولا سبيل إلى معرفة ذلك إلا باستعمال العقول بالنظر في الأشياء .
Moreover, humans are specially endowed with the management of creatures and the trial therein, and seeking the most beneficial for them in intellects, and choosing the good in that, and avoiding the opposite of that, and there is no way to know that except by employing intellects through speculative reasoning in things.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage presents a teleological argument. Maturidi states that humans are uniquely given the ability to manage creation and are tested in it. They must seek what is most beneficial (al-aslah) using their intellects, choose good actions, and avoid their opposites. Such discernment is impossible without using intellects, which means engaging in speculative reasoning (nazar) about things.

Key Terms
البشر
التدبير
المحنة
الأصلح
العقول
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason is optional for moral knowledge; revelation alone suffices.
Correction: Maturidi shows reason is essential because without it, humans cannot discern good from evil to fulfill their stewardship. Reason is the means to understand revelation as well.

Checkpoint

A) To prove the existence of God
B) Because they are entrusted with managing creation and discerning good from evil

Key Takeaway
Human stewardship of creation necessitates the use of speculative reason to discern good from evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Fourth Proof: Universal Recourse to Reason in Adversity
Maturidi argues for the validity of nazar through universal recourse in adversity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's pragmatic argument for the reliability of speculative reasoning (nazar) by observing universal human behavior in times of doubt and calamity.

Core Idea
In times of trouble, everyone turns to reason — that shows it leads to truth.

Maturidi presents a compelling, experience-based argument. He notes that when people face calamities or confusing doubts, they naturally resort to reasoning and reflection. This universal behavior suggests that reasoning is a reliable guide to reality, just as we rely on our senses when uncertain about colors or sounds. In this lesson, you will explore this analogy and its implications for the validity of reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Notice the key terms like النظر (speculative reasoning) and التأمل (deliberation).
2
Identify the core observation and analogy
Maturidi observes that in calamities and doubts, people turn to nazar. Then he compares it to using senses when uncertain about a color or sound. Identify these two parts.
3
Reflect on the implication
If everyone naturally uses reason to reach truths, that suggests reason is a valid tool, just like senses are for physical realities. Ask yourself: does this argument rely on consensus?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن مفزع الكل عنده النوائب واعتراض الشبه إلى النظر في ذلك والتأمل، فدل أنه يدل على الحقائق ويوصل به إليها، على نحو الفرع عند اشتباه اللون إلى البصر ، والصوت إلى السمع ، وكذا كل شي. إلى المحاسة التى بها دركها ، فمثله النظر ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Moreover, the recourse of everyone in times of calamities and the occurrence of doubts is to speculative reasoning in that and deliberation, so it indicates that it leads to realities and connects to them, like the branch when color is uncertain [turns] to sight, and sound to hearing, and likewise everything to the sense by which it is perceived, so similar is speculative reasoning, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage provides a pragmatic argument. Maturidi observes that when people face calamities or doubts, they naturally resort to speculative reasoning (nazar) and deliberation. This universal recourse shows that nazar leads to realities and truths. He draws an analogy: just as one uncertain about a color or sound refers to the relevant sense (sight or hearing), one uncertain about abstract matters refers to reason. Thus, nazar functions like a sense for grasping truths.

Key Terms
النظر
التأمل
الحقائق
الحواس
القياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think reason is purely subjective and unreliable.
Correction: Maturidi shows it is a reliable tool, analogous to senses, because everyone naturally resorts to it in times of doubt.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Maturidi use to illustrate the role of speculative reasoning?
A) A lamp in darkness
B) A sense perception like sight or hearing when uncertain
C) A judge in court
D) A scale for weighing

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the universal human turn to reason in adversity proves its validity as a means to grasp realities, like using senses for physical uncertainties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When calamity strikes or doubts assail you, is your first instinct to reason carefully or to react emotionally? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on the intellect Allah gave you? Commit to turning to nazar as your first response.
?
If universal recourse to reason is evidence of its truth, why do you sometimes avoid deep thought about your beliefs? What fear or laziness holds you back? This lesson demands you ask: are you using your intellect as Allah intended?
?
The analogy compares nazar to sight. If you would not close your eyes when uncertain about a color, why do you shut down your reason when faced with theological confusion? What step will you take this week to deliberately exercise your nazar in a matter of creed?

Moral Knowledge Requires Reasoning
Maturidi argues that moral knowledge depends on speculative reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that moral knowledge of good and evil cannot be fully attained through sense perception or reports alone, and requires deliberate reasoning.

Core Idea
Reason is essential to discern moral truth beyond senses and reports.

Maturidi presents a final proof that speculative reasoning is necessary for moral epistemology. He argues that although sense perception and transmitted reports provide information about actions, the full moral dimension—their beauty or ugliness—requires rational deliberation to be established in the mind and unveiled. This passage demonstrates the indispensable role of reason in moral knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Focus on the flow: Maturidi mentions senses, reports, then reason.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three sources of knowledge
Notice that Maturidi lists sense perception, transmitted reports, and then adds deliberation/reasoning. Understand the roles of each.
Senses and reports are initial but insufficient.
3
Reflect on the necessity of reason
Consider why Maturidi insists that establishing moral aspects in the mind requires ta'ammul and nazar. Relate this to the broader kalam project.
This proof counters those who deny reason's role in morality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن محاسن الأشياء ومساويها ، وما قبح من الأفعال وما حسن منها فإنما نهاية العلم بعد وقوع الحواس عليها وورود الأخبار فيها إذا أريد تقرير كل جهة من ذلك في العقول والكشف عن وجوه ما لا سبيل إلى ذلك إلا بالتأمل والنظر فيها .
Moreover, the beauties of things and their defects, and what is ugly of actions and what is good among them—the limit of knowledge is after sense perception falls upon them and reports arrive concerning them, when one intends to establish each aspect of that in minds and unveil its faces; there is no way to that except through deliberation and speculative reasoning in them.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage presents a final proof: knowledge of the moral qualities of actions (beauty and ugliness, good and evil) cannot be fully attained through sense perception or transmitted reports alone. Even after sensory experience and reports, one needs to establish these aspects in the mind and unveil their dimensions. This requires deliberation (ta'ammul) and speculative reasoning (nazar). Maturidi implies that reason is indispensable for moral epistemology.

Key Terms
محاسن
مساوي
قبح
حسن
التأمل
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Morality is purely based on revelation or social convention; reason is unnecessary.
Correction: Maturidi shows that while senses and reports provide information, reason is required to fully establish moral knowledge in the mind.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is required to fully know the good and evil of actions?
A) Only sense perception
B) Only transmitted reports
C) Sense perception and reports plus deliberation and reasoning
D) Divine revelation alone

Key Takeaway
Moral knowledge requires active reasoning beyond sensory and transmitted information.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral dilemma, do you rely solely on what you've been told or what you sense? This lesson says that is not enough. What moral truth have you been avoiding by not using your reason?
?
Think of a time you accepted a moral judgment without thinking it through. What would it take for you to truly deliberate on it as Maturidi demands?
?
You claim to know right from wrong. But is your knowledge achieved through deliberate reasoning, or just inherited from culture? If the latter, are you truly responsible for your moral choices?

The Quranic Mandate for Speculative Reflection
Maturidi cites Quranic verses commanding nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran explicitly commands reflection (nazar) as a means to attain truth and knowledge of God.

Core Idea
The Quran repeatedly commands reflection, establishing it as a divine obligation.

Imam Maturidi opens his argument by grounding the necessity of nazar directly in the Quran. He cites several verses that command reflection on God's signs in the cosmos and within ourselves, showing that reflection is not an optional intellectual exercise but a divinely mandated path to truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses cited
Read the Arabic passage and identify the four Quranic verses that Maturidi quotes. Notice the variety: signs in the horizons, the camel, creation of heavens and earth, and yourselves.
These verses cover external and internal signs.
2
Understand the verbs used
Maturidi uses verbs like 'encourage', 'necessitate', 'command' (رغب, ألزم, أمر) to show the strength of the Quranic call to reflection.
These verbs indicate obligation.
3
Reflect on the purpose
The passage says reflection makes one 'stand upon the truth and shows the way' (يوقفهم على الحق ويبين لهم الطريق). Consider how reflection leads to truth.
Reflection is a means, not an end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لتظهر الحق بنوره والباطل بظلمته . وعلى ذلك دل الله بالذي ثبت بالأدلة. مع المعجزة أنه منه ، من نحو القرآن الذي عجز الإنس والجن أن يأتوا بمثله الأمر به بقوله : سَنُرِيهِمْ آيَاتِنَا من الآفاق إلى آخر السورة ، وقوله : أَفَلَا يَنظُرُونَ إِلَى الإِبل ، وقوله : إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالأَرْضِ ، وقوله : وَفِي أَنفُسِكُمْ أَفَلَا تُبْصِرُونَ ، وغير ذلك مما رغب فى النظر وألزم الإعتبار وأمر بالتفكر والتدبر ، وأخبر أن ذلك يوقفهم على الحق ويبين لهم الطريق ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ما ليس لمن يتكر النظر على دفعه دليل سوى النظر ، فدل ذلك على لزوم النظر ما به دفعه ، ما لا بد من معرفة ما فى الخلق من الحكمة إذ لا يجوز فعل مثله عبثا وما فيه من الدلالة على من أنشأه أو على كونه بنفسه أو حدث أو قدم ، وكل ذلك مما لا سبيل إلى العلم به إلا بالنظر . على أن البشر خص يملك تدبير الخلائق والمحنة فيها وطلب الأصلح لهم في العقول واختيار المحاسن في ذلك واتقاء مضادة ذلك ، ولا سبيل إلى معرفة ذلك إلا باستعمال العقول بالنظر في الأشياء . على أن مفزع الكل عنده النوائب واعتراض الشبه إلى النظر في ذلك والتأمل، فدل أنه يدل على الحقائق ويوصل به إليها، على نحو الفرع عند اشتباه اللون إلى البصر ، والصوت إلى السمع ، وكذا كل شيء إلى الحاسة التي بها دركها ، فمثله النظر ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أن محاسن الأشياء ومساويها ، وما قبح من الأفعال وما حسن منها فإنما نهاية العلم بعد وقوع الحواس عليها وورود الأخبار فيها إذا أريد تقرير كل جهة من ذلك في العقول والكشف عن وجوه ما لا سبيل إلى ذلك إلا بالتأمل والنظر فيها . وعلى ذلك أمر المكاسب الضارة والنافعة . على أن البشر جبل على طبيعة وعقل ، وما يحسنه العقل غير الذي ترغب فيه الطبيعة ، وما يقبحه غير الذي ينفر منه الطبع ، أو يكون بينها مخالفة مرة ، وموافقة ثانيا ، لا بد من النظر في كل أثر والتأمل ليعلم حقيقة أنه في أي فن ونوع مما ذكرنا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
To make the truth appear in its light and falsehood in its darkness. And upon that God indicated by that which is established through proofs, along with the miracle that it is from Him, such as the Qur'an which humans and jinn are unable to produce the like of it, commanding by His saying: 'We will show them Our signs in the horizons' up to the end of the sura, and His saying: 'Do they not look at the camels?', and His saying: 'Indeed in the creation of the heavens and the earth', and His saying: 'And in yourselves, do you not see?', and other things that encourage reflection (nazar) and necessitate consideration (i'tibar) and command pondering (tafakkur) and deliberation (tadabbur), and informed that that would make them stand upon the truth and show them the way, and there is no power except with God. For one who repeats reflection (nazar) has no proof to refute it except reflection itself, so that indicates the necessity of reflection by which it is refuted. It is necessary to know the wisdom in creation for it is not permissible that a like of it be done in vain, and what is in it of indication to the one who originated it or to its being by itself, or its being originated or eternal, and all of that cannot be known except through reflection. And that human beings are specified with the ability to manage creatures and the trial in them and to seek what is most beneficial for them in intellects and to choose the good in that and avoid its opposite, and there is no way to know that except by using intellects through reflection on things. And that the recourse of everyone in the face of calamities and the obstruction of doubts is to reflection on that and contemplation, so it indicates that it leads to realities and that one reaches them through it, like the branch when color is ambiguous [turning] to sight, and sound to hearing, and likewise everything to the sense by which it is perceived, so likewise is reflection, and there is no power except with God. And that the good qualities and bad qualities of things, and what is ugly of actions and what is beautiful, the ultimate knowledge after the occurrence of the senses upon them and the arrival of reports about them, if one intends to establish each aspect of that in intellects and uncover the aspects that cannot be reached except through contemplation and reflection in them. And upon that is the matter of harmful and beneficial acquisitions. And that human beings are created with a nature ('nature') and intellect, and what the intellect deems good is not what nature desires, and what it deems ugly is not what [nature] shuns, or there is sometimes a difference between them and sometimes agreement; there is no alternative to reflection on every trace and contemplation to know truly in which category and type it is among what we have mentioned, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage is a multi-faceted argument for the necessity of speculative reflection (nazar) in attaining knowledge, particularly of divine matters. It begins by citing Quranic verses that command reflection: showing signs in the horizons, looking at camels, creation, and oneself. These verses demonstrate that God made reflection a means to truth. Then it argues that the only way to refute doubts about the validity of reflection is through reflection itself, implying its foundational role. Next, it states that knowledge of the wisdom in creation—whether it points to a Creator, self-existence, origination, or eternity—requires reflection. Human beings are also entrusted with managing worldly affairs and discerning beneficial from harmful actions, which depends on reflection. The passage analogizes reflection to the senses: just as one turns to sight when colors are unclear, one turns to reflection when truths are obscure. Finally, it explains that distinguishing good from bad in actions and managing acquisitions requires contemplation of traces, especially since human nature and intellect may conflict. The overall argument establishes reflection as the indispensable tool for theological and practical knowledge.

Key Terms
النظر (nazar)
الاعتبار (i'tibar)
التفكر (tafakkur)
التدبر (tadabbur)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reflection is a human invention or optional for knowledge.
Correction: The passage shows that reflection is explicitly commanded by God in the Quran, making it a religious obligation.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do the Quranic verses about reflection ultimately lead to?
A) Worldly success
B) Standing upon truth and showing the way
C) Increased wealth
D) Social status

Key Takeaway
The Quran commands reflection on God's signs as a means to attain truth and guidance, establishing nazar as a divine duty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that Allah commands you to reflect on His signs in the horizons and in yourself. When was the last time you actually did that—not just recited a verse, but paused and let creation speak to you? If you have been neglecting this divine command, what concrete change will you make today to begin obeying it?
?
The verse 'Do they not look at the camels?' (Q88:17) is addressed to all humans, including you. Have you ever pondered the creation of a camel or any animal enough to increase your certainty in Allah's power and wisdom? Or have you passed over these signs without a second thought? Repent for your heedlessness and commit to spending 5 minutes this evening reflecting on one of His signs.
?
Maturidi lists nazar, i'tibar, tafakkur, and tadabbur as commanded acts. Which of these do you actually practice? If you are honest, most are absent from your daily life. How can you claim to seek guidance if you ignore the very means Allah has prescribed? Decide now which of these you will implement tomorrow, and ask Allah for help.

The Inescapable Necessity of Reflection
Maturidi argues nazar is self-validating and necessary for knowledge of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that nazar is self-validating and necessary for knowing the wisdom in creation.

Core Idea
Only nazar can refute doubts about nazar, and it is the sole means to know the wisdom behind creation.

After establishing the Quranic mandate, Maturidi provides a rational argument for the necessity of nazar. He shows that any attempt to deny nazar already relies on nazar, making it unavoidable. He then argues that knowledge of the wisdom in creation—whether it points to a Creator, self-existence, origination, or eternity—is only possible through reflection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the self-refutation argument
Read the first part of the passage: 'whoever repeats reflection has no proof to refute it except reflection itself'. Consider why this makes nazar necessary.
This is a foundational argument in kalam.
2
Identify the objects of knowledge
The passage lists things that can only be known through nazar: wisdom in creation, its indication to the originator, whether it is by itself, created, or eternal.
These are key theological questions.
3
Reflect on the impossibility of knowing otherwise
Note that Maturidi says 'all of that cannot be known except through reflection'. Why can't sense perception or revelation alone suffice?
Revelation also requires reflection to understand.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Reflection in Human Stewardship
Maturidi argues nazar is necessary for human stewardship and ethics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that nazar is essential for humans to fulfill their role as stewards and to discern what is beneficial or harmful.

Core Idea
Humans need reflection to manage creation, choose what is good, and avoid evil.

Maturidi now turns to a practical argument: human beings are entrusted with the care of creation and must use their intellect to seek what is most beneficial. This requires careful reflection on things to know what is good and bad, beneficial and harmful. Without nazar, humans cannot fulfill this responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the human role
Read the first part: 'human beings are specified with the ability to manage creatures and the trial in them'. What does this imply about human responsibility?
This is a key Islamic concept of khilafah.
2
Recognize the need for reflection
The passage states that there is no way to manage and seek benefit except by using intellects through reflection on things.
3
Connect to daily life
Think of a decision you make daily that involves reflection to choose the better option. This shows the universality of nazar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لتظهر الحق بنوره والباطل بظلمته . وعلى ذلك دل الله بالذي ثبت بالأدلة. مع المعجزة أنه منه ، من نحو القرآن الذي عجز الإنس والجن أن يأتوا بمثله الأمر به بقوله : سَنُرِيهِمْ آيَاتِنَا من الآفاق إلى آخر السورة ، وقوله : أَفَلَا يَنظُرُونَ إِلَى الإِبل ، وقوله : إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالأَرْضِ ، وقوله : وَفِي أَنفُسِكُمْ أَفَلَا تُبْصِرُونَ ، وغير ذلك مما رغب فى النظر وألزم الإعتبار وأمر بالتفكر والتدبر ، وأخبر أن ذلك يوقفهم على الحق ويبين لهم الطريق ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ما ليس لمن يتكر النظر على دفعه دليل سوى النظر ، فدل ذلك على لزوم النظر ما به دفعه ، ما لا بد من معرفة ما فى الخلق من الحكمة إذ لا يجوز فعل مثله عبثا وما فيه من الدلالة على من أنشأه أو على كونه بنفسه أو حدث أو قدم ، وكل ذلك مما لا سبيل إلى العلم به إلا بالنظر . على أن البشر خص يملك تدبير الخلائق والمحنة فيها وطلب الأصلح لهم في العقول واختيار المحاسن في ذلك واتقاء مضادة ذلك ، ولا سبيل إلى معرفة ذلك إلا باستعمال العقول بالنظر في الأشياء . على أن مفزع الكل عنده النوائب واعتراض الشبه إلى النظر في ذلك والتأمل، فدل أنه يدل على الحقائق ويوصل به إليها، على نحو الفرع عند اشتباه اللون إلى البصر ، والصوت إلى السمع ، وكذا كل شيء إلى الحاسة التي بها دركها ، فمثله النظر ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أن محاسن الأشياء ومساويها ، وما قبح من الأفعال وما حسن منها فإنما نهاية العلم بعد وقوع الحواس عليها وورود الأخبار فيها إذا أريد تقرير كل جهة من ذلك في العقول والكشف عن وجوه ما لا سبيل إلى ذلك إلا بالتأمل والنظر فيها . وعلى ذلك أمر المكاسب الضارة والنافعة . على أن البشر جبل على طبيعة وعقل ، وما يحسنه العقل غير الذي ترغب فيه الطبيعة ، وما يقبحه غير الذي ينفر منه الطبع ، أو يكون بينها مخالفة مرة ، وموافقة ثانيا ، لا بد من النظر في كل أثر والتأمل ليعلم حقيقة أنه في أي فن ونوع مما ذكرنا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
To make the truth appear in its light and falsehood in its darkness. And upon that God indicated by that which is established through proofs, along with the miracle that it is from Him, such as the Qur'an which humans and jinn are unable to produce the like of it, commanding by His saying: 'We will show them Our signs in the horizons' up to the end of the sura, and His saying: 'Do they not look at the camels?', and His saying: 'Indeed in the creation of the heavens and the earth', and His saying: 'And in yourselves, do you not see?', and other things that encourage reflection (nazar) and necessitate consideration (i'tibar) and command pondering (tafakkur) and deliberation (tadabbur), and informed that that would make them stand upon the truth and show them the way, and there is no power except with God. For one who repeats reflection (nazar) has no proof to refute it except reflection itself, so that indicates the necessity of reflection by which it is refuted. It is necessary to know the wisdom in creation for it is not permissible that a like of it be done in vain, and what is in it of indication to the one who originated it or to its being by itself, or its being originated or eternal, and all of that cannot be known except through reflection. And that human beings are specified with the ability to manage creatures and the trial in them and to seek what is most beneficial for them in intellects and to choose the good in that and avoid its opposite, and there is no way to know that except by using intellects through reflection on things. And that the recourse of everyone in the face of calamities and the obstruction of doubts is to reflection on that and contemplation, so it indicates that it leads to realities and that one reaches them through it, like the branch when color is ambiguous [turning] to sight, and sound to hearing, and likewise everything to the sense by which it is perceived, so likewise is reflection, and there is no power except with God. And that the good qualities and bad qualities of things, and what is ugly of actions and what is beautiful, the ultimate knowledge after the occurrence of the senses upon them and the arrival of reports about them, if one intends to establish each aspect of that in intellects and uncover the aspects that cannot be reached except through contemplation and reflection in them. And upon that is the matter of harmful and beneficial acquisitions. And that human beings are created with a nature ('nature') and intellect, and what the intellect deems good is not what nature desires, and what it deems ugly is not what [nature] shuns, or there is sometimes a difference between them and sometimes agreement; there is no alternative to reflection on every trace and contemplation to know truly in which category and type it is among what we have mentioned, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage is a multi-faceted argument for the necessity of speculative reflection (nazar) in attaining knowledge, particularly of divine matters. It begins by citing Quranic verses that command reflection: showing signs in the horizons, looking at camels, creation, and oneself. These verses demonstrate that God made reflection a means to truth. Then it argues that the only way to refute doubts about the validity of reflection is through reflection itself, implying its foundational role. Next, it states that knowledge of the wisdom in creation—whether it points to a Creator, self-existence, origination, or eternity—requires reflection. Human beings are also entrusted with managing worldly affairs and discerning beneficial from harmful actions, which depends on reflection. The passage analogizes reflection to the senses: just as one turns to sight when colors are unclear, one turns to reflection when truths are obscure. Finally, it explains that distinguishing good from bad in actions and managing acquisitions requires contemplation of traces, especially since human nature and intellect may conflict. The overall argument establishes reflection as the indispensable tool for theological and practical knowledge.

Key Terms
النظر (nazar)
العقل ('aql)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the role of reflection in human affairs?
A) It is optional for those with strong intellect
B) It is the only way to know how to manage and choose what is beneficial
C) It is mainly for scholars
D) It is used only in religious matters

Key Takeaway
Nazar is necessary for humans to fulfill their role as stewards and to make sound ethical decisions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you actively using reflection to know what is truly good and avoid evil in your daily decisions, or are you relying on habits and desires?
?
If nazar is necessary for fulfilling your role as a steward of Allah's creation, how much time do you actually spend in deep reflection about your responsibilities?
?
What is one area of your life where you have been neglecting nazar and just following impulses? How will you change that starting today?

Method of Learning

5 min
The Analogy of Reflection to the Senses
Maturidi uses an analogy comparing nazar to sense perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that nazar is for the intellect what the senses are for physical perception—an indispensable tool for reaching realities.

Core Idea
Nazar guides the intellect to truth, just as the senses guide perception of the physical world.

Maturidi strengthens his argument with a powerful analogy: in times of doubt or confusion, people naturally turn to reflection just as they turn to sight when colors are unclear, or hearing when sounds are ambiguous. This shows that nazar is the intellect's go-to tool for clarifying realities. The analogy is not merely illustrative but demonstrates the foundational role of reflection in human cognition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the situation of ambiguity
Read the passage: 'recourse of everyone in the face of calamities and the obstruction of doubts is to reflection'. Think of a time you faced a doubt; what did you do?
2
Analyze the analogy
Maturidi compares nazar to sight when color is ambiguous, hearing when sound is unclear. How does this show the naturalness of reflection?
3
Draw the conclusion
Since reflection is the recourse in difficulties, it must be a reliable guide to realities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reflection is only for complex matters and not a natural cognitive tool.
Correction: Maturidi shows that reflection is the natural recourse for all humans when faced with doubts or difficulties.

Checkpoint
According to the analogy, in times of doubt people naturally turn to what?
A) Intuition
B) Reflection (nazar)
C) Authority
D) Emotions

Key Takeaway
Nazar is the intellect's natural tool for resolving doubts, just as the senses are for physical perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a calamity or a doubt, is your first reaction to turn to reflection (nazar) and contemplation, or do you panic, complain, or avoid thinking? What one change can you make today to ensure your instinct is to think deeply before reacting?
?
If reflection is as natural and reliable as seeing with your eyes, why do you sometimes neglect to use your intellect when confronting doubts about your faith? Will you commit to spending 10 minutes daily in quiet reflection on a theological question?
?
The passage says everyone resorts to reflection in the face of calamities—have you ever truly trusted your own reasoning to resolve a confusion about Allah? If not, what is holding you back from using the gift of intellect that He gave you?

Method of Learning
intermediate
6 min
Reflection in Distinguishing Good from Evil
Maturidi argues nazar is necessary for moral discernment and resolving natural-intellectual conflicts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that nazar is necessary to discern the moral qualities of actions and to resolve conflicts between human nature and intellect.

Core Idea
Nazar is required to distinguish good from evil and to harmonize nature and intellect.

In the final arguments, Maturidi shows that reflection is needed to assess the good and bad qualities of actions, especially when reports and senses provide information. Moreover, human beings are composed of both nature (tabi'a) and intellect ('aql), which often disagree. Only through reflection can one determine which inclination to follow. This cements nazar as essential for moral and practical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of knowledge
Read the first part: 'the good qualities and bad qualities of things... the ultimate knowledge after the occurrence of the senses and the arrival of reports'. Why is reflection still needed?
Senses and reports give raw data, but reflection interprets them.
2
Understand the conflict between nature and intellect
The second part: human beings have both nature and intellect, which may disagree. Reflection is needed to evaluate which is correct.
3
Apply to everyday ethics
Think of a situation where your nature desires something but your intellect deems it wrong. How does reflection help decide?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That one can rely solely on instinct or revealed texts without reflection to know good and evil.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even after revelation, reflection is needed to apply and understand moral judgments.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is reflection needed even after sense perception and reports?
A) To confirm what the senses say
B) To establish the qualities in the intellect and uncover hidden aspects
C) To create new knowledge
D) To reject reports

Key Takeaway
Nazar is essential for moral discernment and for resolving the conflict between human nature and intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a decision you are currently facing where your natural desires pull one way and your intellect tells you another. Have you sincerely engaged in nazar (reflection) on this matter, or are you letting your nature decide? What would a true act of nazar look like for you right now?
?
You have received many reports (akhbar) about what is good and evil from your upbringing, culture, or religious teachers. According to this passage, these are not enough. When was the last time you personally reflected on a moral teaching rather than simply accepting it? What is one belief you hold that you have never examined through nazar?
?
The passage says nazar is the only way to uncover the true nature of actions. If you truly believed this, what would change about your daily routine? Specifically, how much time do you dedicate to intentional reflection on your actions versus acting on impulse or habit? Write one commitment to increase your practice of nazar this week.

Affirming Names Without Likening
Imam Maturidi explains the method of naming God without tashbih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's method of affirming divine names and attributes without falling into anthropomorphism, using the principle of shahid (witnessed) to gha'ib (absent).

Core Idea
We name God using witnessed terms but always negate similarity.

Imam Maturidi addresses a critical theological puzzle: how can we use human language for God without likening Him to creation? He proposes a method that affirms names and attributes while negating any likeness, grounding our knowledge in the witnessed world (shahid) to speak about the unseen (gha'ib). This passage sets a foundational rule for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the opening assertion and the flow of the argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key principle
Find the sentence that says 'there is no way to know the absent except by indication of the witnessed.' This is the core methodological principle.
3
Understand the template
The author gives the example 'Knower not like the knowers'. This pattern ('X not like the X's') applies to all names and attributes.
4
Reflect on the negation
Consider why the author insists on negating similarity. This guards against literal anthropomorphism.
5
Appreciate the conclusion
The final sentence attributes success to Allah, showing theological humility. The method is a human attempt guided by divine grace.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : وليس فى إثبات الأسماء وتحقيق الصفات تشابه لنفى حقائق ما في الخلق عنه كالمتية والثبات، ولكن الأسماء لما لم تحتمل التعريف ولا تحقيق الذات بحق الربوبية إلا بذلك ؟ إذ لا وجه لمعرفة غائب إلا بدلالة الشاهد. ثم إذا أريد الصفة بالعلو والجلال فذلك طريق المعرفة في الشاهد، وإمكان القول؛ إذ لا يحتمل وسعنا العرفان بالتسمية بغير الذي شاهدنا، ولا الإشارة إلى ما لا نأخذ من الحسن وحق العيان، لو احتمل وسعنا ذلك لقلنا ذلك، لكنا [أردنا) به ما يُسقط الشبه من قولنا : عالم لا كالعلماء، وهذا النوع في كل ما نسميه به ونصفه، والله الموفق
Shaykh Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: There is no likeness in affirming names and verifying attributes to negate the realities of what is in creation from Him, like death and stability. But names do not admit definition nor verification of the essence by the right of lordship except by that? For there is no way to know the absent except by indication of the witnessed. Then when one intends the attribute of highness and majesty, that is the way of knowledge in the witnessed, and the possibility of speech; for we cannot within our capacity have knowledge by naming with other than what we have witnessed, nor indication to what we do not take from the senses and the right of sight. If we could within our capacity do that, we would say that. But we intend by it what removes likeness from our saying: 'Knower not like the knowers', and this type applies to everything we name and describe Him with. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement by Imam Maturidi on how to affirm names and attributes for God without falling into tashbih (anthropomorphism). He begins by asserting that affirming names and attributes does not imply similarity to creation, because we are not affirming the realities found in creation (like death or stability) for God. Rather, names are used to define and verify God's essence, but only through the method of analogy from the witnessed world (shahid). Since we cannot know the absent (gha'ib) except through the witnessed (shahid), we use familiar terms like 'knower' but with the understanding that God is 'knower not like the knowers' ('alim la ka-l-'ulama'). This negation of similarity is applied to every name and attribute we use for God. The ultimate guidance is from Allah.

Key Terms
إثبات الأسماء
تحقيق الصفات
تشابه
الشاهد
الغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Using the same names for God and creation implies literal similarity between them.
Correction: Imam Maturidi clarifies that we use names by analogy from the witnessed world, but always negate any resemblance, as in 'Knower not like the knowers'.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Maturidi, how do we know the attributes of the unseen (gha'ib)?
A) Through direct revelation without analogy
B) Through the indication of the witnessed (shahid)
C) Through philosophical speculation alone
D) By negating all attributes

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi's method affirms divine names by analogy from the witnessed world while negating any resemblance, maintaining God's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Knowing', do you ever unconsciously imagine His knowledge as similar to your own? What specific practice can you adopt tonight to remind yourself that He is 'Knower not like the knowers'?
?
This passage says we cannot know the absent except through the witnessed. Are you content with the limitation of human language when speaking about Allah, or do you find yourself wishing for more direct knowledge? How does this humility before God's transcendence transform your awe of Him?
?
If you truly internalized that every name you use for Allah carries a negation of similarity, which attribute you recite most often (e.g., Ar-Rahman, Al-Aziz) would be most challenged? What change in your heart would that correction demand?

Evidence Is Necessary for Reports
Maturidi argues for the necessity of evidence in reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi insists that reports require evidence, and how all knowledge beyond direct observation relies on inference.

Core Idea
Without evidence, no report can be preferred over another — even everyday knowledge rests on inference.

Imam Maturidi begins by noting that many reports have proven true, but that does not mean we can accept any claim without evidence. In this passage, he establishes a foundational principle: to distinguish truth from falsehood in reports, we need a clear proof (dalil). He then extends this argument to show that even our basic recognition of things involves inferential reasoning, making evidence an unavoidable necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
The OCR has some uncertainties; focus on the key ideas.
2
Identify the central claim about evidence
Find the sentence where Maturidi states that one report is not preferable over another except by evidence. Notice how he frames this as a general principle.
3
Trace how Maturidi applies this to everyday knowledge
Look at the examples he gives: the treatment of a person, recognizing objects, and learning from a teacher. How does he show that these involve inference?
This connects to the common misunderstanding that inference is only for speculative matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فقد ظهر صدق كثير من الأخبار، فلم يكن أحد الوجهين به أولى من الآخر إلا بدليل يوضح، والله الموفق. وقد يعامل بالمعاملة الوحشة، وضرب مما يوذيه ويؤلمه حتى يضطر إلى القول بما لا يحتمل معرفته إلا بالخبر، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وعلى مثله قول المقرين بعلم العيان والخبر المنكرين لعلم الاستدلال، على عقله لوجوه المنافع في الدنيا ولعواقب مأمولة، ليس عنده علم من جهة العيان والخبر، وإنما ذلك بالاستدلال، وما في ذلك من ظنون الإصابة، وكذا معرفة صدق الإخبار وكذبه، مع ما يقال له في كل شيء يعلم مما ليس فيه علم الحس: علمت ذلك؟ فإن قال: بالخبر، يُسأل عن معرفة صدقه وكذبه، وتدخل مع ما كانت الضرورة تلزم ذلك في جميع الملاذ والمضار مما يتقى ويؤتى النظر بما عاين وسمع، ليعلم منشأ العالم أو حدثه وقدمه. وبعد، فإنه ليس في شيء يمنع الاستدلال له خبر في المنع، أو عيان فكأنه بالاستدلال يمنع القول به، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وبعد، فإن معرفة إنسان أو نار أو شيء بالذي شوهد مرة لا يخرج إلا على الاستدلال بالذي عرف، ولو لم يدله للزميه أن لا يعامل أحداً قط، ثم لا يقبل تعليم أحد؛ لأنه لا دليل عنده يعلم أنه من ويجوز أن يوجد بخبره أو لا، ثبت أن كل ذلك استدلال، وهو لازم، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And after that, the truthfulness of many reports has become apparent, so one of the two aspects is not more deserving than the other except by an evidence that clarifies, and God is the granter of success. And [a person] may be treated with harsh treatment and a kind of what harms and pains him until he is forced to say what he cannot know except by report, and there is no power except with God. And similarly is the statement of those who affirm knowledge by direct observation and report while denying knowledge by inference, based on their reasoning about the aspects of benefits in this world and hoped-for consequences, [but] they have no knowledge from direct observation or report; rather that is by inference, and [there is] in that the conjectures of correctness, and likewise knowledge of the truthfulness and falsehood of reports, along with what is said to him in everything known that does not involve sensory knowledge: 'Did you know that?' If he says: 'By report,' he is asked about knowledge of its truthfulness and falsehood, and it enters into what necessity requires in all pleasures and harms, what is avoided and what is approached, through observation and hearing, in order to know the origin of the world or its temporal origination or pre-eternity. And after that, there is nothing that prevents inference, whether a report forbidding it or direct observation; it is as if by inference the statement is prevented, and there is no power except with God. And after that, the knowledge of a person, fire, or something by having once observed it does not come out except through inference by what is known; and if it did not guide him, it would be necessary for him never to deal with anyone, and then not to accept anyone's teaching, because he has no evidence by which he knows that [the person] is from [whom?] and that it is permissible that he exists by his report or not; it is established that all that is inference, and it is necessary, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the necessity of evidence (dalil) in accepting reports and in all knowledge that is not based on direct sensory observation. Maturidi begins by stating that truthfulness of many reports has become clear, but without evidence one cannot prefer truth over falsehood. He then discusses coercion and the reliance on report when knowledge is impossible otherwise. He criticizes those who rely solely on observation and report while denying inferential knowledge (istidlal), pointing out that even their own reasoning about benefits and consequences is a form of inference. He emphasizes that knowledge of the truthfulness of reports requires inquiry, and if someone claims to know by report, he must be asked how he knows it is truthful. This leads to the necessity of inference in all matters, including distinguishing what is beneficial and harmful. Finally, he argues that even recognizing a person or object after a single observation involves inference; without it, one could not interact with anyone or accept teaching. The conclusion is that all such knowledge is inferential and necessary.

Key Terms
خبر (khabar)
دليل (dalil)
استدلال (istidlal)
عيان (ʿiyān)
ظن (ẓann)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inferential reasoning is only for speculative theological matters; everyday knowledge is based on direct observation.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even recognizing a person or object after seeing it once involves inference, and without inference we could not accept teaching or interact with others.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is required to prefer one report over another?
A) The majority of reports
B) Evidence that clarifies
C) Direct sensory observation
D) The reputation of the reporter

Key Takeaway
Evidence is indispensable not only for religious reports but for all knowledge beyond direct sensation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a claim about your faith, do you accept it because it sounds familiar, or do you demand evidence? Where in your life are you relying on reports without verifying them—and what is at stake for your soul if those reports are false?
?
Have you ever dismissed an Islamic teaching because you could not see or experience it directly, ignoring the inferences that point to its truth? What would it mean to submit your intellect to the evidence that Allah has provided through scripture and reason?
?
Maturidi says that even recognizing a person after one sighting is inference. How often do you take for granted the knowledge you have of others, assuming their honesty without proof? What does this say about your reliance on Allah as the ultimate source of all knowledge—and your responsibility to seek truth actively?

Method of Learning
