Indication of the Visible on the Unseen: A Foundational Kalam Method
Maturidi introduces the method of indicating the unseen through the visible and reports a flawed inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi frames the issue of using the visible world as evidence for the unseen, and the problematic conclusion some draw regarding the world's pre-eternity.

Core Idea
The visible is the key to the unseen, but analogy must be used with care.

Maturidi opens a crucial methodological discussion: how can we reason from the world we see to realities we cannot see? This passage presents the initial premise and a controversial inference some have made. As you read, note how Maturidi sets up the problem before offering his own solution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key terms: shahid (visible), gha'ib (unseen), asl (original), far' (branch), qiyas (analogy).
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the argument structure
The passage presents a view that uses analogy to prove the world's pre-eternity. Trace the steps: visible indicates its like, so unseen is like visible, thus unseen is also eternal. Then it indicates a like before it at every time, leading to eternal regress.
Maturidi will later critique this.
3
Reflect on the assumption
Notice the underlying assumption: the branch (far') must not contradict the original (asl). Ask yourself: Is this assumption valid? Does similarity guarantee eternal existence?
This is a key point for kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في دلالة الشاهد على الغائب. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم اختلف في وجه دلالة الشاهد على الغائب، إذ هو أصل للذي غاب عنه، ولا يخالف الأصل فرعه. مع ما كان طريق معرفة الغائب الشاهد، وقياس الشيء نظيره، فيه أثبتوا قدم العالم؛ إذ الشاهد يدل على مثله، فصار الغائب به عالماً أيضاً، ثم يدل في كل وقت على مثله قبله، وفي ذلك إيجاب القدم للكل.
A question concerning the indication of the visible (shāhid) upon the unseen (ghāʾib). Abu Mansur [al-Maturidi], may God have mercy on him, said: Then they differed regarding the manner of the indication of the visible upon the unseen, since it [the visible] is the original for what is absent from it, and the branch does not contradict the original. Along with that, the way of knowing the unseen is the visible, and measuring a thing by its like; by it they affirmed the pre-eternity of the world, because the visible indicates its like, so the unseen became also known by it; then it indicates at every time its like before it, and in that is the affirmation of pre-eternity for the whole.
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage introduces a doctrinal issue (masʾala) concerning how the visible world (al-shāhid) provides evidence for the unseen (al-ghāʾib). Maturidi reports that scholars differed on the precise manner of this indication. The underlying assumption is that the visible is the 'original' (aṣl) and the unseen is the 'branch' (farʿ), and the branch must not contradict the original. The way to know the unseen is through the visible, by analogy (qiyās) of a thing to its like. Some used this to prove the world's pre-eternity (qidam): since the visible indicates its like, and the unseen is like it, the unseen is also eternal; and because at every time the visible indicates a like before it, they concluded that the whole world is eternal. Maturidi sets up this view for later critique.

Key Terms
شاهد (shāhid)
غائب (ghāʾib)
أصل (aṣl)
فرع (farʿ)
قياس (qiyās)
قدم (qidam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any analogy between the visible and unseen is valid and leads to correct conclusions.
Correction: Maturidi will show that analogy must be carefully applied; the inference to the world's pre-eternity is problematic because it overlooks differences between the original and branch.

Checkpoint
According to the first view mentioned, what does the visible world indicate about the unseen?
A) It indicates total difference
B) It indicates similarity, leading to the claim of the world's pre-eternity
C) It indicates that the unseen does not exist
D) It indicates only temporal origination

Key Takeaway
The visible world can indicate the unseen, but the inference that the world is eternal by analogy is problematic and subject to critique.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that what you see in the world around you is an exact pattern for what is unseen about God? When has a flawed analogy led you to a wrong conclusion about your faith? Commit to examining your reasoning against the principles of sound kalam.
?
The scholars differed on the very method of how the visible points to the unseen. Has uncertainty about theological methods ever shaken your confidence? Where do you turn for clarity when analogies fail? Resolve today to study foundational methodologies directly from authoritative sources.
?
In your daily life, you make inferences from the seen to the unseen—about people's intentions, about the future, about the divine. Take a moment to identify one such inference you made recently. Could it be based on an unexamined analogy? How will you align your thinking with the lesson's call for careful methodology?

The Third View: The World Indicates Difference More Clearly
Maturidi explains the third view that the visible indicates both similarity and difference, with difference being clearer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third methodological view on how the visible world points to the unseen, emphasizing that difference is the primary indication and that assuming exact likeness leads to error.

Core Idea
The visible world points more clearly to its difference from God than to similarity.

Maturidi presents a third methodological view among theologians: the visible world indicates both similarity and difference with the unseen, but the indication of difference is stronger. This lesson explores how the world's attributes and effects point to a Creator who is fundamentally different, and why assuming exact likeness leads to absurd conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This passage is complex and has some uncertain phrases, but the overall argument is clear.
2
Identify the list of differences
The passage enumerates several ways the world indicates difference: temporal origination/pre-eternity, creator/self-existence, wisdom/foolishness, choice/nature. Find each pair and notice that each attribute is different from the world itself.
These differences show that the world points to something beyond itself.
3
Understand the rejection of exact likeness
The passage argues that the visible does not indicate that the unseen has an exact like (mithl) to the world. If it did, then every observer could imagine the whole world is like what they observe, which is absurd.
This guards against anthropomorphism and false analogy.
4
Grasp the correct inference via modality
The passage concludes that while the observed does not certify a literal like, knowing its modality (kayfiyya) allows inferring similarity in modality, not identity.
This is the refined method: we infer similarity in the how, not in the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من يقول: يدل على المثل والخلاف ودلالته على الخلاف أوضح، من العالم يدله على حدثه أو قدمه، وقدمه وحدثه ليس هو مثلها ولا نظيرهما، ثم يدله على محدثه أو كون بنفسه. وهما خلافه، ثم يدله على حكمة فاعله وسفهه، واختياره وطبعه، وكل ذلك خلاف ما شاهده. ولا يدله على أن له مثلاً؛ إذ لو كان يدله لكان أن يتوهم كل من عاين يكون كل العالم مثله، وذلك بعيد، فثبت أن الجوهر لا يحقق رؤية نفسه أن يجب مثله شائباً، ويحقق أحد الوجوه التي ذكرناها، لكن إذا عرفت كيفية المشاهد إذا أخبرت بتلك الكيفية الغائب، علمت أنه مثله، لا أن ذلك يحقق المثل.
And among them is one who says: It indicates the similar and the different, and its indication of difference is clearer: from the world it indicates to him its temporal origination or its pre-eternity, and its pre-eternity and temporal origination are not like them nor their parallels; then it indicates to him its originator or its self-existence, and both of these are different from it; then it indicates to him the wisdom of its agent or his foolishness, and his choice or his nature, and all that is different from what he observed. And it does not indicate that it has a like; because if it indicated that, it would be possible for everyone who observes to imagine that all the world is like it, and that is far-fetched. So it is established that the substance does not certify the seeing of itself that its like must be pure, and it certifies one of the aspects we mentioned, but if you know the modality of the observed, when you inform the unseen by that modality, you know that it is like it, not that this certifies the like.
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage presents the third view on how the visible indicates the unseen. Proponents of this view argue that the visible world indicates both similarity and difference, but the indication of difference is clearer. For example, from the world one can infer its temporal origination (ḥudūth) or pre-eternity (qidam), but these attributes are not themselves similar to the world. Similarly, the world points to a creator (muḥdith) or self-existence (kawn bi-nafsih), both of which are different from the world. The world also indicates wisdom or foolishness in its agent, choice or nature—all different from what is observed. Importantly, the visible does not indicate that the unseen must be exactly like it; if it did, then everyone who observes a part of the world could imagine the whole world to be like that part, which is absurd. Rather, the correct method is: if you understand the modality (kayfiyya) of the observed and apply that modality to the unseen, you can know that it is similar, but this does not entail complete identity. This view thus avoids the false analogy that leads to atheism or anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
مثل (mithl)
خلاف (khilāf)
محدث (muḥdith)
كون بنفسه (kawn bi-nafsih)
كيفية (kayfiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world's attributes (like temporality) are similar to the Creator's attributes.
Correction: The passage stresses that temporal origination and pre-eternity are not like the world; they are different categories.

Checkpoint
According to the third view, what does the visible world indicate more clearly?
A) Its own similarity to the unseen
B) Its difference from the unseen
C) That the unseen does not exist
D) That the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
The visible world points to its Creator primarily through difference, not through exact similarity; assuming exact likeness leads to false conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God, do you unconsciously imagine Him as similar to something you know? How does this passage challenge you to purify your conception of tawhid?
?
If the world primarily indicates God's difference from creation, how should that affect your awe and reverence when you reflect on His attributes?
?
Are you more inclined to look for God's 'signs' in similarities or in the unique aspects of creation? Which tendency is more spiritually dangerous, and why?

Refuting the Original-Branch Reversal
Maturidi refutes the claim that the visible is the original and the unseen the branch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi reverses the claim that the visible is the original and the unseen is the branch, and why the unseen (the Pre-eternal) is the true original.

Core Idea
The invisible divine is the original; the visible world is derived from it.

Maturidi confronts a common methodological assumption: that we understand the unseen by analogy with the visible. But he shows this is backwards. The Pre-eternal existed before anything visible, so the unseen is the original, and the visible is the branch. This passage corrects a fundamental error in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Maturidi begins with a direct rejection: 'What is claimed about the original and the branch is reversed.'
The passage is dense; reading it slowly helps.
2
Identify the reversal argument
Find the reason Maturidi gives: the Pre-eternal (al-Qadim) and pre-eternity (al-qidam) existed before anything by which inference could be made. Therefore, the unseen cannot be a branch of the visible; it is the original.
This is the core logical move.
3
Understand the analogy
Maturidi uses the example of a building coming from a builder, or writing from a writer. These actions are accidents, not substances. They show that the visible world points to an originator, not that the visible is the standard for the unseen.
The analogy reinforces the reversal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما زعم من الأصل والفرع فمقلوب؛ لما كان القديم والقدم ولم يكن ما به استدلال، فلذلك لم يجب جعله فرعاً لهذا، بل هو الأصل لكون هذا به. ثم كل كائن بغيره من طريق العقل تخارج من جوهرة في الشاهد كالبناء والكتابة، وكل أنواع الأفعال والأقوال التي هي أعراض لمن كن لم يجز أن يلحقهم بالجوهر والصفة، فمثله الذي به العالم.
And what is claimed about the original and the branch is reversed; because the Pre-eternal and pre-eternity existed, and there was nothing by which inference was made, therefore it is not necessary to make it a branch of this, rather it is the original for this being [established] by it. Then everything that comes into being through another by way of reason, emerging from a substance in the visible, like building and writing, and all kinds of actions and statements that are accidents for those who were not, it is not permissible to attach them to the substance and attribute, so its like is that by which the world [exists].
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage refutes the assumption that the visible (shahid) is the original (asl) and the unseen (gha'ib) is the branch (far'). Maturidi argues that the situation is actually reversed: the Pre-eternal (al-Qadim) and pre-eternity (al-qidam) existed before there was anything by which inference could be made. Therefore, the unseen (the divine) is the original, and the visible world is derived from it. Then he adds that everything that comes into being (kain) through another, by way of reason, emerges from a substance in the visible—like a building from a builder, or writing from a writer. These actions and statements are accidents (a'rad) and cannot be attached to the substance and attribute in the same way. Thus, the true 'like' (mithl) is that by which the world exists, i.e., the creator. The passage corrects the methodological error of treating the contingent as the standard for the necessary.

Key Terms
أصل (aṣl)
فرع (farʿ)
قديم (qadīm)
قدم (qidam)
عرض (ʿaraḍ)
جوهر (jawhar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the visible world is the standard for understanding the unseen divine, making the visible the original and the unseen the branch.
Correction: Maturidi argues the opposite: the Pre-eternal (unseen) is the original, and the visible world is derived from it. The visible world cannot serve as the original because it is contingent and came into being after the Pre-eternal.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the claimed relationship between original and branch?
A) It is correct as stated
B) It is reversed because the Pre-eternal is the original
C) It is irrelevant to the discussion
D) There is no distinction between original and branch

Key Takeaway
The Pre-eternal (Allah) is the original, and the visible world is the branch derived from Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your life, do you treat your immediate circumstances (the visible) as the ultimate reality, or do you live with the awareness that the Pre-eternal Allah is the original and your entire existence is a branch of His will? How would your priorities shift if you truly internalized this reversal?
?
In what specific area of your faith do you find yourself using the visible world as the measure for the unseen—perhaps in judging divine mercy by your own experiences, or in limiting Allah's power by what you can perceive? Today, what step can you take to submit your reasoning to the truth that the Pre-eternal is the original?
?
The passage tells us that the Pre-eternal existed with nothing else from which to infer. How does that silence your tendency to demand evidence for the unseen? Will you accept that your inability to fully comprehend is not a flaw in the truth, but a reflection of your contingency?

The Unseen is Known Through Proofs, Not Senses
Maturidi refutes two objections by distinguishing sensory and rational knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that rational truths must be imaginable is invalid, and that knowledge of the unseen is acquired through proofs.

Core Idea
The unseen is known through proofs, not senses; an objection based on imagination misunderstands reason.

In this passage, Maturidi confronts a common objection: that theological claims about creation from nothing are irrational because they cannot be imagined. He argues that this objection mistakenly expects sensory evidence for non-sensory matters. Using vivid analogies, he shows that each kind of knowledge has its proper organ: the intellect for the unseen, the senses for the seen. He then redefines 'imagination in the mind' as existence through proofs, not sensory representation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.
Notice that Maturidi is addressing two specific objections.
2
Identify the first argument: the analogy of senses
Maturidi compares the objector to someone trying to distinguish sounds by sight or colors by hearing. Reflect on why this analogy refutes the demand for sensory evidence.
This shows that each sense has its domain; intellect has its own.
3
Understand the second argument: redefinition of 'imagination'
Maturidi says that 'imagination in the mind' actually means existence through proofs, not a mental image. This refutes the objection that the concept is inconceivable.
Thus, the objector's premise is based on a misunderstanding.
4
Consider the common misunderstanding
The misconception is that rational knowledge must be imaginable. How does Maturidi correct this? Connect to the lesson objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله: وما ذكر من الخروج عن المعقول بما لا يتصور في فقد بينا وبعد: فإن ذلك عقل خص به من لا عقل له، لأنه طلب معرفة ما ليس طريقه الحس بالحس، فهو كمن يريد أن يميز بين الأصوات بالبصر وبين الألوان بالسمع، وكذا كل معروف بحس أحب أن يعقل ذلك بغيره فيقصر عنه عقله، فمثله ما كان طريق العلم به غير الحواس، فأراد الوصول إليه بها لم يسعنه عقله. وهذا الجواب جواب لقوله أيضا: كون شيء من غير شيء خارج من المعقول. وللأمرين جواب آخر وهو أن يقال: يعنى بالتصور في الوهم الوجود بالأدلة.
The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: And what was mentioned of departing from the rational by that which is not imagined in — we have already explained. And after: That is a reason that is particular to one who has no reason, because he seeks knowledge of that whose path is not the senses by the senses. He is like one who wants to distinguish between sounds by sight and between colors by hearing. Likewise, everything known by a sense, if someone loves to understand that by something else, his intellect falls short of it. So the example of that is: something whose path of knowledge is not the senses, if he wants to attain it by them, his intellect does not avail him. And this answer is also an answer to his statement: 'A thing coming from nothing is outside the rational.' And for the two matters there is another answer: that the meaning of 'imagination in the mind' is existence through proofs.
Source pages p. 631

Commentary
This passage is a response to two objections: (1) the claim that the idea of something 'departing from the rational' is invalid because it cannot be imagined, and (2) the claim that 'something coming from nothing is irrational'. Maturidi first says that only someone lacking intellect would try to understand non-sensory matters using the senses, like distinguishing sounds by sight or colors by hearing. Similarly, if something is known through a specific sense, that sense cannot be replaced by another. Therefore, matters whose path of knowledge is not the senses cannot be apprehended by the senses. So the objection that such concepts are irrational is invalid because they are known through intellectual proofs (أدلة). He then gives a second answer: 'imagination in the mind' (التصور في الوهم) actually means existence through proofs, not sensory representation. Thus, the objections are refuted.

Key Terms
معقول (rational)
حس (sense)
تصور في الوهم (imagination in the mind)
أدلة (proofs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational knowledge must be imaginable or perceivable by the senses.
Correction: Maturidi shows that matters known through intellect are not subject to sensory imagination; they are established through proofs, not sensory representation.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi respond to the objection that something coming from nothing is irrational?
A) He admits it is irrational but says it is still true.
B) He argues that such concepts are known through intellectual proofs, not sensory imagination.
C) He uses sensory examples to prove it is rational.
D) He says it is only for those with special reason.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of the unseen is attained through intellectual proofs, not sensory imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you struggle to imagine a theological truth (like the nature of the afterlife), do you find yourself doubting it? How might Maturidi's analogy of mixing senses challenge you to trust proofs over mental imagery? Will you consciously remind yourself that 'not imaginable' does not mean 'untrue'?
?
Think of a time you dismissed an Islamic belief because it felt 'unreasonable' based on your sensory experience. What would it mean for you to instead seek out the proofs (أدلة) for that belief? Are you willing to commit to studying rational evidence for matters that discomfort you?
?
Maturidi says that demanding sensory imagination for rational matters is 'a reason particular to one who has no reason.' In what areas of your life do you silently insist on tangible evidence before submitting to truth? Can you identify one unseen reality you need to accept through proof, and take a concrete step to seek that proof today?

Answer to the Objection: Creation from Nothing is Rational
Maturidi refutes the claim that creation ex nihilo is irrational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that creation ex nihilo is irrational, and why it is a category error to apply sensory standards to non-sensory realities.

Core Idea
Creation from nothing is rational; the objection confuses sensory and non-sensory domains.

Maturidi responds to an objection that creation ex nihilo is 'outside reason'. He argues that this objection stems from a mistaken attempt to apply sensory criteria to a non-sensory matter. In this lesson, we will see how he uses an analogy of mixing senses to illustrate the fallacy, and then provides a second answer clarifying the meaning of 'conception in the imagination'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's own argumentative flow come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage contains two distinct answers to the objection.
2
Understand the analogy of senses
Maturidi compares the objector to someone who tries to distinguish sounds by sight and colors by hearing. This shows the absurdity of applying one sense to another's domain.
The analogy highlights that sensory standards cannot evaluate non-sensory realities.
3
Identify the two answers
The passage first gives the analogy as a refutation, then adds a second answer: that 'conception in the imagination' should be understood as existence by evidence, not imaginative impossibility.
4
Reflect on the doctrine
Consider why this argument matters for Islamic theology. Maturidi defends the rationality of creation ex nihilo against those who demand sensory proof.
This connects to the broader topic of divine attributes and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد: فإن ذلك عقل خص به من لا عقل له، لأنه طلب معرفة ما ليس طريقه الحس بالحس، فهو كمن يريد أن يميز بين الأصوات بالبصر وبين الألوان بالسمع، وكذا كل معروف بحس أحب أن يعقل ذلك بغيره فيقصر عنه عقله، فمثله ما كان طريق العلم به غير الحواس، فأراد الوصول إليه بها لم يسعنه عقله. وهذا الجواب جواب لقوله أيضا: كون شيء من غير شيء خارج من المعقول. وللأمرين جواب آخر وهو أن يقال: يعنى بالتصور في الوهم الوجود بالأدلة، فهو لازم، ولا نقول بما ليس فيه ذلك.
And after: For that is an intellect particular to one who has no intellect, because he seeks knowledge of what is not attainable by the senses through the senses. He is like one who wants to distinguish sounds by sight and colors by hearing. Likewise, every thing known by a sense, if one loves to understand it by another sense, his intellect falls short of it. Similarly, what the way of knowledge of it is other than the senses, if one seeks to reach it by them, his intellect does not suffice him. And this answer is also an answer to his saying: 'Something coming from nothing is outside reason.' And for both matters there is another answer: that what is meant by 'conception in the imagination' is existence by evidence, so it is necessary, and we do not say what does not have that.
Source pages p. 631

Commentary
Maturidi responds to the objection that creation from nothing (ex nihilo) is irrational. He argues this objection comes from someone who lacks intellect because they try to evaluate non-sensory realities using sensory methods. It is like using sight to judge sounds. Just as each sense has its domain, knowledge of the super-sensible (like origination from nothing) comes through evidence (أدلة), not sensory imagination. The term 'conception in the imagination' (تصور في الوهم) should mean existence established by proofs, not imaginative impossibility.

Correction: Maturidi shows it is a category error to demand sensory evidence for a non-sensory reality; the correct standard is evidence (adilla), not imagination.

The Principle of Tawhid: Beginning as Tashbih, Ending as Tawhid
Maturidi states the fundamental principle of tawhid discourse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle that speaking about Allah's attributes must begin with terms from creation but end with affirming His uniqueness, avoiding both ta'til and tashbih.

Core Idea
Tawhid begins with comparison but ends with uniqueness.

This lesson introduces a foundational principle from Imam al-Maturidi on how we should speak about Allah's attributes. Maturidi explains that human language and reason are necessarily constrained—we can only use words we know from creation. But he warns that this must not lead to anthropomorphism; instead, we must affirm the attributes while simultaneously negating any likeness to creation. Let's read his precise words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the literal English translation. Pay attention to the key terms: tashbih, tawhid, ta'til.
Try to grasp the overall argument before diving into details.
2
Identify the three key concepts
Identify tashbih (comparison), tawhid (affirmation of uniqueness), and ta'til (divestment). Notice how Maturidi defines each in relation to divine discourse.
These three concepts are the pillars of his method.
3
Understand the necessity and method
Ask yourself: Why must we start with tashbih? How is the negation of tashbih included within the affirmation? Maturidi uses the example of afterlife rewards and punishments to illustrate necessity.
The key is that we affirm attributes 'not like the creation' (لاجعل نفي التشبيه ضمن الإثبات).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور: والأصل في حرف التوحيد أن ابتداءه تشبيه وانتهاءه توحيد، دفعت إلى ذلك الضرورة، إذ بالمدرك المفهوم يستدل على ما قصرت الأفهام من إدراك ما عن الأوهام، نحو ما يُدرك ثواب الآخرة وعقابها بلذات الدنيا والأذيات التي فيها، وكذا وصف الله تعالى بالمدرك من خلقه للدلالة والعبارة، فقيل: عالم وقادر، ونحو ذلك؛ إذ في الإمساك عن ذلك تعطيل، وفي تحقيق المعنى الموجود في خلقه تشبيه، فوصل به، لا كالعلماء، ونحوه ليجعل نفي التشبيه ضمن الإثبات. فهذا فما ألزمت ضرورة العقل القول به والسمع جميعا فأما ما لا سمع فيه ولا في العقل احتماله فالتسمية به جرأة عظيمة، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Abu Mansur said: The principle regarding the letter of tawhid is that its beginning is tashbih (comparison) and its end is tawhid (affirmation of unity). Necessity drove to that, for by what is perceived and understood one infers what intellects fall short of grasping beyond imaginations, like how the reward of the Hereafter and its punishment are perceived through the pleasures and pains of this world. Similarly, the description of Allah Almighty by what is perceived from His creation for indication and expression, so it is said: 'knowing', 'powerful', and the like. For refraining from that is ta'til (divesting God of attributes), and actualizing the existing meaning in His creation is tashbih (comparison), so He is described by it, not like the scholars [؟], and the like, so that the negation of tashbih is placed within the affirmation. Thus, this is what the necessity of reason and scripture have compelled to say. As for what has no scriptural basis nor any possibility in reason, naming by it is great audacity. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage states the foundational principle of the science of tawhid (monotheism) as articulated by Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. He declares that the 'letter of tawhid' (حرف التوحيد) begins with tashbih (comparison) but ends with tawhid (affirmation of God's uniqueness). By 'beginning' he means the initial step of using terms borrowed from created things (like 'knowing', 'powerful') to speak about God. This is a necessity (ضرورة) because human intellects can only reason from what is perceived (المدرك المفهوم) to what lies beyond. He gives an analogy: we understand the rewards and punishments of the afterlife through the pleasures and pains of this world. Likewise, we describe God using attributes perceived in creation, e.g., 'knowing' and 'powerful'. Avoiding such descriptions leads to ta'til (stripping God of all attributes), which is unacceptable. However, affirming these attributes in the exact sense they have in created beings would be tashbih (anthropomorphism). So, the method is to affirm the attributes while simultaneously negating any likeness to creation—placing the negation of tashbih within the affirmation. This is what reason and revelation necessitate. Any term that has neither scriptural warrant nor rational basis is presumption (جرأة عظيمة). The passage thus provides the crucial hermeneutical principle: use terms from creation for God but always with the simultaneous negation of similarity.

Key Terms
حرف التوحيد
تشبيه
توحيد
تعطيل
ضرورة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi's principle allows anthropomorphism because it starts with tashbih.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the beginning is tashbih only as a necessary starting point, but the end is tawhid, and the negation of tashbih is inherent within the affirmation.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why must the description of God begin with tashbih (comparison)?
A) Because God is like His creation
B) Because reason cannot access any knowledge without comparison
C) Because necessity compels using perceived concepts to speak of the unseen
D) Because scripture explicitly commands using anthropomorphic language

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's principle is that we must use terms from creation for Allah but always include the negation of similarity, thus avoiding both stripping Allah of attributes and likening Him to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
Maturidi says necessity drove this principle. Where in your own faith journey have you been pushed by necessity—rather than comfort—to deepen your understanding of Allah? Will you embrace that discomfort as a path to truer tawhid?

The Principle of Tawhid: Beginning with Tashbih, Ending with Tawhid
Maturidi states the principle that tawhid begins with tashbih and ends with tawhid due to necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle that theological discourse on tawhid necessarily begins with tashbih (likening) and ends with tawhid (declaring uniqueness), and how this avoids both ta'til and anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Tawhid begins with tashbih to avoid ta'til, but ends by negating similarity within affirmation.

Maturidi opens a key methodological discussion: how can we speak about Allah without either stripping Him of attributes or likening Him to creation? He argues that necessity forces us to start from what we know, but we must always negate any similarity. This passage lays out that delicate balance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key terms: tashbih, tawhid, ta'til. Let the argument flow.
The passage is dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the core principle
Maturidi states: 'The beginning is tashbih, the end is tawhid.' Why is tashbih the beginning? Because we need to use understood concepts. Why is tawhid the end? Because we must negate likeness.
3
Examine the reasoning by necessity
He says necessity drove to that. He gives the example of reward/punishment in the afterlife understood through worldly pleasures/pains. Then he applies it to divine attributes: we must speak, but we add 'not like' to negate tashbih.
This is the keystone of the solution.
4
Understand the consequences
He warns that remaining silent leads to ta'til, and affirming the full created meaning leads to tashbih. The only path is affirmative negation. Then he distinguishes what is required by reason and scripture from what is audacious.
This guards against both extremes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابو منصور : والأصل في حرف التوحيد أن ابتداءه تشبيه وانتهاءه توحيد ، دفعت إلى ذلك الضرورة ، إذ بالمدرك المفهوم يستدل على ما قصرت الأفهام من إدراك ما عن الأوهام ، نحو ما يُدرك ثواب الآخرة وعقابها بلذات الدنيا والأذيات التي فيها ، وكذا وصف الله تعالى بالمدرك من خلقه للدلالة والعبارة ، فقيل : عالم وقادر، ونحو ذلك ؛ إذ في الإمساك عن ذلك تعطيل ، وفي تحقيق المعنى الموجود في خلقه تشبيه ، فوصل به، لا كالعلماء، ونحوه ليجعل نفى التشبيه ضمن الإثبات . فهذا فما ألزمت ضرورة العقل القول به والسمع جميعا فأما ما لا سمع فيه ولا في العقل احتماله فالتسمية به جرأة عظيمة، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Abu Mansur said: The principle in the matter of tawhid is that its beginning is tashbih (anthropomorphism) and its end is tawhid (affirmation of oneness), necessity drove to that, for by the perceived and understood we infer what intellects fall short of perceiving beyond imaginings, like what is perceived of the reward of the Hereafter and its punishment by the pleasures and pains of this world, and likewise the description of Allah Almighty by what is perceived from His creation for indication and expression, so it was said: 'knower' and 'powerful', and the like; for in refraining from that is ta'til (divesting of attributes), and in actualizing the meaning present in His creation is tashbih, so He [his description] is connected with it [the created term], 'not like the knowers' and the like, so as to place negation of tashbih within affirmation. So this is what the necessity of reason and scripture together compelled to say. As for what there is no scripture about nor reason's capacity, then naming with it is great audacity. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 641

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational principle for discussing Allah's attributes. Maturidi states that the discourse of tawhid (theology of divine oneness) begins with tashbih (likening God to creation) and ends with tawhid (declaring His uniqueness). This is forced by necessity: we can only understand abstract realities through perceived examples, just as we conceive of afterlife reward and punishment based on worldly pleasures and pains. Similarly, we describe Allah using terms like 'knower' and 'powerful' that we know from creation. The alternative – remaining silent – leads to ta'til (divesting Allah of attributes), while fully asserting the created meaning would be tashbih. The solution is to use the term but add a negation of similarity, e.g., 'not like the knowers', thereby embedding the negation of tashbih within the affirmation. This is obligatory upon both reason and revelation. Any term not supported by scripture nor rationally conceivable is an audacious naming.

Key Terms
تشبيه
توحيد
تعطيل
ضرورة العقل
السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tawhid requires avoiding any form of comparison between Allah and creation, including in language.
Correction: Maturidi argues that some comparison (tashbih) is necessary at the start to enable understanding, but it must be negated within the affirmation to achieve true tawhid.

Checkpoint
What is the result of refraining from using any descriptive terms for Allah according to this passage?
A) Tawhid (oneness)
B) Tashbih (anthropomorphism)
C) Ta'til (divesting of attributes)
D) Tasdiq (affirmation)

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's principle teaches that to speak of Allah we must begin with analogical terms from creation but always negate any similarity, thereby avoiding both ta'til and tashbih.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Knowing,' do you inwardly and immediately add 'but not like the knowers'? If not, your heart may be guilty of tashbih. Will you train yourself to make this negation a habit in your supplications and thoughts?
?

?

Method of Learning

Methodological Principle for Understanding Divine Speech
Maturidi explains that divine speech must be understood differently from human speech due to divine transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's methodological principle that divine attributes should not be understood by analogy with creation, requiring suspension of judgment (waqf) rather than definitive interpretation.

Core Idea
Stop where Allah stops; do not transfer created meanings to the Creator.

Imam Maturidi presents a foundational principle for interpreting divine speech: our natural inclination is to understand words based on our experience with created things. But since Allah is absolutely transcendent, we must resist this impulse. This lesson unpacks his argument and introduces the method of tafwid (stopping) as the correct approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
The Arabic may have uncertain readings; focus on the overall logic.
2
Identify the core principle
Maturidi states that because Allah is exalted above similarities, we cannot transfer meanings derived from creation to Him. Find where he makes this explicit.
Look for the phrase 'لم يجز أن يفهم من الإضافة إليه المفهوم من غيره'.
3
Understand the two premises
The argument rests on two premises: (1) Allah is known differently before hearing the term, and (2) terms in the witnessed world have multiple meanings. Note how Maturidi uses these to block anthropomorphism.
The second premise is illustrated with examples like 'above', 'Throne', and 'istiwā'.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the proper response is to stop (waqf) and not to cut (qat') or force a specific interpretation. See how he links this to the Qur'anic verses of promise/threat and the disconnected letters.
The trial (miḥna) is in stopping, not in claiming to know the exact meaning.
5
Reflect on the broader methodology
Ask yourself: how does this principle protect tawhid? How does it relate to other attributes like hand or face? This passage is a key foundation for the approach of tafwid.
Maturidi does not reject interpretation outright, but limits it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل في هذا أن الأمر يضيق على السامع بما يقدره من المفهوم عن الخلق في الوجود، وإذ لزم القول في الله بالتعالى عن الأشباه ذاتا وفعلا، لم يجز أن يفهم من الإضافة إليه المفهوم من غيره في الوجود، مع ما كان الوقوف على هو عليه على احتمال المعنى يصرف إليه الكلام في الخلق بما به قبل سمع ذلك الكلام، والله سبحانه عرف قبل سمع ذلك الكلام على غير الذي عرف عليه الخلق، لم يجز صرف التأويل إلى ما فهمه من الخلق : إذ سبق العلم المتقدم منه، ذلك المعنى معنى قد يفهم من الشاهد من على، ومن العرش، ومن الاستواء معان مختلفة، لم يجز صرف ذلك إلى أوحش وجه، وثمة لأحسن ذلك مساغ ما كان الله يمتحن بالوقوف في أشياء كما جاء من نعوت الوعد والوعيد، وما جاء من الحروف المقطعة، وغير ذلك مما يؤمن المرء أن يكون ذا مما المحنة فيه الوقف لا القطع، والله أعلم.
The principle in this is that the matter becomes constricted for the listener by what he estimates of the understanding from creation in existence. And since the statement about Allah is necessary as being exalted above similarities in essence and action, it is not permissible to understand from the attribution to Him what is understood from other than Him in existence, along with the fact that the stopping upon what He is with the possibility of the meaning to which the speech is directed concerning creation was according to what was before hearing that speech. And Allah glorified is known before hearing that speech in a way other than that by which creation is known. It is not permissible to direct the interpretation to what he understood from creation, because the prior knowledge from Him preceded. That meaning—it is a meaning that may be understood from the witnessed world from above, and from the Throne, and from istiwā'—different meanings. It is not permissible to direct that to the harshest aspect, and then for the best of that there is a way, as long as Allah tests by stopping at things, as came from the descriptions of promise and threat, and what came of the disconnected letters, and other things by which a person believes that the trial in them is to stop not to cut. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 673

Commentary
This passage is a methodological clarification about interpreting divine attributes. Maturidi argues that when a person hears a divine attribute term (like 'istiwā''), his understanding is naturally colored by his experience with created things. But since Allah is absolutely transcendent (mut'aalī) in essence and action, we must not transfer that created understanding to Him. Moreover, before hearing such terms, Allah is already known in a way that is unlike any creature. Therefore, it is invalid to force an interpretation based on human understanding. The same term can have multiple meanings in the witnessed world (e.g., 'above' can be spatial, metaphorical, etc.), so we should not resort to the most repulsive (i.e., anthropomorphic) interpretation. Instead, the proper response is to stop and affirm, just as we do with other revealed matters like the promises and threats of the Qur'an, or the disconnected letters (al-muqatta'āt). The trial (miḥna) in these matters is to stop (waqf) and believe, not to cut (qat') or definitively interpret in a humanlike manner.

Key Terms
التعالي
الإضافة
التأويل
الوقف
الحروف المقطعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine attributes must be either interpreted metaphorically or taken literally in a humanlike way, with no third option.
Correction: Maturidi presents a third way: stop (waqf) at the meaning without specifying, affirming that Allah is known differently and that the exact modality is beyond human comprehension.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it not permissible to interpret divine attributes based on what we understand from creation?
A) Because human language is inadequate for divine matters.
B) Because Allah is already known in a way different from creation before hearing the term.
C) Because the Qur'an is in a special divine Arabic that cannot be translated.
D) Because the Companions unanimously rejected interpretation.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that divine attributes must be understood without analogical transfer from creation, and the proper response is to stop (waqf) rather than to interpret definitively.

Will you accept that your mind cannot grasp His reality? What would it take for you to be comfortable with that uncertainty?
?
The principle here is that the trial is in the stopping, not in the cutting. Are you someone who needs to 'figure out' every divine attribute to feel secure in your faith? This passage demands that you trust Allah's transcendence more than your own understanding. Can you let go of the need for a clear image and instead believe with your heart? What specific attribute do you struggle most with? Commit to repeating 'I believe as Allah intended without how' every time that doubt arises.

Maturidi says your understanding is constricted by your experience with creation. Your view of 'mercy', 'anger', or 'love' is shaped by human examples. How do you approach divine mercy? The Principle of Following the Texts on Ru'ya
Maturidi states the methodological principle for ru'ya: follow the texts, negate creaturely meanings, and do not interpret.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the methodological principle for affirming ru'ya (vision of Allah) as taught by Imam al-Maturidi: to affirm the texts exactly as they came, to negate any creaturely attributes, and to refrain from speculative interpretation.

Core Idea
Follow the texts, negate creaturely meanings, and do not interpret.

In this short but foundational passage, Imam al-Maturidi lays down the essential rule for dealing with scriptural references to seeing Allah in the Hereafter. He instructs us to affirm the reality of ru'ya exactly as the texts have come, to strip away any meanings that belong to created beings, and to avoid interpreting the texts beyond what has been transmitted. This principle keeps faith anchored in revelation and protects against both anthropomorphism and unwarranted figurative readings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Notice how the Imam states three distinct instructions in one sentence.
The passage is dense; take time to absorb each part.
2
Identify the three components of the principle
The principle has three parts: (1) affirm ru'ya according to what has come, (2) negate every creaturely meaning, and (3) do not interpret because no interpretation has come. Understand each part separately.
These three components safeguard the doctrine from different errors.
3
Reflect on the rationale for not interpreting
Maturidi gives a clear reason for not interpreting: no authoritative interpretation (tafsir) has come from revelation. This means we cannot impose our own reasoning on the texts about ru'ya. Ask yourself why this restriction is important.
This relates to the broader principle of tafwid (consigning the modality to Allah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: والأصل فيه القول بذلك على قدر ما جاء، ونفى كل معنى من معانى الخلق، ولا يفسر؛ لما لم يجئ في ذلك تفسير، والله الموفق.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And the principle regarding it is to say concerning it according to what has come, and to negate every meaning from the meanings of creation, and not to interpret it, because no interpretation has come regarding that. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 681

Commentary
This short passage states a key methodological principle for the doctrine of ru'ya (vision of Allah). Maturidi says the fundamental rule (al-asl) is to affirm the vision as it has come in the revealed sources (the Quran and hadith), without adding or subtracting. One must negate (nafy) any meaning that belongs to created things—i.e., do not ascribe creaturely attributes to the vision. Moreover, one should not interpret (tafsir) the texts about ru'ya in a way that goes beyond the literal meaning, because no authoritative interpretation has been transmitted. The principle is one of tafwid (consigning the modality to Allah) while affirming the reality. Maturidi ends by invoking Allah's guidance.

Key Terms
الأصل (al-asl)
الرؤية (ru'ya)
التفسير (tafsir)
معاني الخلق (ma'ani al-khalq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any rational interpretation of ru'ya texts is permissible or even necessary to avoid anthropomorphism.
Correction: Maturidi restricts interpretation to what has been transmitted. Since no authoritative interpretation (tafsir) has come from revelation, we must not engage in speculative interpretation. Instead, we affirm the reality while negating creaturely meanings.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what should be done with texts about ru'ya?
A) Interpret them metaphorically
B) Affirm them as they came, negating creaturely meanings, and not interpret them
C) Reject them as anthropomorphic
D) Only accept them if they are rational

Key Takeaway
The principle for ru'ya is to affirm the texts exactly as they have come, negate any creaturely attributes, and refrain from speculative interpretation—consigning the modality to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine seeing Allah in the Hereafter, do you catch yourself forming a mental image or attributing human-like features? How does Maturidi's command to 'negate every meaning from the meanings of creation' expose the subtle shirk in your own heart, and what will you do to purify your belief?
?
The principle says 'do not interpret it, because no interpretation has come regarding that.' Are you willing to leave some of your most burning questions unanswered, trusting that Allah's wisdom is greater than your need to understand? What specific question about the unseen are you clinging to that you need to release to Allah?
?
Maturidi ends with 'And Allah is the granter of success.' Do you genuinely believe that only Allah can grant you the ability to affirm His attributes without distortion? In what area of your life have you been relying on your own intellect rather than begging Allah for success in submitting to revelation?

The Principle of Affirming Vision of Allah
Maturidi states the principle for affirming vision of Allah without anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the methodological principle for affirming the vision of Allah as taught by Imam al-Maturidi: to affirm only what has been revealed, to negate any created attributes, and to refrain from interpretation where no transmitted interpretation exists.

Core Idea
Certainty in seeing does not require full comprehension.

Imam al-Maturidi begins by illustrating a key point: we see the moon with certainty, yet we do not know its precise limits. This sets the stage for the theological principle that affirming Allah's vision does not require anthropomorphic understanding. In this passage, he lays down the rule for dealing with divine attributes: stick to the revealed text, negate creaturely meanings, and avoid unwarranted interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the author's words sink in before analyzing.
2
Notice the example of the moon
Maturidi uses the moon to show that we can be certain of seeing something without knowing its precise limits. This analogy is crucial for understanding the principle.
This guards against the misconception that vision requires full comprehension.
3
Identify the three parts of the principle
The principle has three components: affirm as revealed, negate created meanings, and do not interpret without transmitted interpretation. Understand each part and how they work together.
These three parts prevent both anthropomorphism and baseless allegorical interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالقمر: أنه لا يُعرف حده ولا سعته ليوقف ويحاط به ويرى بيقين، / ولا قوة إلا بالله. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والأصل فيه القول بذلك على قدر ما جاء، ونفى كل معنى من معانى الخلق، ولا يفسر؛ لما لم يجئ [فى ذلك تفسير]، والله الموفق.
By the moon: that its limit and extent are not known to be stopped and encompassed, and it is seen with certainty. And there is no power except with Allah. Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The principle concerning it is to say about it according to what has come, and to negate every meaning from the meanings of creation, and not to interpret, because no interpretation has come concerning that. And Allah is the Granter of success.
Source pages p. 681

Commentary
This passage defines the methodological principle for affirming the vision of Allah (ru'ya). It begins by giving an example: we see the moon with certainty, yet we do not know its precise limit or extent. This illustrates that certainty in seeing does not require full comprehension. The author, Imam al-Maturidi, then states the principle: in matters of the divine, we affirm only what has been revealed in the texts, and we negate any attributes that belong to created beings (ma'ani al-khalq). Moreover, we do not engage in interpretation (tafsir) when no interpretation has been transmitted. This guards against both anthropomorphism (tashbih) and excessive allegorical interpretation (ta'wil). The passage is a statement of methodology and a foundation for the subsequent refutation of the Mu'tazila who denied vision of Allah.

Key Terms

الخلق
تفسير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That seeing Allah requires full comprehension of His essence, like seeing a physical object.
Correction: Maturidi shows that certainty of vision does not depend on knowing limits; we affirm vision as revealed while negating any creaturely attributes.

D) To deny vision entirely because it cannot be comprehended.

Key Takeaway
The principle for affirming the vision of Allah is to affirm only what is revealed, negate any created attributes, and refrain from interpretation without transmitted basis.

The Principle of Metaphorical Interpretation in Kalam
Maturidi states the principle of metaphorical interpretation in kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle of metaphorical interpretation in kalam and how linguistic context determines whether a word is taken literally or figuratively.

Core Idea
Context is key: words are diverted from literal meaning when used in a customary context or paired with a specific purpose.

Maturidi begins by establishing a foundational hermeneutic principle: words are not always taken at face value. In theological discourse, especially when dealing with divine attributes, one must consider the customary usage and the intended purpose. This passage lays out the rule and applies it to Quranic verses about 'seeing' Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to get the overall meaning. This first reading gives you the author's own flow.
Notice the direct, instructional style: Maturidi states the principle, then gives examples.
2
Identify the main principle and its conditions
Locate the sentence that states the principle. What are the two conditions under which a word is diverted from its literal meaning?
The conditions are: (1) if it is upon a customary matter, or (2) if the intended purpose is paired with it.
3
Analyse the Quranic examples and the linguistic rule
Look at the two Quranic verses cited and the subsequent linguistic explanation. How do the examples illustrate the rule? What does the rule say about the verb 'see' when used with a person vs. when used with their action?
The key distinction is between seeing the essence and seeing the action or state.
4
Reflect on the application to the story of Moses
Consider how Maturidi applies this principle to the story of Moses (implied from 'You shall not see Me'). Why does the context of the request matter?
The passage uses this to show that the prohibition of vision does not necessarily negate the possibility of seeing Allah's actions or signs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : والأصل في الكلام أنه إذا كان على أمر معهود أو يقرن به المقصود إليه صُرف عن حقيقته وإلا لا، نحو قوله: أَلَمْ تَرَ إِلَى رَبِّكَ كَيْفَ مَدَّ الظَّلِّ و أَلَمْ تَرَ كَيْفَ فَعَلَ رَبُّكَ، وأصله أن من قال: رأيت فلانا أو نظرت إلى فلان لم يحتمل غير ذاته، وإذا قال: رأيته يقول كذا ويفعل كذا أنه لا يريد به رؤية ذاته، قتله أمر قصة موسى عليه السلام وهذه الآية.
The jurist Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The principle in speech is that if it is upon a customary matter or the intended purpose is paired with it, it is diverted from its literal meaning, otherwise not. For example, His saying: 'Have you not seen your Lord, how He extended the shadow' and 'Have you not seen how your Lord dealt with [the owners of the elephant]'. And its origin is that whoever says 'I saw so-and-so' or 'I looked at so-and-so' does not imply other than his essence; but if he says 'I saw him saying such-and-such and doing such-and-such', he does not intend by it the vision of his essence. All that is [based on] the matter of the story of Moses and this verse.
Source pages p. 683

Commentary
This passage states a methodological principle in kalam: when a word is used in a customary context or paired with a specific purpose, it is taken figuratively, not literally. Maturidi gives two Quranic examples where 'seeing' (رأى) is used not for visual perception of God's essence but for understanding His actions. He then explains the linguistic rule: when one says 'I saw so-and-so' it refers to the person's essence, but when one says 'I saw him saying/doing such-and-such' it refers to perceiving his action, not his person. This principle is then applied to the story of Moses, where the command 'You shall not see Me' (لن تراني) is interpreted in light of this rule.

Key Terms
أصل في الكلام
حقيقة
مجاز
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'seeing' Allah is always literal in the Quran, implying that human eyes can perceive Allah's essence.
Correction: This passage shows that the type of 'seeing' depends on context: when paired with an action or customary usage, it is metaphorical, referring to understanding or perceiving Allah's signs and actions, not His essence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, when is a word diverted from its literal meaning?
A) When it is a divine attribute
B) When it is used in a customary context or paired with a specific purpose
C) When it contradicts reason
D) When it appears in the Quran

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's principle establishes that context—customary usage and intended purpose—determines whether a word is taken literally or figuratively, a crucial tool for interpreting divine attributes in the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quranic verses about Allah's attributes, do you insist on a literal meaning that your mind cannot grasp, or do you trust that the customary usage of the language points you to a deeper understanding? What is at stake for your faith if you choose the wrong path?

The Necessity of Promise and Threat for Moral Motivation
Maturidi states the need for promise and threat to overcome the soul's aversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the promise of reward and threat of punishment are essential to motivate the soul to obey divine commands.

Core Idea
Without promise and threat, the soul cannot be compelled to do what it dislikes.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi opens with a psychological observation: the soul naturally flees from what it dislikes. Overcoming this aversion to obey God requires a powerful motivator. Maturidi identifies the divine promise of reward and threat of punishment as the only effective means to make obedience easy. Let's see how he builds this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how Maturidi describes the soul's aversion and the solution.
2
Identify the problem and solution
The problem is the soul's natural dislike of hardship. The solution is presenting the promise and threat. Ask yourself: why is this the only way?
Maturidi uses the phrase 'لا سبيل إلا' (no way except).
3
Reflect on the result
When the promise and threat are presented, abandoning pleasures becomes easy and bearing burdens becomes light. This is the core claim.
Consider how this relates to everyday moral motivation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجاهدة النفس وحملها على ما يكرهه الطبع، والذي يكرهه تنفر عنه النفس فلا يجد الممتحن على قهره وصرفه إلى ما يريده ويؤمر به سبيلا إلا إحضار الوعد والوعيد، حتى إذا رأى ذلك سهل عليه ترك الملاذ، وهان عليه تحمل المؤن العظام. وبعد، فإن البشر خلق خلقا قبح عليه فعل الذي لا يقصد به نفع العواقب، أو لا تتقى به ضرر العواقب، فلا بد أن يجعل لأعماله ذلك، وذلك حق الوعد والوعيد، ولولا ذلك لكان يستوي عواقب العدو والولى، وعلى ما تفاوتا هما بحيث الاختيار والإيثار، يجب تفاوت عواقبها، وبالله التوفيق. كذلك وقد أمكن أن نجعل الثواب كله فضلا إذ قد سبق من الله من النعم ما استحق الشكر عليه بعامة ما احتمل الوسع، فيكون الثواب فضلا من الله. ثم المضاعفة في ذلك كقوله تعالى: مَنْ جَاءَ بِالْحَسَنَةِ فَلَهُ عَشْرُ أَمْثَالِها وَمَنْ جَاءَ بِالسَّيِّئَةِ فَلَا يُجْزَى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا، فذكر في السيئة ما توجبه الحكمة من الجزاء وضاعف في الثواب على ما تحتمله الأفضال، إذ ذلك أصله، ولا قوة إلا بالله. فهذا فيما احتمله عقولنا مما يلزم الأمر والنهي، ما كان فيما جاء بهما الرسل عن الله دليل كاف يلزم القبول بعظم الحكمة فهما لو قصرت عقولنا عن ما في العقل إذا ترك استعماله كسائر الجوارح لم يعتدل الوقوف على ذلك، مع تعطيلها عن المنافع التي هي سببها، فمثله العقل، مع ما كان الذي ذكرت في سائر الجوارح هو حق الفعل أيضا. وإشارته، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Striving against the soul and compelling it to what it naturally dislikes, and what it dislikes the soul flees from, so the one tested finds no way to subdue it and turn it to what he wants and is commanded except by presenting the promise and the threat, until when he sees that, abandoning pleasures becomes easy for him, and bearing great burdens becomes light upon him. Moreover, humanity was created with a nature such that an action not intended for beneficial consequences or not warding off harmful consequences is considered ugly; therefore it is necessary to assign to their actions that [purpose], and that is the right of promise and threat. Were it not for that, the consequences of the enemy and the friend would be equal, and according to the difference between them in terms of choice and preference, their consequences must differ. And with God is success. Likewise, it is possible that we make all reward a favor, since from God have preceded blessings that necessitate gratitude to the extent of one's capacity, so reward becomes a favor from God. Then multiplication in that is as His saying, exalted: 'Whoever comes with a good deed shall have ten times its like, and whoever comes with an evil deed shall be recompensed only with its like.' So He mentioned in the evil deed what wisdom requires of recompense, and multiplied the reward according to the capacity of favors, for that is its origin. And there is no power except with God. This is what our intellects can grasp of what is necessitated by command and prohibition; as for what the messengers brought from God, sufficient evidence that necessitates acceptance due to the greatness of wisdom, even if our intellects fall short of that, just as the intellect if its use is neglected like other limbs does not become balanced in understanding that, along with neglecting them for the benefits for which they are the cause. So the intellect is like that, with what I have mentioned about other limbs being the reality of action. And its indication. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 700

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the necessity of reward and punishment (wa'd wa wa'id) for moral action. The author, Imam Maturidi, begins by describing the human struggle against the soul's natural aversion to hardship. He states that the only effective motivator to overcome this aversion is the presentation of divine promise and threat. When a person sees the promised reward and threatened punishment, enduring hardship becomes easy. Maturidi then grounds this in human nature: people inherently deem actions ugly if they do not lead to beneficial outcomes or avoid harmful ones. Therefore, actions must have consequences tied to their moral quality, which is precisely what promise and threat provide. Without this differentiation, the outcomes of the enemy and the friend would be indistinguishable, but since they differ in choice and preference, their consequences must differ accordingly. He then discusses reward as fundamentally a divine favor (fadl), since God's prior blessings already obligate gratitude. The multiplication of rewards for good deeds and the exact recompense for evil deeds, as stated in the Quranic verse (6:160), follow from wisdom. Finally, Maturidi relates this to the intellect: just as the senses and limbs have functions, the intellect must be used to grasp the evidence from the messengers, otherwise it becomes imbalanced. The conclusion underscores that understanding these matters requires the use of reason alongside revelation.

Key Terms
الوعد
الوعيد
مجاهدة النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the only way to subdue the soul and turn it to what is commanded?
A) Philosophical argument
B) Presenting the promise and threat
C) Force and coercion
D) Social pressure

Key Takeaway
The soul can only be motivated to obey difficult commands through the prospect of reward and fear of punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an act of obedience that your soul finds difficult, do you actively bring to mind the promise of reward and fear of punishment, or do you rely on your own strength? What does your answer reveal about your trust in Allah's words?
?
If you truly believed that every hardship endured for Allah's sake will be compensated beyond measure, would you still procrastinate or avoid that obedience? What does your hesitation tell you about the conviction of your faith?
?
Is your obedience driven more by hope of reward or fear of punishment? Which one dominates, and is that balanced according to the necessity of both promise and threat as taught in this passage? How will you adjust starting today?

Human Nature Requires Actions to Have Consequences
Maturidi argues from human nature that actions require differential consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that human nature demands that actions have different consequences based on their moral quality.

Core Idea
Actions without beneficial or harmful consequences are considered ugly by human nature.

Maturidi now turns to a deeper principle: humans inherently judge actions that have no purpose in terms of consequences as ugly. Therefore, for moral actions to be meaningful, they must lead to different outcomes. This establishes the rational basis for promise and threat beyond mere motivation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read carefully the Arabic and translation. Focus on the phrase 'فعل الذي لا يقصد به نفع العواقب'.
2
Identify the moral principle
Maturidi states that humans are created with a nature that regards as ugly any action not aimed at beneficial consequences or avoidance of harmful ones. This is a universal human intuition.
Consider if this is always true.
3
Apply to enemy and friend
He then argues that without differentiated consequences, the fates of the enemy and friend would be equal. Since they differ in choice and preference, their consequences must differ.
This is a rational argument for justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجاهدة النفس وحملها على ما يكرهه الطبع، والذي يكرهه تنفر عنه النفس فلا يجد الممتحن على قهره وصرفه إلى ما يريده ويؤمر به سبيلا إلا إحضار الوعد والوعيد، حتى إذا رأى ذلك سهل عليه ترك الملاذ، وهان عليه تحمل المؤن العظام. وبعد، فإن البشر خلق خلقا قبح عليه فعل الذي لا يقصد به نفع العواقب، أو لا تتقى به ضرر العواقب، فلا بد أن يجعل لأعماله ذلك، وذلك حق الوعد والوعيد، ولولا ذلك لكان يستوي عواقب العدو والولى، وعلى ما تفاوتا هما بحيث الاختيار والإيثار، يجب تفاوت عواقبها، وبالله التوفيق. كذلك وقد أمكن أن نجعل الثواب كله فضلا إذ قد سبق من الله من النعم ما استحق الشكر عليه بعامة ما احتمل الوسع، فيكون الثواب فضلا من الله. ثم المضاعفة في ذلك كقوله تعالى: مَنْ جَاءَ بِالْحَسَنَةِ فَلَهُ عَشْرُ أَمْثَالِها وَمَنْ جَاءَ بِالسَّيِّئَةِ فَلَا يُجْزَى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا، فذكر في السيئة ما توجبه الحكمة من الجزاء وضاعف في الثواب على ما تحتمله الأفضال، إذ ذلك أصله، ولا قوة إلا بالله. فهذا فيما احتمله عقولنا مما يلزم الأمر والنهي، ما كان فيما جاء بهما الرسل عن الله دليل كاف يلزم القبول بعظم الحكمة فهما لو قصرت عقولنا عن ما في العقل إذا ترك استعماله كسائر الجوارح لم يعتدل الوقوف على ذلك، مع تعطيلها عن المنافع التي هي سببها، فمثله العقل، مع ما كان الذي ذكرت في سائر الجوارح هو حق الفعل أيضا. وإشارته، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Striving against the soul and compelling it to what it naturally dislikes, and what it dislikes the soul flees from, so the one tested finds no way to subdue it and turn it to what he wants and is commanded except by presenting the promise and the threat, until when he sees that, abandoning pleasures becomes easy for him, and bearing great burdens becomes light upon him. Moreover, humanity was created with a nature such that an action not intended for beneficial consequences or not warding off harmful consequences is considered ugly; therefore it is necessary to assign to their actions that [purpose], and that is the right of promise and threat. Were it not for that, the consequences of the enemy and the friend would be equal, and according to the difference between them in terms of choice and preference, their consequences must differ. And with God is success. Likewise, it is possible that we make all reward a favor, since from God have preceded blessings that necessitate gratitude to the extent of one's capacity, so reward becomes a favor from God. Then multiplication in that is as His saying, exalted: 'Whoever comes with a good deed shall have ten times its like, and whoever comes with an evil deed shall be recompensed only with its like.' So He mentioned in the evil deed what wisdom requires of recompense, and multiplied the reward according to the capacity of favors, for that is its origin. And there is no power except with God. This is what our intellects can grasp of what is necessitated by command and prohibition; as for what the messengers brought from God, sufficient evidence that necessitates acceptance due to the greatness of wisdom, even if our intellects fall short of that, just as the intellect if its use is neglected like other limbs does not become balanced in understanding that, along with neglecting them for the benefits for which they are the cause. So the intellect is like that, with what I have mentioned about other limbs being the reality of action. And its indication. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 700

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the necessity of reward and punishment (wa'd wa wa'id) for moral action. The author, Imam Maturidi, begins by describing the human struggle against the soul's natural aversion to hardship. He states that the only effective motivator to overcome this aversion is the presentation of divine promise and threat. When a person sees the promised reward and threatened punishment, enduring hardship becomes easy. Maturidi then grounds this in human nature: people inherently deem actions ugly if they do not lead to beneficial outcomes or avoid harmful ones. Therefore, actions must have consequences tied to their moral quality, which is precisely what promise and threat provide. Without this differentiation, the outcomes of the enemy and the friend would be indistinguishable, but since they differ in choice and preference, their consequences must differ accordingly. He then discusses reward as fundamentally a divine favor (fadl), since God's prior blessings already obligate gratitude. The multiplication of rewards for good deeds and the exact recompense for evil deeds, as stated in the Quranic verse (6:160), follow from wisdom. Finally, Maturidi relates this to the intellect: just as the senses and limbs have functions, the intellect must be used to grasp the evidence from the messengers, otherwise it becomes imbalanced. The conclusion underscores that understanding these matters requires the use of reason alongside revelation.

Key Terms
الفعل الأخلاقي
نفع العواقب
ضرر العواقب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that reward and punishment are arbitrary divine decrees.
Correction: Maturidi shows that they are grounded in human nature and rationality.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say would happen if actions did not have differentiated consequences?
A) Everyone would be equal
B) The enemy and the friend would have equal outcomes
C) No one would act morally
D) God would be unjust

Key Takeaway
Human nature requires that moral actions lead to different consequences, justifying divine promise and threat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own choices today, do you truly believe that they carry weight and will lead to different outcomes? Or do you sometimes act as if consequences don't matter? This passage says that would be 'ugly' by your own nature. How does that sit with you?
?
Think of a person you consider an 'enemy' and a person you consider a 'friend'. Do you expect the same final outcome for both? If not, why? And does your expectation align with the justice that Maturidi argues is inherent in human nature?
?
The passage says that actions without beneficial or harmful consequences are considered ugly by human nature. Reflect on an action you did today that had no apparent consequence. Did it feel meaningless? How does that feeling support the necessity of divine promise and threat?

Reward as Divine Favor
Maturidi explains that all reward is divine favor and cites Quranic verse on multiplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers all reward to be a divine favor (fadl) and how multiplication of reward is an additional grace.

Core Idea
Reward is fundamentally a favor from God, not a debt owed.

In this section, Maturidi addresses the nature of divine reward. He argues that since God has already bestowed countless blessings that obligate gratitude, any additional reward is a favor. He then cites a Quranic verse to illustrate how God multiplies good deeds but recompenses evil deeds exactly, reflecting wisdom and grace.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the phrase 'نجعل الثواب كله فضلا'.
2
Understand the logic of favor
Maturidi says that prior blessings from God necessitate gratitude; therefore any reward beyond that is a favor, not a right.
Contrast this with the idea of earning reward.
3
Analyze the Quranic verse
He quotes the verse about ten times for good and exact recompense for evil. He explains that this multiplication is due to the capacity of divine favors.
Consider why evil is not multiplied.
4
Reflect on the implication
This shows that the system of reward and punishment is not purely retributive but infused with grace.

?

?

?

Does the exactness of divine justice terrify you, or does it drive you to cling to God's mercy as the only hope? This week, how can you actively remind yourself that even forgiveness is an unearned favor, not a right?

The Role of the Intellect in Understanding Revelation
Maturidi discusses the necessity of using intellect to understand divine evidence from messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that using the intellect is necessary to grasp the evidence from the messengers, just as other limbs must be used for their functions.

Core Idea
The intellect must be actively used to understand revelation, otherwise it becomes imbalanced.

In the final part of the passage, Maturidi connects the previous discussions to the role of the intellect. He asserts that what has been said is within the grasp of the intellect regarding command and prohibition. But when it comes to the messengers' reports, even if the intellect falls short, the evidence is sufficient. He draws an analogy: just as neglecting to use other limbs leads to imbalance, neglecting the intellect prevents proper understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Look for the analogy of the intellect with other limbs.
2
Understand the scope of the intellect
Maturidi distinguishes between what the intellect can grasp (the rational basis for command) and what the messengers bring (which may exceed the intellect but has sufficient evidence).
3
Analyze the analogy
He compares the intellect to other limbs: if not used, it cannot function properly. Similarly, the intellect must be exercised to understand the messengers' evidence.
This emphasizes the duty to use reason in theology.
4
Connect to the overall argument
This supports the necessity of both reason and revelation in moral theology.

Source Text
