Knowing God Through the Hidden Self
Maturidi evaluates the saying about knowing God through the hidden self.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's assessment of the saying that knowing one's hidden self leads to knowing God, and how causal reasoning plays a role in knowing the Creator.

Core Idea
Knowing God through reflection on causes is sufficient to know the Maker.

Imam Maturidi encounters a popular saying that claims self-knowledge leads to knowledge of God. He carefully explains what the 'hidden self' truly refers to—not mere introspection but a capacity for causal reasoning and reflection. In this lesson, we will see how Maturidi affirms this method while clarifying its proper scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the saying and its definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the exact wording of the people's claim and their definition of 'hidden self'. Note the key terms.
Pay attention to the terms 'الكيان', 'تدبير الخلائق', and 'الفكر والنظر'.
2
Identify Maturidi's evaluation
After understanding the definition, notice that Maturidi says 'what they said is good' and then explains how this method suffices for knowing God. Look for the condition or limitation he mentions.
He says there may be sufficiency 'عن درك ما خفي من أحواله'.
3
Reflect on the role of causes
Maturidi emphasizes that the hidden self operates through thought (fikr) and reflection (nazar) on causes (asbab). Consider why causal reasoning is central to this methodology and what it implies about how we know God.
This connects to the broader kalam method of argument from contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم: من عرف نفسه الخفية عرف ربه، ونفسه الخفية هي الكيان المجعول لصلاح الأمور واحتمال المعالي، وملك تدبير الخلائق ودرك الخفيات من الأمور بالفكر والنظر في الأسباب.
وما قالوه حسن، وقد يقع بما ذكرت في معرفة الصانع كفاية عن درك ما خفي من أحواله، ووصل إلى العلم بما استتر، وظهر بالأسباب، وبه يُعرف ما خفى منه، سمي نفسًا أم لا، وظهر، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And some people said: 'Whoever knows his hidden self knows his Lord, and his hidden self is the entity made for the soundness of affairs and the assumption of high matters, and the possession of the management of creatures and the perception of hidden things through thought and reflection on causes.'
And what they said is good, and by what I mentioned there may be, in knowing the Maker, sufficiency from perceiving what is hidden of His states, and one arrives at knowledge of what is concealed, and it appears through causes, and by it what is hidden from Him is known, whether it be called self or not, and it appears, and there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 702

Commentary
This passage presents and evaluates a saying attributed to some people: 'Whoever knows his hidden self knows his Lord.' The author first explains what they mean by 'hidden self'—it is the inner essence created to manage affairs, assume high aims, govern creation, and perceive hidden realities through thought and causal reasoning. Imam Maturidi then gives his assessment: this saying is good, and the method described (reflection on causes) can indeed provide sufficient knowledge of the Maker without needing to perceive the hidden aspects of God directly. By examining external causes and effects, one arrives at knowledge of what is concealed, and thus God is known. The author leaves open whether this inner faculty is called 'self' or not; the essential point is that it serves as a means to know God.

Key Terms

الكيان
تدبير الخلائق
الفكر والنظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That knowing one's hidden self is purely introspective or mystical, without need for reasoning on causes.
Correction: Maturidi explains that the 'hidden self' is defined as the entity that perceives hidden things through thought and reflection on causes, thus causal reasoning is essential.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Maturidi, what is the 'hidden self' (النفس الخفية) as understood by the group cited?
A) A mystical intuition separate from reason
B) The entity created for managing affairs and perceiving hidden things through thought and causal reflection
C) The physical body
D) The soul after death

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms that knowledge of God is attainable through reflection on causes, which is the function of the hidden self.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If causal reasoning is sufficient to know the Maker, then why do you still struggle with doubts about God when you see cause and effect in your life? Would you commit to a daily habit of reflecting on one cause to strengthen your certainty?
?
Your hidden self is meant to perceive 'hidden things' through thought. What hidden realities about God have you neglected to uncover because you rely on secondhand knowledge rather than your own reflection? Will you begin today to use this faculty?

The First Source of Doubt: Taqlid
Maturidi identifies the first source of doubt as blind following.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi identifies blind following (taqlid) as a primary obstacle to accepting the proofs of tawheed.

Core Idea
Blind following blocks reflection on divine proofs.

Maturidi begins his analysis of doubt by pinpointing taqlid—the unthinking acceptance of others' beliefs. He argues that this habit turns people away from reflecting on the clear evidences for tawheed and leads them to follow their desires instead.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms related to taqlid.
The passage is from a high-OCR source, so some words are unclear.
2
Identify the components of taqlid
Look for phrases that describe what taqlid involves: whom they follow, what they abandon, and what they turn to.
The text mentions 'ترك التفكر في الأدلة' (abandoning reflection on evidences).
3
Understand the consequence
Reflect on the result of taqlid as described: leading to misery ('أسباب الشقاء').
This shows the gravity of blind following in matters of creed.
4
Connect to your own context
Consider how blind following might manifest today and why it is a barrier to true faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرأيت الشبهة اعترضت البشر من أوجه ثلاثة: أحدها التقليد بمن ألفت نفسه الشبهة لمن تصبح إليه، فترك التفكر في الأدلة، وأقبل على أماني النفس صحبتهم والوصول ثقة إلى شهوات النفس، أو اتهاما لآرائهم أن تهى بهم إو إنمامهم وغيره من أسباب الشقاء حتى يبلغ بهم العياذ بشره النف. عاداتها. نشا والثاني نظر إلى الوجود مما يقع تحت الحواس فوجده يتقلب من حال بالمواد والأغذية، وتولد بعض عن بعض، وظنوا أن كون الأشياء شيء، والفروع لا عن أصل محال وجوده؛ لأنهم لم يعاينوا ذلك، الشاه هو دليل الغائب. ثم تفرقوا: فمنهم من يقول: على هذا أمر العالم في...
Then I saw that doubt has intervened upon mankind from three aspects: one of them is taqlid (blind following) of one whose soul has become accustomed to doubt, whoever seeks him, so he leaves reflection upon the evidences, and turns to the wishes of the soul in their companionship and attaining confidently to the desires of the soul, or suspicion of their opinions that they mislead them or complete them and other causes of misery until it leads them to refuge from the evil of the souls. Their habits. Grew. And the second is looking at existence through what falls under the senses, and he found it changing from state through materials and nutrients, and the generation of some from others, and they thought that the existence of things something, and branches without a root are impossible in existence; because they did not witness that, the witness is the evidence of the unseen. Then they diverged: among them are those who say: upon this, the affair of the world is in...
Source pages p. 710

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the three sources of doubt (shubuhat) that prevent people from accepting the proofs of tawheed. The author, after presenting the rational proofs, turns to address the obstacles. The first source is taqlid (blind following): people become accustomed to following someone who is himself doubtful, so they abandon intellectual reflection on the evidence and instead pursue their desires and the company of others, leading to misery. The second source is over-reliance on sensory perception: people observe the constant change in the material world—things transforming through nutrition and generation—and mistakenly conclude that all things arise from other things without an ultimate origin, because they have not witnessed the first creation. They incorrectly assume that what is not witnessed cannot exist, thereby denying the unseen Creator. The author notes that this leads to further divergence among people. The third source of doubt is not fully preserved in the text (it cuts off), but it is implied by the enumeration of three aspects.

Key Terms
الشبهة
التقليد
التفكر
أماني النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that following scholars is always beneficial, but Maturidi warns against blind following that abandons reflection.
Correction: Taqlid in matters of creed is a source of doubt because it prevents one from using their intellect to examine the evidence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the first source of doubt that prevents people from reflecting on the evidences?
A) Sensory perception
B) Taqlid (blind following)
C) Philosophical sophistry
D) The complexity of the world

Key Takeaway
Taqlid is the first obstacle to tawheed because it replaces reasoned reflection with unexamined habit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which area of your life are you blindly following without reflection? What proof from this lesson demands that you stop?
?
Maturidi says taqlid leads you to follow your desires. What desire in your heart is preventing you from seeking the truth about Allah?
?
Are you more attached to your habits than to the evidence for tawheed? What will you change today to break free from that attachment?

The Second Source of Doubt: Over-reliance on the Senses
Maturidi identifies the second source of doubt as over-reliance on the senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi critiques the over-reliance on sensory observation as a cause of disbelief in the unseen Creator.

Core Idea
Sensory change can mislead if taken as the whole truth.

The second source of doubt arises from an uncritical trust in sensory experience. People observe the constant transformation in the material world and mistakenly conclude that everything must arise from pre-existing matter, denying the possibility of creation from nothing. Maturidi corrects this by affirming that the sensory world points to the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the progression from observation to false conclusion.
The text cuts off at the end, but the main argument is complete.
2
Identify the key terms
Look for terms like 'الحواس', 'المواد والأغذية', 'تولد بعض عن بعض', and 'دليل الغائب'.
3
Understand the false conclusion
People observe that things change and are generated from other things. They conclude that everything must have a material origin, denying creation ex nihilo.
The text says: 'وظنوا أن كون الأشياء شيء، والفروع لا عن أصل محال وجوده'.
4
Connect to the principle of dalil al-gha'ib
The author states: 'الشاه هو دليل الغائب' meaning the witness (observed world) is evidence for the unseen (Creator). Realize how this refutes the doubt.

?

Al-Maturidi's Refutation: The Deficiency of Intellects
Maturidi responds to the previous views, highlighting their inadequacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Maturidi refutes deviant theological views by pointing to the deficiency of human intellects in grasping divine wisdom.

Core Idea
Human intellect cannot fathom divine wisdom, let alone debate it.

Imam al-Maturidi concludes his discussion of deviant sects with a powerful reminder. He argues that if these groups had truly reflected on the proofs already presented, they would have recognized their own intellectual limitations. This lesson examines that critique and its implications for theological methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the tone—it is a direct refutation.
2
Identify the core argument
What does Maturidi say these groups would have realized if they had reflected properly?
The answer relates to intellectual deficiency and inability to grasp even human wisdom.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage ends by noting that their own choices prevent them from making claims. How does this seal the refutation?
The point is that their own flawed reasoning invalidates their position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : ولو أنعم هؤلاء الفرق النظر فيما تقدم من ذكر الأدلة لعلموا قصور عقولهم وبكم عن الوقوف على الحكمة البشرية فضلا عن أن يحيدلوا بحكمة الربوبية ، مع ما فيها إليه صاروا في الاختيار منع لهم عن دعوى
The jurist Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: If these factions had deeply considered the mention of the proofs earlier, they would have known the deficiency of their intellects and their muteness regarding grasping human wisdom, let alone that they should debate the wisdom of divinity, with what in it they came to in choice, preventing them from the claim.
Source pages p. 712

Commentary
This passage is the author's direct response to all the previously mentioned deviant views. Imam al-Maturidi (referred to as 'al-faqih Abu Mansur') states that if these groups had truly reflected on the proofs that were presented earlier in the book (the previous evidence for tawhid), they would have realized the inadequacy of their own intellects. They are unable to comprehend even human wisdom fully, let alone the wisdom of divine lordship. The author also notes that their own choices in adopting these views actually prevent them from making any valid claim (to truth). This serves as a conclusive rebuttal, emphasizing that the fault lies in their intellectual limitations and not in the proofs themselves.

Key Terms
فقيه (jurist)
أدلة (proofs)
قصور عقول (deficiency of intellects)
حكمة ربوبية (divine wisdom)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that al-Maturidi is dismissing all rational inquiry.
Correction: He is only criticizing the flawed reasoning of these specific groups for not properly applying reason to the proofs.

Checkpoint

A) They lack intelligence altogether
B) They did not deeply consider the earlier proofs
C) The proofs themselves are insufficient
D) God has sealed their hearts

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi's refutation hinges on the notion that deviant groups did not properly reflect on the proofs, leading to intellectual deficiency and self-defeating claims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Reflect on one area of your faith where you have not deeply engaged with the scriptural or rational proofs. What would it take for you to study that area with the humility that acknowledges the deficiency of your intellect?

Human Agreement and Corruption of Tawhid
Maturidi states that all humans acknowledge tawhid in general but corrupt it in detail.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's claim that all humans initially affirm tawhid in general but most corrupt it through interpretation, and the exception of a group from the people of Islam.

Core Idea
All humans start with tawhid, but most corrupt it through interpretation.

Maturidi begins his discussion of sects with a striking thesis: every human who reflects on tawhid initially accepts it in a general sense. However, each group then nullifies that acceptance through their specific interpretations, except one group from the people of Islam. This passage forms the foundation for Maturidi's critique of deviant sects.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the key terms: 'in general' (في الجملة) and 'interpretation' (التفسير).
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the general affirmation
Maturidi says all humans who have 'consideration for tawhid' give it in general. This means they accept the oneness of God in a broad, undetailed sense. Think about what 'in general' means here – it is not yet specified.
This sets up a contrast with the detail that comes later.
3
Identify the exception
Note the exception: 'except a group from the people of Islam who adhered to what the entirety gave them.' This group holds on to the general affirmation without nullifying it through interpretation.
This likely refers to the true followers of the Islamic creed.
4
Reflect on the implication
This thesis implies that deviation from true tawhid occurs not from outright rejection but from misinterpreting the initially accepted general belief. Maturidi will later critique various sects for this reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : أعطى جميع البشر ممن له نظر التوحيد في الجملة، ثم نقض كل فريق منهم ما أعطى في الجملة بالتفسير إلا فريق من أهل الإسلام لزموا ما أعطاهم الجميع.
Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: All humans who have consideration for tawhid gave it in general, then every group among them nullified what they gave in general by interpretation, except a group from the people of Islam who adhered to what the entirety gave them.
Source pages p. 718

Commentary
This passage presents a thesis from Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. He states that all people who reflect on tawhid (the oneness of God) initially affirm it in a general, undetailed sense. However, each group then proceeds to nullify that general affirmation through their specific interpretations. The only exception is a group from the people of Islam ('ahl al-Islam) who remained faithful to the general affirmation that all humans originally held. This sets the stage for the subsequent critiques of various sects.

Key Terms
التوحيد
الجملة
التفسير
نقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'group from the people of Islam' refers to a specific sect like the Ash'aris or Salafis.
Correction: It likely refers to the true followers of the Islamic creed who hold to the general affirmation without corrupting it through interpretation.

B) They gave tawhid in general
C) They gave tawhid in detail
D) They argued over tawhid

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's thesis: all humans initially affirm tawhid in general, but most nullify it through their particular interpretations, except the true adherents of Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The World as the Sole Path to Knowing God
Maturidi states the only path to know God is through the world's indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's foundational epistemological principle that knowledge of God is attained exclusively through rational inference from the created world, not through direct sensory or revelatory means.

Core Idea
The world is the only path to know God.

This lesson presents the cornerstone of Maturidi epistemology. Imam Maturidi argues that direct access to God is impossible; instead, we must reason from the world's existence. This passage establishes the method of theological reasoning based on contingent creation pointing to a necessary Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without translating. Then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms like 'أصل', 'دلالة', 'شاهد'.
The passage is dense but each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the central principle
The passage begins with 'والأصل' – what is the principle? It states that knowledge of God is only possible through the world's indication. Underline this claim.
3
Understand the negation of other ways
Maturidi specifically excludes the senses (حواس) and revelation (سمع) as direct means. Why? Because God is not an object of sense nor directly heard in revelation without inference.
This sets kalam apart from empirical or fideistic approaches.
4
Analyze how the world indicates God
The world testifies to God 'from the aspect of witnessing to Him with the attribute, not the essence.' Consider what this means: we know God's attributes through creation, not His essence directly.
The evidence given is 'existence after non-existence' – this points to a Creator.
5
Reflect on the proof from contingency
The passage ends with 'for existence after non-existence is the evidence of bringing into existence and originating.' This is the core argument: contingent things need a cause.
This is a concise statement of the contingency argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والأصل أن الله تعالى إذ لا سبيل إلى العلم به إلا من طريق دلالة العالم عليه ، بانقطاع وجوه الوصول إلى معرفته من طريق الحواس عليه أو شهادة السمع ، ثم الشاهد يدل عليه من وجه الشهادة له بالصفة لا من وجه الشهادة بالذات ؛ إذ الوجود بعد أن لم يكن هو دليل الإيجاد والإحداث
Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: The principle is that God, exalted be He, since there is no way to knowledge of Him except through the indication of the world upon Him, due to the cessation of the means of arriving at knowledge of Him through the way of the senses or the testimony of hearing. Then the witnessed [world] indicates Him from the aspect of witnessing to Him with the attribute, not from the aspect of witnessing to the essence; for existence after non-existence is the evidence of bringing into existence and originating.
Source pages p. 728

Commentary
This passage is a foundational epistemological principle by Imam Maturidi. He states that knowledge of God can only be attained through the world's indication (dalalat al-'alam). He explains that the senses and revelation (the testimony of hearing) cannot directly access God. The world (the shahid, witnessed) testifies to God in terms of His attributes (ṣifa), not His essence (dhat). The proof is that things exist after not existing, which points to a Creator who brings into existence and originates. This passage sets up the method of kalam: arguing from the contingent world to the necessary existence of God and His attributes.

Key Terms
أصل
دلالة
عالم
شاهد
ذات
صفة
إيجاد
إحداث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that direct sense experience or revelation alone suffices for knowledge of God.
Correction: Maturidi emphasizes the necessity of inference from the world; senses and revelation are not direct channels.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the only way to attain knowledge of God?
A) Through direct sensory perception.
B) Through the testimony of revelation alone.
C) Through the indication of the world on Him.
D) Through intellectual intuition without any external means.

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi establishes that knowledge of God is only possible through rational inference from the contingent world, not through direct sensory or revelatory means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often say you 'feel God in your heart' or 'see God in nature'. But Maturidi insists that the world only indicates God's attributes, not His essence. Have you ever mistaken your emotional or sensory experience as direct knowledge of God? What does this lesson demand you change about how you seek knowledge of God?
?
If the only path to knowledge of God is through rational inference from the world, how seriously have you taken the study of that rational proof? Have you ever actually worked through the argument from contingency to strengthen your certainty? If not, what is stopping you from doing that today?
?
The passage says the senses and revelation are cut off from directly accessing God. This means even the Quran is not a direct view of God, but a sign pointing to Him. How does that affect your relationship with scripture? Do you read the Quran to 'see God' or to infer His attributes? What shift in your intention does this require?

Answering Obstinate Questions: Discipline Over Proofs
Maturidi defines obstinate questions and their proper response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi prescribes discipline rather than rational proofs for responding to obstinate theological questions.

Core Idea
Some questions aren't asked for knowledge but for contention — answer with deterrence.

Not every theological question deserves a detailed proof. Imam Maturidi identifies a category of questions asked purely out of obstinacy (ta'annut). In this passage, he explains that the correct response to such questions is not to engage in argumentation but to discipline the questioner in a way that deters further obstinacy. This practical teaching protects the integrity of theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice how Maturidi defines the category and then gives the rule for responding.
The passage is short but dense; pay attention to the key terms.
2
Identify the two parts of the ruling
First, Maturidi describes a type of question: questions that have no meaning except as obstinacy. Second, he prescribes the response: discipline (ta'dib) that prevents the behavior, not proofs (istidlal).
This is a clear categorical statement.
3
Understand the rationale
Ask yourself: why would proofs be inappropriate here? The commentary explains that engaging with obstinate questions gives them undeserved legitimacy. The goal is to stop the obstinacy, not to convince.
Connects to the learning objective.
4
Reflect on application
Think of a real or hypothetical scenario where someone asks a question not to learn but to argue. How would Maturidi's advice apply?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سأل نفسه عن : أشياء لا معنى للسؤال عنها إلا عن التعنت ، وحق جواب شأن التعنت التأديب بما يمنعه ، لا الاستدلال بالأدلة على نحو ما بينا من
Then he asked himself about things about which there is no meaning to ask except out of obstinacy. And the proper answer for the case of obstinacy is discipline that prevents it, not the adducing of proofs, in the manner we have explained from
Source pages p. 729

Commentary
This brief passage defines a category of questions that are asked not for knowledge but out of obstinacy ('anad). Maturidi states that the appropriate response to such questions is not to engage in proof-giving (istidlal) but to discipline the questioner in a way that stops the obstinacy. This reflects a practical theological method: not all inquiries deserve a detailed kalam response.

تأديب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think all theological questions should be answered with proofs, regardless of intent.
Correction: Maturidi teaches that obstinate questions do not deserve proofs; they require discipline that stops the behavior.

Checkpoint

A) By providing detailed proofs
B) By ignoring the questioner
C) By discipline that prevents the obstinacy

Key Takeaway
Not every question deserves a proof; obstinate questions call for discipline, not debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a recent disagreement where you kept offering proofs but the other person remained unconvinced. Could it be that their question was not for knowledge but for obstinacy? Are you willing to practice discipline—to stop arguing—even if it feels like losing the debate? What will you do the next time you sense obstinacy?
?
When a doubt arises in your own heart that you nurse rather than genuinely seek to resolve, are you behaving like an obstinate questioner toward yourself? What discipline would deter that inner ta'annut? How will you cut off that train of thought today?
?
Is there a relationship where you are known for always having an answer, and the other person hides defiance behind questions? Will you have the courage to withhold proofs and instead apply the discipline of silence or redirection? Make a plan for the next conversation with that person.

Why Abandoning Speculation is Not Safer
Maturidi presents and refutes the objection that abandoning speculation is safer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers speculation an intellectual necessity, refuting the claim that abandoning it is safer.

Core Idea
The intellect itself, not a prior act, makes speculation obligatory.

Some argue that not engaging in theological speculation is safer because you avoid error. But Maturidi turns this on its head: the very faculties that make us human demand that we seek truth. In this passage, he shows that the intellect, not a prior act of speculation, obligates us to investigate, and that human desires for pleasure and fear of pain make inquiry inevitable.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and rebuttal
Read the full Arabic passage and translation carefully. Identify the initial claim (abandoning speculation is safer) and then the author's rebuttal.
Notice the structure: objection then answer.
2
Identify the role of the intellect
Find where Maturidi states that the obligation to speculate arises not from prior speculation but from the intellect itself. Understand why he says this makes speculation necessary.
The intellect distinguishes good from bad and compels seeking benefit.
3
Examine human nature as proof
Look at how Maturidi describes human desires (pleasure, survival, fear of pain) and argues that these drives force one to investigate what is beneficial and harmful.
This practical necessity shows speculation cannot be avoided.
4
Conclude why the safer claim fails
Reflect on why abandoning speculation would lead to 'inevitable ruin' according to Maturidi. Understand that ignorance does not provide safety.
The intellect's compulsion makes non-speculation impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال قوم : ترك النظر أسلم ؛ لما لا يأمن الناظر بالظفر بالحق ، ثم فيه فتح باب الحجة على نفسه مما لو امتنع ليأمن العطب من حيث لولا هو لم يتخلله عنه السبيل الذي يظنه أو الباطل ليلزمه حجة الله ؛ إذ بالفكر والبحث إرادة ما يضطر إلى العلم بأن الحق فيما انكشف له ، مع اشتباه خاطر الرحمن في الأمر والتحذير من خاطر الشيطان. وفي ترك النظر والبحث أمن ذلك ؛ إذ لم ينكشف له ما يلزمه التمييز ، ولا يخطر بذهنه ما يبعثه على الطلب ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ومن ألزم النظر والبحث فيقول : في تركه عطبه لا محالة ؛ لأن لزوم النظر ليس عقيب نظر تقدمه بل عقيب الذي به يقع النظر والبحث وهو العقل الذي به يعرف المحاسن والمساوئ ، وبه يعلم فضله على سائر الحيوان ، وبه يعرف ملك تدبير أمر الأنام ، مع ما يأتي عليه ذهنه وفطنته تبدده وفناه بما نال لذة الحياة ، وركبت فيه شهوة البقاء وعظيم ألم أسباب الفناء ، لو اعترضه فلا بد من البحث في درك ما يلائمه ويبقى ألذ الأشياء وأشهاها عنده مما يأبى عقله المخاطرة بروحه في الامتحان بالأشياء دون تكلف ما يطلعه على الغايات منها فيتقيه ، والنافع من ذلك فيجتلبه إما بالبحث عن تعرف من يثق بخبره وبأمر خيانته فما يدله عليه ، فيصدر في كل ذلك عن رأيه ، أو أن يجهد في الامتحان بنفسه بالقليل الذي من الملاذ ، يريه عاقبته مما يؤمن عن مثله المالك لقلته، فيكون في الأمرين جميعا لزوم البحث . ما يدفعه جهله بما جبل عليه من الشهوات، مما يبعثه عليه نفسه عما يصيبه من المكروه وما يحل به من الألم، إلى النظر في حال نفسه إنه بما صار كذلك، أو هل كان كذلك في الأبد ؟ أو أي وجه صار كذلك ؟ لا يسلم من بعض الخواطر التي تمنعه عن ترك النظر في أحوال نفسه ليعرف به مباديه ما لا بد من أن يعلم أنه لذلك بنفسه أو بمن له في نفسه تدبير . مع يعرف ما به صلاحه وفساده، وما عليه من النعم وعنه من الدفاع، وفي كل ذلك اضطرار إلى النظر ولزوم الحجة وبالله التوفيق
Some people said: Abandoning speculation is safer; because the speculator is not secure in attaining the truth, and it opens the door of argument against himself, whereas if he refrained, he would be safe from ruin, without encountering the path he supposes or falsehood and thus being obligated by the proof of God. For through thought and research there is an impulse necessitating knowledge that the truth is in what is revealed to him, along with confusion between the inspiration of the Merciful and the warning against the whisper of Satan. And in abandoning speculation and research there is safety from that; since what necessitates discrimination is not revealed to him, nor does anything occur to his mind that impels him to seek. And there is no power except with Allah. And one who necessitates speculation and research says: In abandoning it there is ruin inevitably; because the obligation of speculation does not follow from a prior speculation, but rather from that by which speculation and research occur, namely the intellect, by which good and bad are known, and by which he knows his superiority over other animals, and by which he knows the king who manages the affairs of mankind, along with what his mind and intelligence bring him, dissipating his mortality and his finitude, through what he obtains of the pleasure of life, and the desire for survival and the great pain of the causes of annihilation are ingrained in him. If this confronts him, he must search to grasp what suits him and what remains as the most delightful and desirable things to him, what his intellect forbids him from risking his soul in testing things without effort to reveal their purposes so he can guard against them, and the beneficial ones he seeks – either by seeking knowledge from one whose report he trusts and whose trustworthiness he knows, so that their guidance is enough for him, or by exerting himself in testing himself with the little that is of pleasures, showing him its consequence from which one is secure from the like of the possessor of few [means]. So in both cases, the necessity of search remains. What drives his ignorance due to what he was created upon of desires, urges him from what afflicts him of dislike and what befalls him of pain, to reflection on his own state: how he became thus, or whether he was always thus in eternity, or from which aspect he became thus. He is not safe from some thoughts that prevent him from abandoning reflection on his states to know their beginnings and that he must know that he is so by himself or by one who has disposal over him. Along with knowing what constitutes his well-being and corruption, and the blessings upon him and the defense on his behalf. And in all that, there is necessity for speculation and obligation of proof. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 734

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. First, some people claim that abandoning speculation (nazar) is safer because the speculator may fail to attain truth and thus open himself to arguments against religion, whereas the non-speculator avoids this risk. The author then rebuts this: abandoning speculation leads to certain ruin. The reason is that the obligation to speculate does not arise from a prior act of speculation, but from the intellect itself, which compels humans to seek what benefits and harms them. The author describes human nature: love of pleasure, desire for survival, fear of pain. Given these, one must inevitably search for what is conducive to lasting pleasure and protection from harm. The speculator either relies on trustworthy reports or tests things personally. In both cases, speculation is necessary. Moreover, due to innate desires, every person is driven to reflect on their own origin and well-being, and to distinguish good from bad. Thus, speculation is unavoidable, and the claim that it is safer to abandon it is false.

Key Terms
نظر (speculation)
عقل (intellect)
حجة (proof)
عطب (ruin)
طلب (seeking)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Not speculating about God and religion avoids responsibility and is safer.
Correction: The intellect itself obligates speculation, and human nature demands it; ignoring this leads to ruin.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does abandoning speculation lead to inevitable ruin?
A) Because it is a sin.
B) Because the intellect compels seeking benefit and harm.
C) Because one cannot know God without it.
D) Because it is forbidden.

Key Takeaway
Speculation is an intellectual and existential necessity, not a voluntary choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you avoiding deep questioning about your faith because it feels safer to stay where you are? What would it cost you to continue avoiding intellectual inquiry, knowing that your intellect already demands it?
?
The author says even trusting a scholar requires speculation. Is your trust in religious authorities based on careful reflection or on convenience? Are you willing to examine the basis of your taqlid today?
?
Think of something you desire—a pleasure or ambition—that might distract you from seeking truth. How is your innate fear of loss or desire for permanence actually calling you to reflect on your origin and purpose? Will you answer that call or ignore it?

The Call to Abandon Speculation is a Satanic Whisper
Maturidi argues that the call to abandon speculation is satanic, with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the suggestion to abandon intellectual speculation is a satanic plot to prevent humans from fulfilling their intellectual trust and attaining knowledge of the Creator.

Core Idea
Abandoning speculation is not piety; it is a satanic whisper.

Imam Maturidi presents a powerful argument: the very call to stop using one’s intellect is a whisper from Satan. He shows that such a call distracts the believer from the fruit of the intellect and the trust that leads to success. The evidence is clear: rational reflection on creation diverts from base desires, so Satan tries to block it.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Absorb the flow and key terms. Then read the English translation to understand the argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'خاطر الشيطان' (whisper of Satan).
2
Identify the claim and evidence
Identify the author's claim: the call to abandon speculation is known with certainty to be from Satan. Then find the evidence: using the intellect to reflect distracts from desires, so Satan tries to stop it.
The evidence is a logical inference, not a direct quote from revelation.
3
Reflect on the implication
Think about how this applies to your own life. Have you ever felt a desire to stop thinking deeply about faith? This passage helps you recognize that as a possible satanic whisper.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما يعلم بيقين أن هذا الذي سيل له ترك النظر هو خاطر الشيطان ؛ إذ ذلك عمله ليصده عن ثمرة عقله ويفزعه لأمانته التي لديها ينال الفرصة ويظفر بالبغية، دليل ذلك أن استعمال العقل بالفكر بالأشياء ليعرف ما استتر منها من المبادئ والنهايات ، ثم فيما يدله على حدثها ومحدثها يشغله عن شهوات النفس ليعلم أن ذلك هو صنيع الشيطان .
Along with what is known with certainty that this which he [the one saying] 'abandon speculation' is a whisper of Satan; for that is his work to turn him away from the fruit of his intellect and to frighten him from his trust by which he attains opportunity and achieves the goal. The evidence for that is that using the intellect by thinking about things to know what is hidden of beginnings and ends, then what guides him to their temporality and their originator, distracts him from the desires of the soul, so he knows that this is the work of Satan.
Source pages p. 735

Commentary
This passage provides an additional proof that the call to abandon speculation is from Satan. The author states it is known with certainty that such a call is a satanic whisper (khātir al-shayṭān). Satan's aim is to prevent the person from attaining the fruit of his intellect (thamaratu 'aqlihi) and to frighten him from fulfilling the trust (amāna) that leads to success and the attainment of his goal. The evidence is that when one uses the intellect to reflect on things, to know their hidden beginnings and ends, and especially to realize their temporality (ḥadath) and that they have a Creator (muḥdith), this distracts him from base desires. Therefore, the one who tries to turn him away from this reflection is serving Satan's purpose.

Correction: The author argues that such abandonment is actually a satanic trap to prevent one from using the intellect to know the Creator and to fulfill the trust.

Key Takeaway
The call to stop using the intellect for theological reflection is a satanic whisper, as it diverts from fulfilling the trust of reason.

The Three Beneficial Outcomes of Speculation
Maturidi enumerates three outcomes of speculation, all beneficial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's argument that theological speculation (nazar) always yields a benefit, regardless of the outcome, because it either leads to guidance, temporary worldly pleasure, or peace from anxiety.

Core Idea
Speculation never fails to benefit; every outcome is a profit.

Imam Maturidi, in this passage, makes a remarkable claim: engaging in theological speculation is never a loss. He enumerates three possible outcomes, each carrying a distinct benefit. Before you read his words, consider his initial analogy about the proper use of limbs—just as we must use our limbs for benefit, so too must we use our intellect. Now, prepare to see how speculation always yields profit, whether in guidance, worldly enjoyment, or peace of heart.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the confident tone. Maturidi uses an analogy about limbs to introduce the importance of using the intellect properly.
Pay attention to the phrase 'خصال ثلاثة'—three qualities/outcomes.
2
Identify the three outcomes
After the introduction, Maturidi lists three outcomes. Try to locate each one in the Arabic. The first leads to happiness, the second to punishment, the third to peace.
The outcomes are marked by 'إما أن... أو... أو'.
3
Understand why each outcome is beneficial
The commentary explains that even the second and third outcomes have benefits: the second enjoys worldly pleasures, the third gains relief from fear. Reflect on how this shows speculation is never harmful.
4
Apply the lesson to your own thinking
Consider how this principle applies today: when we engage in deep theological questions, we always gain something. Even if we don't reach certainty, we benefit from the search.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنه إذ لم يجز إعمال شيء من الجوارح عن المنافع التي جعلت فيها ، ولا كفها عن أعمالها بها ، بل يجب كفها عن الوجوه الضارة واستعمالها بالوجوه النافعة ، فالعقل والنظر اللذان بهما تعرف المنافع والمضار أحق أن لا يتهاونا بهما . وما كان الناظر عند ورود المخاطر لا يعدو خصالا ثلاثة : إما أن ينضى به نظره إلى العلم بحدثه وأن له محدثا يجزيه بالإحسان ويعاقبه بالإساءة، فيجتنب ما يسخطه ويقبل على ما يرضيه فيسعد وينال شرف الدارين ، أو يفضى به إلى نفي ما ذكرنا فيتمتع بصنوف اللذات ، أما العقاب فينتظره في الآخرة ، أو يفضى به إلى العلم باستغلاق باب العلم بحقيقة ما دعي إليه ، فيستريح قلبه ويزول عنه الوجل الذي يعتريه إذا فزعته الخواطر ، فيعلم إذا أنصف أنه على ربح في نظره من كل وجه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Moreover, since it is not permissible to use any of the limbs for the benefits for which they were created, nor to refrain them from their functions with it, but rather one must restrain them from harmful uses and employ them for beneficial purposes, then the intellect and speculation by which benefits and harms are known are more entitled that they not be neglected. And the one who speculates when facing dangers does not exceed three qualities: either his speculation leads him to knowledge of his temporality and that he has an Originator who rewards for good and punishes for evil, so he avoids what displeases Him and pursues what pleases Him, thus he becomes happy and attains the honor of the two abodes; or it leads him to deny what we have mentioned, so he enjoys various pleasures, but punishment awaits him in the Hereafter; or it leads him to knowledge that the door of knowledge of the reality of what he was called to is closed, so his heart rests and the fear that used to overcome him when thoughts frightened him disappears, so he knows, if he is fair, that he is profiting from his speculation in every way. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 735

Commentary
This passage is a logical enumeration of the possible outcomes of engaging in theological speculation. The author first establishes an analogy: just as we must use our bodily limbs for their intended benefits and avoid harmful uses, so too must we use our intellect and speculation for their proper purpose—discerning benefit and harm. Then he states that a person who speculates when facing the dangers of theological questions can only arrive at one of three outcomes. (1) The best outcome: he gains certain knowledge of his own temporality (ḥadath) and that he has a Creator (muḥdith) who rewards and punishes, leading him to piety and happiness in this world and the next. (2) A negative outcome: he denies these truths and indulges in worldly pleasures, but punishment awaits in the Hereafter—still, he has enjoyed temporary benefit. (3) An agnostic outcome: he concludes that the reality is unknowable, his heart is at peace, and he is freed from the fear that once troubled him. In all three cases, the speculator profits: either by eternal salvation, temporary pleasure, or relief from anxiety. Thus speculation is never harmful.

Key Terms
جوارح (limbs)
نظر (speculation)
خصال ثلاثة (three qualities)
حدث (temporality)
محدث (originator)
شرف الدارين (honor of the two abodes)
استغلاق باب العلم (closing of the door of knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think theological speculation either leads to guidance or misguidance, with no middle ground.
Correction: Maturidi shows there is a third outcome: agnosticism, which brings peace of heart. Moreover, even denial has a temporary benefit, so speculation is always profitable.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the third possible outcome of theological speculation?
A) He achieves paradise.
B) He becomes an atheist.
C) He finds peace in the closed door of knowledge.
D) He continues to doubt.

Key Takeaway
Speculation (nazar) never fails to benefit: it either leads to salvation, worldly pleasure, or peace from uncertainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these three outcomes do you most closely identify with when you ponder the deepest questions of existence? What does that reveal about your current spiritual state?
?
If you found yourself in the third outcome—convinced that the door to knowledge is closed—would you still consider yourself on a profit? Why or why not?
?
Are you truly using your intellect and speculation for beneficial purposes, or do you neglect them out of fear of the outcomes? What one step can you take today to engage more deeply with the truths this passage describes?

God Always Provides Understanding for His Commands
Maturidi answers an objection about God commanding the incomprehensible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible for God to command something that cannot be understood, and how He provides a way for understanding through either intellect or revelation.

Core Idea
God’s commands always come with a path to understanding.

A common objection in theology is whether God can command something that is beyond human comprehension. In this passage, Imam Maturidi directly addresses this question, clarifying that God never leaves a command without providing a means to understand it. Let's see how he dismantles this objection using the foundations of intellect and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the opening objection: if God can command something in the intellect that is not understood, why can't He address something not understood through revelation?
Notice the objector assumes a symmetry between intellect and revelation.
2
Examine Maturidi's response
Read the answer which denies the premise. Maturidi asserts there is no difference between the two cases, and it's not permissible to make such an absolute statement.
3
Identify the two channels of understanding
Look for the statement that God provides a way for understanding every command, either through the intellect or through hearing (revelation). Note that this covers all commands.
4
Understand the implication for those who cannot grasp
The passage says whoever's understanding falls short is outside the command. Reflect on what this means for accountability.
5
Connect to the foundations (usul)
Finally, note that determining which type a command belongs to is done through reflection and thought on the foundations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إذ جاز أن يأمر الله العبد في عقله بما لا يفهم لم لا جاز أن يخاطبه بما لا يفهمه؟ قيل: لا فرق بينهما، ولا يجوز الإطلاق عليه بالذى ذكرت، وما من شيء يأمر الله به إما يبعث العقل عليه أو يخاطب السمع إلا وقد جعل الله لفهم ذلك سبيلا، ومن قصر فهمه عن احتماله فهو خارج عن الأمر، لكن جهات الأصول هي بالنظر والفكر أن ذلك من أي نوع، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
If it is said: Since it is permissible for God to command the servant in his intellect what he does not understand, why is it not permissible for Him to address him with what he does not understand? It is said: There is no difference between them, and it is not permissible to make the absolute statement as you mentioned. For there is nothing that God commands except that either the intellect impels towards it or it addresses the hearing, but God has made for understanding that a way. And whoever's understanding falls short of bearing it is outside the command. However, the aspects of the foundations are through reflection and thought as to which kind that is. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 736

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector suggests that if God can command something in the intellect that is not understood, He could also address something not understood through revelation. The answer denies the premise: God does not command anything without providing a way to understand it, either through reason (al-'aql) or through hearing (al-sam') i.e., revelation. The one who cannot understand is excluded from the command. The foundations (al-usul) are known through reflection and thought, determining which type the command belongs to.

Key Terms
أمر (command)
عقل (intellect)
سمع (hearing/revelation)
فهم (understanding)
أصول (foundations)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God can command arbitrary things without providing any understanding, either because of His absolute power or because of human limitation.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that God always provides a means of understanding—through intellect or revelation—so no command is truly incomprehensible.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, how does God ensure His commands are understood?
A) Through intellect only
B) Through hearing only
C) Through either intellect or hearing
D) Through neither

Key Takeaway
Every divine command is accompanied by a means of understanding, either through intellect or revelation, so no command is truly incomprehensible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a command of God that you do not understand, do you assume it is incomprehensible or do you actively seek the path He has provided? What does your immediate reaction reveal about your trust in His guidance?
?
Think of a time you dismissed a divine command because it seemed beyond your reason or difficult to accept. How does Maturidi’s principle that every command has a path to understanding confront your excuse, and what step will you take to find that path today?
?
If your understanding falls short of a command, are you willing to humbly admit you are 'outside the command' and strive to enter it through reflection and seeking knowledge, or do you tend to rationalize your distance from it? What would change if you took the phrase 'And there is no power except with God' as your starting point?

Knowledge of God Must Be Inferred
Maturidi summarizes the epistemology of knowing God through inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that knowledge of God and His commands is an accident that can only be attained through inference from one's own states, not through innate intuition.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God is a discovered accident, not an innate given.

Imam Maturidi opens his summary by laying the epistemological foundation for all theological inquiry. He asserts that knowledge of God is not self-evident; rather, it is an accident ('arad) that must be acquired through deliberate inference (istidlal). The path to this knowledge is through reflecting on one's own physical and experiential states, which reveal dependence on a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's own logical flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'عرض لا يدرك إلا بالاستدلال' — this is the core claim.
2
Identify the three key components
Identify the three key components: (1) the principle that knowledge of God is an accident inferred, (2) the method of inference from one's own states, and (3) the evidence from necessity and well-being.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does Maturidi emphasize that this knowledge is an 'accident'? What does that imply about its attainability? Connect to the idea that reflection (nazar) is obligatory.
This links to the broader kalam principle that knowledge of God is not automatic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقية أبو منصور رحمه الله: وأصله أن العلم بالله وبأمره عرض لا يدرك إلا بالاستدلال، وقد أظهر به ما يستدل من أحوال نفسه التي عليها مداره، ما بينا أن الضرورة تبعثه على النظر وتدفعه إلى الفكر فيما يرى من أحواله وأعضائه ومنافعه ومضاره التي في الجهل بها عطبه وفى العلم بها صلاحه، وفى صلاحه بها على علمه بأنه لم يكن دبر ما ذكرت من أحوال تضطره إلى معرفته ومن قام هو به، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The faqih Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: Its foundation is that knowledge of God and of His command is an accident that is not perceived except through inference. And He has shown by it what is inferred from the states of his soul on which his affair revolves. We have explained that necessity impels him to reflection and drives him to thought about what he sees of his states, his limbs, his benefits, and his harms, ignorance of which is his ruin and knowledge of which is his well-being. And in his well-being with them [there is] evidence of his knowledge that he was not the manager of what I mentioned of states that compel him to know Him and of the one who established him. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 736

Commentary
This passage is a foundational summary by Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. He states that the principle (asl) is that knowledge of God and His command is an accident ('arad) that can only be grasped through inference (istidlal). He has shown that one can infer from one's own states, which are the basis of his affair. Necessity (darura) drives one to reflect on his states, limbs, benefits, and harms; ignorance leads to ruin, knowledge to well-being. The well-being he experiences is evidence that he himself was not the manager of these states, pointing to a creator and sustainer.

Key Terms
علم (knowledge)
استدلال (inference)
عرض (accident)
ضرورة (necessity)
نظر (reflection)
صلاح (well-being)
فساد (ruin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of God is innate and does not require any reasoning or inference.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that knowledge of God is an accident that is only attained through inference (istidlal). It is not self-evident but must be derived from reflection on one's own states.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how is knowledge of God attained?
A) Through direct intuition
B) Through inference from one's own states
C) Through revelation only
D) Through tradition

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of God is an accident that can only be gained through inference from one's own created states, proving one is not the originator of oneself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever honestly felt that you are the ultimate controller of your own life? When you reflect on your own body—the beating of your heart, the healing of a wound—how can you still maintain that belief after reading Maturidi's argument? This week, pause each morning and consciously attribute your very existence and well-being to the One who manages you.
?
Maturidi says ignorance of your own states leads to ruin. Are you actively reflecting on the signs of Allah within yourself, or do you live heedlessly? Commit to a daily practice of reflecting on one limb or ability as a sign of your contingency.
?
If knowledge of God is an accident that requires inference, then what are the 'states' in your life right now that are driving you to that inference? Identify one personal benefit or harm you've experienced today, and use it as a proof of the One who manages you. Will you let necessity push you to true knowledge?

The Permissibility of Divine Command Beyond Understanding
Maturidi answers the first objection regarding God's command and understanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's answer to the objection that if God can command what is not rationally understood, He could also address what is not understood at all.

Core Idea
God always provides a means to understand His commands, whether through reason or revelation.

This lesson addresses a theological objection: if God can command what the intellect does not grasp, why can He not address something entirely incomprehensible? Maturidi responds by affirming that there is no difference — God always provides a path to understanding His commands, whether through intellectual reflection or scriptural address. Those who fail to understand have fallen short of the command's scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the first objection: 'If it is permissible for God to command His servant in his intellect with what he does not understand, why is it not permissible for Him to address him with what he does not understand?' Then read Maturidi's answer.
Pay attention to the structure: objection followed by response.
2
Understand the principle
Notice that Maturidi states there is no difference between the two cases. He asserts that for every command, God provides a way of understanding — either through intellectual impulsion or scriptural address.
This principle undercuts the objection.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this implies about human responsibility: if understanding is always possible, then failure to understand is a shortcoming on the servant's part. The phrase 'outside the command' indicates exclusion from obligation.
The final line about 'reflection and thought' hints at methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إذ جاز أن يأمر الله العبد في عقله بما لا يفهم لم لا جاز أن يخاطبه بما لا يفهمه ؟ قيل : لا فرق بينهما ، ولا يجوز الإطلاق عليه بالذى ذكرت ، وما من شيء يأمر الله به إما يبعث العقل عليه أو خطاب السمع إلا وقد جعل الله لفهم ذلك سبيلا ، ومن قصر فهمه عن احتماله فهو خارج عن الأمر، لكن جهات الأصول هي بالنظر والفكر أن ذلك من أي نوع ، ولا قوة إلا بالله. فإن قال : إذ لا عذر فى الشاهد للعبد أقبل من قوله لسيده : لم أعلم أن فعلى يسخطك فأتركه ، ولو علمت ذلك لا نزجرت مما فعلت ، لم لا كان ذلك في حكمة الله مقبولا ؟ قيل : ذلك إنما حسن بيننا لارتفاع ما به يُعرف الأمر من الدليل عليه ، وأما الله سبحانه فقد جعل لعبده على الأمر بما أمره به دليلا وحرك ذهنه بالخواطر ونبهه بصنوف العبر ، فإنما أتى من قبل تركه النظر ، وذلك فعله ، فيصير بما هو معتذرا محجوجا ؛ إذ بفعله أعرض عن ذلك ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
For if it is said: If it is permissible for God to command His servant in his intellect with what he does not understand, why is it not permissible for Him to address him with what he does not understand? It is said: There is no difference between them, and it is not permissible to apply absolutely what you mentioned. There is nothing God commands, whether the intellect impels to it or the address of hearing, except that God has made a way for understanding it. Whoever's understanding falls short of bearing it is outside the command. But the aspects of the foundations are by reflection and thought to determine which type it is. There is no power except with God. Then if it is said: Since there is no excuse in the witnessed realm for a servant if his statement to his master is accepted: 'I did not know that my action displeases you, so I will leave it,' and if I had known that, I would have refrained from what I did. Why would that not be accepted in the wisdom of God? It is said: That is only good among us because the evidence by which the command is known is absent. But God Almighty has placed for His servant evidence for the command with which He commanded him, and stirred his mind with thoughts, and alerted him with various signs. So it came only from his abandonment of reflection, and that is his action. Thus he becomes, while apologizing, argumentatively defeated; for by his action he turned away from that. There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 736

Commentary
This passage presents two objections and their answers regarding God's command and human understanding. The first objection asks: If God can command what is not rationally understood, why can He not address what is not understood at all? The answer is that there is no difference; God always provides a means to understand His commands, whether through reason or revelation. Those who fail to understand fall short. The second objection argues that in human affairs, ignorance is no excuse, so why should God accept ignorance? The answer explains that in human affairs, evidence is often absent, but God has provided clear evidence for His commands through thoughts and signs. Therefore, ignorance is due to the servant's neglect of reflection, and his excuse is invalid.

Key Terms
أمر
عقل
خطاب
فهم
نظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's command can be entirely beyond understanding.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that God always provides a means to understand His commands.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens to someone whose understanding falls short of bearing God's command?
A) They are punished.
B) They are outside the command.
C) They are still obligated.
D) They are excused.

Key Takeaway
God never commands without providing a way for understanding, so failure to understand results from the servant's limitation, not God's injustice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command of Allah that seems difficult or beyond your current understanding, do you trust that He has provided a way for you to grasp it, or do you use confusion as an excuse to delay obedience? What specific step will you take this week to seek that understanding?
?
The passage says that those whose understanding falls short are 'outside the command.' Have you ever dismissed a religious obligation as irrelevant to you because you didn't fully comprehend its wisdom? Which command are you most prone to ignore, and how can you humbly re-engage with it?
?
Reflect on a time you felt frustrated by a teaching of Islam that seemed irrational. Did your frustration lead you to reject it, or did it drive you to deeper reflection (nazar)? What is one question about your faith that you have been avoiding, and how will you honestly confront it using both reason and revelation?

The Invalidity of Ignorance as an Excuse Before God
Maturidi answers the second objection regarding the acceptance of ignorance as an excuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ignorance of God's command is not a valid excuse according to Maturidi.

Core Idea
God provides clear evidence for His commands, so ignorance is due to one's own neglect of reflection.

The second objection raises a comparison with human interactions: if a servant in this world is excused for ignorance, why would God not accept the same excuse? Maturidi explains that in human affairs, evidence is often lacking, but God has furnished abundant evidence for His commands through thoughts and signs. Therefore, ignorance stems from abandoning reflection, making the excuse invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the second objection: 'Since there is no excuse in the witnessed realm for a servant if his statement to his master is accepted: "I did not know that my action displeases you..." why would that not be accepted in the wisdom of God?'
Note the analogy drawn from human social relations.
2
Understand Maturidi's response
Notice the contrast: in human affairs, the excuse is accepted because evidence is absent, but God has provided evidence for His command.
3
Recognize the root cause
Maturidi states that the servant's ignorance comes from his own abandonment of reflection, and he is thus defeated in his excuse.
This shifts responsibility to the servant.
4
Reflect on responsibility
Consider the implication: humans are responsible to seek understanding through reflection and the signs God has placed.
The phrase 'there is no power except with God' reminds of divine assistance.

Knowledge of God Through Inference from One's Own State
Maturidi asserts knowledge of God requires inference from one's own state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that knowledge of God must be attained through inference (istidlal) from one's own states, not through innate knowledge or blind faith.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God is an accident; it requires inference from your own state.

Maturidi begins by establishing a foundational methodological principle: knowledge of God and His command is not something we are born with or receive without effort. He argues that it is an accident (a contingent attribute) that can only be attained through deliberate inference. In this passage, he points to the necessity that drives every human to reflect on their own body and circumstances, leading to the recognition of a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's words settle before interpreting.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify Maturidi's claim
Find the statement that knowledge of God is an 'accident' (ʿaraḍ) that is only attained through inference (istidlāl). Notice that this sets up the entire argument.
This is a key technical term in kalām.
3
Trace the argument from necessity to inference
Follow how Maturidi says necessity (ḍarūra) impels one to reflection (naẓar) and thought (fikr) about one's own states, limbs, benefits, and harms. This leads to recognizing that the order could not be self-arranged.
This is the core of the inferential process.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider the conclusion: through well-being (ṣalāḥ) in these things, one comes to know that a Creator arranged them. This makes introspection a path to knowledge of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقية أبو منصور رحمه الله : وأصله أن العلم بالله وبأمره عرض لا يدرك إلا بالاستدلال ، وقد أظهر به ما يستدل من أحوال نفسه التي عليها مداره ، ما بينا أن الضرورة تبعثه على النظر وتدفعه إلى الفكر فيما يرى من أحواله وأعضائه ومنافعه ومضاره التي في الجهل بها عطبه وفى العلم بها صلاحه ، وفى صلاحه بها على علمه بأنه لم يكن دبر ما ذكرت من أحوال تضطره إلى معرفته ومن قام هو به ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Al-Faqih Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Its foundation is that knowledge of God and His command is an accident that is only attained through inference. He has manifested through it what can be inferred from his own states upon which it revolves. We explained that necessity impels him to reflection and drives him to thought regarding what he sees of his states, limbs, benefits, and harms — in ignorance of which is his ruin and in knowledge of which is his well-being. And in his well-being through them is his knowledge that he was not the one who arranged what I mentioned of states that compel him to know Him and that He is the one who established them. There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 736

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement by Maturidi. He asserts that knowledge of God and His commands is an accident (a contingent quality) that cannot be attained except through inference (istidlal). The evidence for this is found in one's own states: the necessity (darura) that forces a person to reflect on his own body, limbs, benefits, and harms. Through this reflection, one recognizes that these are well-ordered and that the order could not have been self-arranged. This leads to the knowledge that there is a Creator who arranged them. Thus, introspection is a path to knowledge of God.

Key Terms
علم بالله (knowledge of God)
استدلال (inference)
ضرورة (necessity)
نظر (reflection)
فكر (thought)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of God is innate or comes from revelation alone without rational effort.
Correction: Maturidi argues that knowledge of God is an accident that can only be attained through inference from one's own state, emphasizing the necessity of reflection and thought.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the foundation for attaining knowledge of God?
A) Direct inspiration
B) Inference from one's own state
C) Blind faith
D) Philosophical speculation

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of God requires active inference from the order in one's own existence, not passive acceptance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Maturidi says necessity impels you to this reflection. Do you feel that necessity in your daily life, or have you become comfortable with the 'well-being' of this world without tracing it back to its Giver? What would it take to restore that urgency?

Maturidi's Critique of Ibn Shabib's Argument
Maturidi criticizes the lengthy discussion and points out a flaw in the premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi critiques Ibn Shabib's argument by pointing out a self-contradictory premise regarding the body's first state.

Core Idea
A flawed premise undermines an entire argument, no matter how eloquent.

Imam Maturidi here examines an argument that was cherished by Ahl al-Tawhid but criticized for its excessive length and, more importantly, a critical logical flaw. He shows that Ibn Shabib's own statement about the body's first state contradicts the very foundation of his reasoning. This lesson will guide you through that critique and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice Maturidi's initial acknowledgment and his shift to critique.
Pay attention to the terms 'تطويل' (prolixity) and 'تداخل' (interpenetration) as they signal the nature of the criticism.
2
Identify the flaw Maturidi highlights
Maturidi says if Ibn Shabib had been fair, he would have found what prevents his argument: his statement that the body in its first state is neither at rest nor in motion. This contradicts the idea that a body must always be in one of these states.
This is the crux of the lesson. Understand how a single premise can unravel an entire argument.
3
Reflect on the method of critique
Notice that Maturidi does not reject the entire argument immediately; he first acknowledges its respected status. Then he dissects its logical structure to find the inconsistency.
This approach teaches us to critique arguments fairly and precisely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وهذه عبارة لم يزل أهل التوحيد يعتزون بها ، لكنه أطنب فيها السؤال والجواب ، فذكرت ذلك على الإيماء إلى ما ذكر دون البسط. ثم عورض بالحركة إنها جسم ، فقال : هذا لا يسأل عنه من يقول بقدم الجسم لأنها حدثت بالحس ، وقال : للإحالة أن يكون في المكان الأول على التداخل ، وفي المداخلة إيجاب حركة أخرى للانتقال فيكون غير جسم ، مع لو جعلت الثانية متداخلة يلزم تداخل الأجسام بلا نهاية ، ولو جاز هذا لجاز تداخل الدنيا في بيضة ، ومثله أجاب في التلاقى ، وذلك كله تطويل بلا نفع ولو أنصف لوجد ما يمنعه عن دليله وهو قوله : الجسم في أول حاله ليس بساكن ولا متحرك فأخلاه عما ذكر ، وفي ذلك سبق عن الذي وصف ، لكن من يقول
The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: This expression is one with which the people of tawhid have always been proud, but he went into excessive detail in the question and answer. So I mentioned it by alluding to what was mentioned without elaboration. Then it was objected by (saying) that motion is a body. He said: This is not asked of one who says the body is eternal because it (motion) is perceived by sense. And he said: It is impossible for it to be in the first place by interpenetration, and in interpenetration there is an obligation of another motion for transition, thus it becomes non-body. And if the second is allowed to interpenetrate, it would necessitate the interpenetration of bodies infinitely. And if this were allowed, it would allow the world to interpenetrate an egg. And likewise he answered concerning meeting. All of that is prolixity without benefit. Had he been fair, he would have found what prevents his argument, and that is his statement that the body in its first state is neither at rest nor in motion, so he emptied it of what was mentioned. And in that there is precedence over what he described, but whoever says...
Source pages p. 737

Commentary
This passage is Maturidi's evaluation of Ibn Shabib's argument. He first acknowledges that the argument is respected by Ahl al-Tawhid but criticizes its excessive length in question and answer. He then reports additional objections and responses, such as the objection that motion is a body, and responses involving interpenetration (tadakhul). Maturidi dismisses all this as prolix without benefit. He then identifies what he considers a fatal flaw: Ibn Shabib himself stated that the body in its first state is neither at rest nor in motion. This contradicts the premise that a body is never free from motion or rest. Therefore, the argument is undermined.

Key Terms
عبارة (expression)
أطنب (excessive detail)
تطويل (prolixity)
تداخل (interpenetration)
أول حاله (first state)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Maturidi fully endorses Ibn Shabib's argument because he calls it a proud expression of Ahl al-Tawhid.
Correction: Maturidi critiques both its length and its logical flaw. He points out that the premise that the body in its first state is neither at rest nor in motion contradicts the argument's own foundation.

Checkpoint
What specific flaw does Maturidi identify in Ibn Shabib's argument?
A) The argument relies on sense perception
B) The argument assumes the body initially is neither at rest nor in motion
C) The argument uses interpenetration incorrectly
D) The argument is too brief

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that a single self-contradictory premise can render an entire argument invalid, regardless of its eloquence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever presented an argument or defense of your faith that, upon honest reflection, contained a hidden contradiction between its own premises? How would you correct that now after seeing Maturidi's method?
?
Maturidi did not accept Ibn Shabib's argument simply because it was from a respected scholar; he scrutinized it until he found the flaw. Is there a belief you hold because of authority that you have not critically examined? What would it cost you to examine it today?
?
If your own reasoning about God or creation had a self-contradiction at its core, would you want to know? What steps will you take to invite that kind of honest critique into your learning?
