Maturidi's Critique of Philosophical Ignorance
Maturidi opens his refutation with a critique of philosophical ignorance and borrowing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the philosophers' doctrine arises from ignorance and borrowed terminology, leading to self-contradiction.

Core Idea
Philosophers borrowed monotheistic language without understanding its implications.

Maturidi does not merely reject the philosophers' views; he diagnoses their root cause: ignorance of divine blessings and reliance on illusion. He further exposes how they took concepts from monotheists but misapplied them, resulting in logical inconsistencies. This passage lays out the opening of his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to capture Maturidi's flow.
Notice the rhetorical force of his language.
2
Identify Maturidi's diagnosis
Find where Maturidi explains the root cause of the philosophers' deviation—ignorance and misguidance.
He blames their ignorance of God's blessings and deviation from guidance.
3
Analyze the borrowed concept
Note how Maturidi says they heard the description of God from monotheists and applied it to the hyle.
This is the core of his critique—misapplication of terminology.
4
Reflect on the self-contradiction
See how Maturidi concludes that they nullified what they had affirmed, highlighting their inconsistency.
They contradict themselves by borrowing without regard for logical consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله: فمن تأمل ما صار هؤلاء إليه علم أنهم أوتوا ذلك لجهلهم نعم الله فهموا عن سبيل الرشد فضلوا، ثم بعثتهم حيرة الضلال إلى الاستيناس بمثل هذا الخيال الذي لا يصير عليه عقل ولا يستجلبه هوى، والله المستعان ولولا ذلك ما الذي كان يعرفهم أن ابتداء العالم ما ذكر؟ ثم اسمه الذي وصف ليس فيها ثم ذكرا. وعمله الذي نعت ليس في جوهر العالم دليله ولا في السمع احتماله، لكنهم سمعوا قول أهل التوحيد في وصف الله بالذي صفوا به الهيولى عندهم، ولم ينظروا فيما ألزمهم التقول به، فرجعوا فنقضوا ما قد أثبتوه؛
The jurist (al-faqīh), may God have mercy on him, said: Whoever reflects on what these people have come to knows that they were given that due to their ignorance of God's blessings; they deviated from the path of right guidance and went astray. Then the bewilderment of misguidance led them to find comfort in such an illusion which no intellect can affirm nor desire attract. And God is the Helper. Were it not for that, what would make them know that the beginning of the world is as they mentioned? Then the name which they described is not in it, then they mentioned. And the action which they attributed has no evidence in the jawhar of the world nor is its acceptance possible from revelation. But they heard the statement of the people of tawḥīd describing God with what they described the hyle with, and they did not consider what their saying imposed upon them. So they returned and nullified what they had affirmed.
Source pages p. 747

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the refutation. The author (al-faqīh, i.e., Maturidi) states that anyone who examines the philosophers' doctrine realizes it arises from ignorance of God's blessings. Their misguidance leads them to take comfort in a fantasy that no rational person accepts. He then questions how they could know the origin of the world as they claim, since their description of the primordial state lacks evidence both from the nature of jawhar and from revelation. He further argues that they borrowed the language of monotheists (ahl al-tawḥīd) who describe God, and applied it to the hyle, but without considering the implications. Consequently, they contradicted themselves and nullified their own claims.

خيال
أهل التوحيد
هيولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is simply dismissing philosophy out of hand without reasoned argument.
Correction: Maturidi provides a reasoned critique: he identifies ignorance, misuse of monotheistic language, and self-contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what led the philosophers to their doctrine?
A) Deep rational inquiry
B) Ignorance of God's blessings and misguidance
C) Following revelation
D) Empirical observation

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the philosophers' doctrine stems from ignorance and misuse of monotheistic concepts, leading to self-contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you borrowed religious language or concepts without understanding their full implications, simply to appear knowledgeable or fit in? What did that cost you?
?
Maturidi says the philosophers found 'comfort in an illusion' because of their bewilderment. Where in your own life are you clinging to an illusion because facing the truth feels too difficult?
?
Are you truly reflecting on the blessings of God, or have you taken them for granted, allowing your heart to deviate from the path of guidance? What blessing have you ignored today that, if acknowledged, would change your direction?

The Foundational Principle: Revelation and Analogy from the Witnessed to the Unseen
Maturidi establishes the principle of relying on revelation or analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen as the correct methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's foundational principle that theological and cosmological questions must be addressed either through revelation or sound analogical reasoning from the witnessed world to the unseen, and how this principle invalidates materialist theories of the world's origin.

Core Idea
Revelation and analogy from the present to the absent are the only valid paths to knowledge of the unseen.

In this key passage, Imam Maturidi establishes the methodological foundation for discussing the origins of the world and natural phenomena. He argues that we cannot rely on conjectures about stars and natures without either divine revelation or a proper analogical inference from what we witness in the world to what lies beyond it. This principle sets the stage for his critique of materialist and astrological theories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal English translation. Try to get a general sense of Maturidi's argument before analyzing details.
The passage is dense; don't worry about fully understanding every word on the first read.
2
Identify the two epistemological sources
Maturidi begins by stating that the proper principle (al-asl) is to rely on either revelation (al-sam') or analogical deduction from the present (al-hadir) to the absent (al-gha'ib). Find these terms in the Arabic text and note how he presents them as the only valid methods.
3
Analyze the argument about natures
Focus on the part where Maturidi says everything with a nature (tab') does not change except by a changer, who is either wise (hakim) or foolish (safih). Consider how this supports the need for a wise originator rather than blind material forces.
The outcomes ('awaqib) reveal the nature of the changer.
4
Reflect on the refutation of materialist theories
Notice how Maturidi applies this principle to invalidate the theories of the materialists, including their claims about mixture, motion of stars, and hyle. Ask yourself: why do these theories fail according to Maturidi's criterion?
They fail because they do not follow the correct method and because they imply change without a wise changer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : والأصل فى هذا عندنا وفيما ذكر من النجوم والطبائع أن لا يخلو من أن يرجع في ذلك إلى السمع، وفيهم سماع أهل التوحيد أثبت براهين الصدق أو يستدل بالحاضر الموجود على الغائب، فإن كان هذا معهم طريقه فيجب؛ إذ الموجود على حال و بالوجود اعتباره - أن يكون الذي به وجد بهذه الصفة، فيبطل قولهم فى حدوث العالم فيبطل قولهم فى حدوث العالم بالامتزاج ويتحرك النجوم، وتقليب القوة الهيولى، والهيولى والقوة جميعا، وإن كان على اعتبار معان في الموجود يدل عليه فان الأصل أن كل ذي طبع لا يتغير عما عليه إلى خلاف إلا بمغير حكيم أو سفيه، لكن يظهر أمرهما بالعواقب، فمثله الأصل الذي أشاروا إليه إنه لا يصير على غير تلك الحال مما يصلح عواقبه إلا بحكيم؛ إذ هي وذلك يبطل أصلهم ويثبت أن الأصل احتمل ما احتمل يجعل غيره كذلك، وفى ذلك حدثه بمحدث حكيم، وبالله التوفيق وأيضا أن المعلوم فيما كان طبعه الإحراق أنه لا يحرق إلا المطبوع لاحتمال الإحتراق، وكذا التسويد وكل حال وجوهر ، ثم ليس في طبع المحتمل الرفع إلى القابل فيه بالطبع، ولا في طبع المحرق أن إلى من يحتمل ذلك، ومن أراد في الشاهد ذلك لا يتهيأ له دون العلم بالوجود والجمع بينهما، فعلى ذلك أمر ذلك يصير كذلك، فيه، والله الموفق . هم تلك الأصول التى قالوها في موات لا تدبير لهن الغائب، فيبطل الذى راموا إثباته ويصير هو بمعنى ما ما إذا كان مع جميع
The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The principle in this regarding us and regarding what was mentioned of stars and natures (al-taba'i') is that it is not free from that one must refer in that to revelation (al-sam'), and among them [there is] the hearing of the people of tawhid who established proofs of truthfulness, or one deduces from the present existent to the absent. If this is their way, then it is necessary; for the existent is in a state and by existence its consideration is that that by which it exists is by this attribute. So their statement about the origination of the world is invalidated, their statement about the origination of the world by mixture and the motion of stars, and the turning of the power of hyle, and the hyle and power all together. And if it is upon consideration of meanings in the existent that indicate it, then the principle is that everything with a nature (tab') does not change from what it is to its contrary except by a wise (hakim) or foolish (safih) changer, but their matter appears by the outcomes. So likewise the principle they indicated: that it does not become upon other than that state from what makes its outcomes suitable except by a wise [changer]; for it and that invalidates their principle and establishes that the principle [i.e., hyle] accepted what it accepted, makes another like it, and in that is its origination by a wise originator. And with Allah is success. And also: it is known that what has the nature of burning does not burn except that which is naturally disposed to bear burning, and likewise blackening and every state and atom. Then it is not in the nature of the receptacle to raise to the receiver in it by nature, nor in the nature of the burner to [reach] to that which bears it. And whoever intends that in the witnessed [world] is not able to do so without knowledge of existence and combining them. So upon that, the matter becomes like that. And Allah is the granter of success. These are the principles they stated about dead things that have no management, and the absent, so what they sought to establish is invalidated, and it becomes in the sense of 'what if it were with all'.
Source pages p. 750

Commentary
This passage is a foundational methodological statement by the author. He begins by asserting that the proper principle in discussing stars and natures is to rely on revelation (sam') or on analogical deduction from the present (shahid) to the absent (gha'ib). If their theory follows this method, it is acceptable; otherwise it is invalid. He then shows how their theories (origination by mixture, motion of stars, turning of power of hyle) are invalidated because they do not follow this principle. He then presents an argument about natures (taba'i'): anything that has a nature does not change except by a changer, who is either wise or foolish; the outcomes reveal the changer's nature. Since the theories they propose would require foolishness or inconsistency, they are invalid. Then he argues that a thing with a nature only acts upon what is naturally disposed to receive that action, and this requires knowledge and combination, which in the unseen points to a wise originator. Finally, he concludes that the principles they stated about lifeless things and the unseen are false, and their intended proof is invalidated.

Key Terms
سمع (revelation)
حاضر (present)
غائب (absent)
طبع (nature)
حكيم (wise)
سفيه (foolish)
امتزاج (mixture)
تقليب (turning)
إحراق (burning)
مطبوع (naturally disposed)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the nature of things themselves is sufficient to explain their origin and behavior without reference to a creator.
Correction: Maturidi argues that everything with a nature does not change except by a changer, and that changer's wisdom or foolishness is revealed by outcomes. This points to a wise originator, not to nature alone.

Checkpoint
According to the foundational principle, what is the correct method for studying the origin of the world and natural phenomena?
A) Pure rational speculation without reliance on revelation.
B) Reliance on revelation or analogical deduction from the present to the absent.
C) Empirical observation alone.
D) Accepting the theories of the astrologers and materialists.

Key Takeaway
The proper method for understanding the unseen is to rely on revelation or analogical reasoning from the witnessed world, and this method refutes materialist theories by showing that all change requires a wise originator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate the existence of Allah, do you truly rely on the method of revelation and analogy from the witnessed world, or do you sometimes let your own desires shape your understanding of the unseen? Examine the last time you thought about Allah's attributes—was your reasoning grounded in this principle? Commit to reading the Qur'an with this methodology.
?
The passage shows that even materialists implicitly acknowledge the need for a wise changer through their own principles. Have you ever ignored evidence of design in your own life because it was uncomfortable to admit that you are not in control? Consider one area where you resist acknowledging Allah's wisdom, and sincerely repent.
?
Maturidi refutes the idea that nature acts independently. In your daily life, do you sometimes act as if the world runs on its own, forgetting that every change happens by Allah's will? How can you train yourself to see Allah's hand in every event, from the rising of the sun to the beating of your heart? Start a daily practice of saying 'Ma sha Allah' for every occurrence.

The Three States of Sensory Perception
Maturidi explains the foundation of sensory perception and its three states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three states of sensory perception according to Imam Maturidi and how they relate to the reliability of sensory knowledge.

Core Idea
Sensory perception varies due to direction, affliction, and veils, but the senses themselves are reliable.

Imam Maturidi begins by establishing a foundational principle of sensory perception: each sense direction perceives only what faces it. He then explains how variations occur through affliction or veiling, leading to three distinct states. This passage grounds his epistemology in the reliability of sense experience, a key point in his theological method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structured presentation: principle, then three states.
2
Identify the three states
The passage lists three states: reversal (no perception), removal of veils (true perception), and mixture (partial perception). Find each one and note how Maturidi defines them.
These states arise from conditions affecting the sense direction.
3
Understand the cause of variation
Maturidi explains that variation comes from either an affliction (āfah) in the sense direction or a veil covering the object. Ask yourself: how does this protect the reliability of sensation?
The senses themselves are not flawed; external factors cause variation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that all knowledge from sensation is true; there is no real variation in sensory knowledge itself. Consider why he emphasizes this.
This is important for his overall epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : والأصل فى هذا أن الإنسان إذا اشتمل على حدود وجهات فكل جهة منه تقابل جهة من المدرك، لا يُدرك بتلك الجهة غير الجهة التي قابلته، فإذا اعترضت الافة في جهته التى بها يدرك مقابلها أو غشى مقابلها شيء ستره فيذهب مقدار ذلك من الجهة ومقابلها، فيكون كالإدراك بغير الجهة التي هي لذلك النوع من الإدراك، فيكون الأحوال ثلاثة: بقلب الجهة لا يدرك منه شيئا البتة، وتقريرها مع ارتفاع السواتر كلها فيدرك به حقيقة المدرك، أو الاختلاط، فعلى تفاوت ذلك يتفاوت الدرك، وكل ذلك حق الحس معلوم بالحس، فلم يرد في علم الحس اختلاف البتة في الحقيقة، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The foundation in this is that when a human encompasses limits and directions, each direction of his faces a direction of the perceived object. He does not perceive by that direction other than the direction that faces it. So if an affliction intervenes in the direction by which he perceives its counterpart, or something covers its counterpart and veils it, then the amount of that direction and its counterpart goes away, and it becomes like perception by a direction other than the one that is for that type of perception. So the states are three: by reversing the direction he perceives nothing at all; its establishment with the removal of all veils so that he perceives the reality of the perceived object; or mixture. So according to the variation of that, perception varies, and all that is true of sensation known by sensation. So no variation at all has occurred in the knowledge of sensation in reality, and there is no power but Allah.
Source pages p. 754

Commentary
This passage defines the mechanism of sensory perception and its possible variations. Maturidi begins by stating the principle: when a person has multiple sense directions (e.g., sight from different angles), each direction perceives only what faces it. If an affliction (āfah) affects that direction, or something veils the object, perception from that direction is impaired. This results in three states: (1) complete reversal of direction yields no perception; (2) removal of all veils gives true perception; (3) mixture of conditions leads to partial perception. He emphasizes that all sensory knowledge is true in itself—there is no inherent contradiction in sensation; variation is due to external factors. The passage underscores the reliability of the senses as a source of knowledge.

Key Terms
إدراك
جهة
آفة
حقيقة
اختلاط
سواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Variation in sensory perception means the senses are unreliable or subjective.
Correction: Maturidi argues that variation is due to external factors (affliction, veils), not inherent unreliability. Sensory knowledge itself is true and consistent.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how many states can sensory perception take?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Sensory perception can be in three states (no perception, true perception, or mixed) due to external factors, but the senses themselves are reliable sources of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you misjudged someone or something based on your senses? What 'affliction' or 'veil' might have been present that distorted your perception? Will you actively work to remove those veils before forming judgments?
?
Your senses are reliable by design, yet you often blame them for your mistakes. Could it be that the real flaw lies in neglecting to verify which 'state' your perception is in? How will you check yourself before believing your senses are telling the full truth?
?
Maturidi teaches that reversal of direction yields no perception. In what areas of your religious understanding have you completely reversed direction—rejecting truth because you approached from the wrong angle? What step will you take today to realign your direction toward Allah?

The Samarrian's Challenge: Dreams and Wakefulness
Samarrian argument equating dream and wakeful perception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Samarrian argument that dreams and wakeful perceptions are epistemically similar and that knowledge of wakefulness is acquired, not necessary.

Core Idea
The Samarrian claims dream and wakeful knowledge are indistinguishable without external criteria.

This lesson presents the Samarrian's objection to Maturidi: if a dreamer can perceive impossible things, and the wakeful person perceives similar content, then how can we privilege wakeful knowledge? Maturidi faithfully records their argument before refuting it. Pay attention to the two key claims: the distinction is due to the intellect in sleep, and knowledge of wakeful soundness is acquired.

Guided Path
1
Read the Samarrian's initial argument
Start by reading the first part of the Arabic passage where the Samarrian draws an analogy between the dreamer and the wakeful. Notice how he tries to erase the difference by saying 'فلعل أمر اليقظان على هذا' (so perhaps the matter of the wakeful is like this).
The Samarrian is constructing a skeptical challenge.
2
Identify the proposed distinction
The Samarrian claims the intellect in sleep allows impossible perceptions (e.g., seeing oneself dead). He argues that the dreamer does not actually believe these impossibilities, so they are not contradictions. Find this part and understand his attempt to defend the coherence of dream content.
3
Examine the claim of acquired knowledge
The Samarrian then asserts that knowing the soundness of wakefulness and corruption of sleep is acquired (اكتساب), not necessary. He also admits that some dreams can be true, but attributes that to a 'kingdom' or health. Note this subtle concession.
This sets up Maturidi's response in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بما يرى النائم، فيخرج على ما يرى، فلعل أمر اليقظان على هذا، أو ما يعلم يعلم ذا من ذا فزعم أن الذي يفرق بين الأمرين أنه في العقل في حال النوم، نحو أن يرى نفسه ميتا، والميت لا يعلم أو يرى رأسه ملقى فى حجره، ومثله لا يحتمل رؤية اليقظان. فإن قيل : كيف يتوهم النائم المحال، لا يثبت في الوهم ؟ قيل : عند ما يرى نفسه فى المنام لا يعتقدها حية ميتة، وذلك هو وكذلك إذا رأى رأسه ملقى لا يتوهمه في مكانين. وزعم أن العلم بصحة ما في اليقظة وفساد ما في النوم اكتساب، دلیله ما ذكرت، قال : وقد يرى في المنام ما يصح، ذلك إنما بملك بريه أو بما ذلك في الأصحاء أو بعض ذلك قال الفقيه رحمه الله : والأصل فى هذا ما فى الأول : إن النائم ذو آفة يعرفها بما يعلم به يقظته، وذلك حق الحس، إنه يرى في النوم مضطرا، و في اليقظة لا، وكذلك يبقى ألم ما يضرب في حال اليقظة، ويعرف لذة ما به يغتذى، ويوجب وليس بيننا وبين هؤلاء في هذه الأحوال مسألة، إنما بيننا إلزام حق اليقظة وتحقيقه بضرورة بما ذكرنا، ثم تغير ذلك؛ إنما ذلك للآفات التي تعترض. وجملته أن الطبيعة أو النجوم أو الأغذية لا يحتمل أن تولد ذلك، ولا فيها ما ذلك، وأن لكل شيء من ذلك مضرة ومنفعة، وما به الغلبة والاعتدال، فلا يحتمل وجود مثله بالطبع ولا بالنجم من حيث خروج ذلك على ما فيه من الحكمة والاتقان، وما يوجبه الطبع لا يحتمل ذلك، وقد مر بيان ذلك، والله الموفق.
Then he (the Samarrian) countered with what the dreamer sees, claiming that the matter of the wakeful person is similar, or that one does not know which is which. He claimed that what distinguishes the two is the intellect in the state of sleep, such as when one sees himself dead—and the dead does not know—or sees his head placed in his lap, and the like of which the wakeful cannot see. If it is said: How does the dreamer imagine the impossible, which is not established in the imagination? He said: When he sees himself in the dream, he does not believe himself to be alive or dead; that is it. Similarly, if he sees his head placed, he does not imagine it in two places. And he claimed that knowledge of the soundness of what is in wakefulness and the corruption of what is in sleep is acquired, and its evidence is what I mentioned. He said: And sometimes the dreamer sees something that is sound—that is only by the kingdom of ... or by what is in healthy people or some of that. The faqih, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The foundation in this is what was in the first: the dreamer is afflicted with an affliction (āfah) that he knows by what he knows of his wakefulness, and that is the truth of sensation. He sees in sleep necessarily (muḍṭarran), and in wakefulness not. Similarly, the pain of a blow remains in wakefulness, and he knows the pleasure of what he eats and causes. There is no dispute between us and these people regarding these states; rather between us is the obligation of the truth of wakefulness and its verification by necessity (ḍarūrah) as we have mentioned. Then the change of that is only due to afflictions that intervene. The summary: Nature, stars, or foods cannot generate that, nor do they have that in them. For everything there is harm and benefit, and what has predominance and balance. So the existence of such cannot be by nature or by star, given its emergence upon wisdom and perfection. And what nature necessitates cannot bear that. This has already been explained. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 755

Commentary
This passage addresses the Samarrian's objection that dream states and wakefulness are similar, with no clear distinction. Maturidi presents the Samarrian argument: the difference lies in the intellect during sleep; the dreamer sees impossible things like being dead, which a wakeful person cannot. The Samarrian attempts to answer an objection about how the impossible is imagined by saying the dreamer does not actually believe the impossible. Then the Samarrian claims knowledge of wakeful truth is acquired, and some dreams can be true. Maturidi (referred to as 'al-faqih') responds the foundation is that the dreamer is under an affliction (āfah) that he recognizes by comparing to his wakeful state—this is a matter of sensory truth. He sees necessarily in sleep, not in wakefulness. The author insists there is no real dispute: the truth of wakefulness is established by necessity (ḍarūrah). Any changes in perception are due to intervening afflictions. He concludes that nature, stars, or foods cannot produce such perceptions because everything has a balance of benefit and harm, and such wisdom and perfection cannot arise from mere natural necessity.

Key Terms
نائم
يقظة
عقل
اكتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That dreams and wakeful perceptions are epistemically equivalent and that no rational distinction exists.
Correction: Maturidi will argue that the dreamer is under an affliction (āfah) and that wakeful knowledge is necessary (ḍarūrī), not acquired.

Checkpoint
According to the Samarrian, what distinguishes dream from wakeful perception?
A) The dreamer's belief in the content
B) The intellect in the state of sleep
C) The presence of an affliction (āfah)
D) The dreamer's acquired knowledge

Key Takeaway
The Samarrian challenges the reliability of wakeful knowledge by equating it with dream perception, arguing that their distinction relies on the intellect in sleep and that certainty is acquired.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that our certainty about being awake is not self-evident but must be acquired. Think of a time when you were unsure if something was a dream or real. How did you resolve that doubt? Are you now certain that your current state is wakeful? What is the basis for that certainty, and could you defend it if challenged like the Samarrian?
?
The Samarrian argues that in dreams we see impossibilities but do not truly believe them. When you sin in your waking life, do you sometimes act as if you are in a dream—seeing the truth but not fully believing it? What would it take for you to truly 'wake up' and act with the conviction of the wakeful?
?
The Samarrian says that knowledge of wakefulness is 'acquired.' How have you acquired certainty about the most fundamental truths of Islam? Are there areas where your certainty feels more like dream-perception than wakeful knowledge? What one step could you take today to move that certainty from acquired to necessary?

Maturidi's Refutation: Necessity of Wakefulness and Affliction of Dreams
Maturidi's refutation: necessity of wakefulness and affliction of dreams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that wakeful knowledge is necessary (ḍarūrī) and that the dreamer is under an affliction, invalidating the Samarrian's equivalence.

Core Idea
Wakeful knowledge is necessary; dreams are caused by affliction.

This lesson presents Maturidi's direct response to the Samarrian. He argues that the foundation is the dreamer's affliction (āfah), recognized by comparison to wakefulness. Wakeful perception is necessary (ḍarūrī), while dream perception is not. Maturidi then extends this to reject natural or astral causes for such perceptual changes, emphasizing wisdom and perfection in creation.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's foundational claim
Read the second part of the passage starting from 'قال الفقيه'. Maturidi states that the dreamer has an affliction (āfah) known by contrasting with wakefulness. He emphasizes that the dreamer sees necessarily in sleep but not in wakefulness—meaning dream content forces itself upon the dreamer without volition.
This reverses the Samarrian's claim: dreams are not equivalent because they lack the voluntary aspect of wakeful perception.
2
Understand the role of necessity (ḍarūrah)
Maturidi insists there is no real dispute: the truth of wakefulness is established by necessity (ḍarūrah). Changes in perception are due to intervening afflictions. Identify this key point and how it grounds certainty.
3
Analyze the rejection of natural/astral causes
Maturidi concludes that nature, stars, or foods cannot produce the complex perceptions of dreams or wakefulness because of the inherent wisdom and balance (حكمة وإتقان) in creation. He states that what nature necessitates cannot produce such order.
This final part ties the refutation to broader theological principles of causality.

Source Text

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That dreams and wakefulness are equally reliable because both involve perception.
Correction: Maturidi argues that wakeful perception is necessary (ḍarūrī) and reliable, while dream perception is caused by an affliction and lacks the same epistemic status.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how does the dreamer perceive in sleep?
A) Voluntarily
B) Necessarily (مضطرا)
C) By acquired knowledge
D) By intellectual reasoning

Key Takeaway
Wakeful knowledge is necessary and reliable; dreams are the result of an affliction, and the order of perception cannot be explained by natural causes but reflects divine wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your wakeful senses every moment, but this lesson says that truth is established by necessity. When has your own ‘wakeful’ reasoning been clouded by an affliction—a desire, a habit, a prejudice—so that you mistook a dream for reality? Will you now inspect those areas with the same necessity Maturidi demands?
?
Maturidi argues that the order of perception reflects divine wisdom (ḥikmah) and perfection (itqān). Look at your own daily life: where do you take that order for granted, treating your perceptions as mere products of nature rather than gifts from a wise Creator? What would change if you saw every clear thought as a sign of His wisdom?
?
You have experienced dreams that felt real, yet this passage teaches they are afflictions. Consider: are there beliefs you hold that are equally afflicted—accepted without the necessity of wakeful evidence? What would it cost you to test them against the truth of wakefulness, and are you willing to pay that price?

The Sufficiency of Evidence for Tawhid
Maturidi asserts the sufficiency of evidence for tawhid from callers and creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that even the minimal evidence of truthful callers and the inherent testimony of creation are sufficient to establish tawhid.

Core Idea
Even one true proof is enough; creation itself speaks of its Creator.

Maturidi begins by addressing a potential objection: what if people of tawhid had only the proofs from truthful callers and clear signs? He asserts that would be sufficient, especially since deniers have nothing comparable. Then he intensifies: in fact, everything in existence testifies by its own essence that it is created and created by a Wise Creator. This passage establishes that the evidence for tawhid is overwhelming and accessible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the flow. Notice the conditional structure beginning with 'ولو لم يكن' and the rhetorical intensifier 'فكيف'.
The passage is short but dense; take time to understand each phrase.
2
Identify the two arguments
First, Maturidi says even the proofs of truthful callers alone would suffice. Second, he points out that everything in creation testifies to its createdness. These are complementary arguments.
The testimony of creation is intrinsic and universal.
3
Reflect on the rhetorical question
Consider why Maturidi asks 'فكيف' (how much more so). It bridges the minimal evidence to the overwhelming evidence of creation. This shows the depth of the proof for tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحد على اتقاء ما يضره إذا لم يعلمه ؛ فعلى ذلك خلق الأمرين في الحكمة أوجب مع ما في ذلك من دلالة غنى الفاعل وتمام قوته وعلمه بما وأتم من خلق أحدهما ولا قوة إلا بالله ولو لم يكن لما عليه أهل التوحيد سوى أدلة صدق الدعاة إليه ، والبراهين النيرة معهم معهم، وهم الوسل ، مما لا يوجد شيء من ذلك لأحد من منكرى الصانع الواحد لكان ذلك كافيا ، فكيف وما من شيء إلا وهو بجوهره يشهد بحدثه ، وأنه حدث المحدث حكيم . لولا تعنت الملحدين بما ادعوا من قدم الأعيان مما لا سبيل لهم فى الرجوع إليه إلا إلى تقليد من ليس دليل ، أو جعله سفهه ، وهو عجزه عن الوقوف على كون شيء لا عن شيء دليلا له . ولا ريب أن كلا منهم يعلم من نفسه جهلا بأشياء نفسه جهلا بأشياء ، ثم العلم بها ، وعجزا عن أشياء ، ثم قدرته عليها ، وضرورة إلى أشياء ، ثم غنى عنها ، فحق من هذا وصفه أن لا يثق برأيه ، ولا ينفع ما يرى انه من اشارة عقله مع ما لا يخلو أن من رد ذلك إلى الطبائع التي لا تعقل ما يولد منها وبها ، وكذلك النجوم ، أو إلى عدد من الصانعين مما كان بدء أمرهم الجهل والعمى ، أو إلى تقليد أقاويل فى قدم الأشياء على ما [هي] عليه مما يتناقض ويتضاد ، فأنى لهم العقل هذه الأصول المتجاهلة الذين هم فروعها ، أو الوقوف على حقائق الأشياء حتى يدعون فى شيء حكمة أو سفها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
One is to avoid what harms him if he does not know it; accordingly, the creation of the two matters in wisdom is more obligatory, along with what indicates the Self-sufficiency of the Agent and the perfection of His power and His knowledge of what [?] and more perfect than creating one of them. And if there were not for the people of tawhid except the proofs of the truth of the callers to it, and the luminous evidences with them—they are the means—while none of that exists for any denier of the sole Maker, that would be sufficient. How so, and there is nothing except that it testifies by its essence to its createdness, and that it was created by a Wise Creator? Were it not for the obstinacy of the atheists by what they claimed of the eternity of entities, with no way for them to refer back except to blind imitation of one who is not proof, or to make his foolishness—and it is his inability to understand that a thing comes from nothing—as proof for him. And there is no doubt that each of them knows from himself ignorance of some things, then knowledge of them, and incapacity for some things, then power over them, and necessity for some things, then independence from them. So it is fitting for one described thus that he should not trust his opinion, nor does what he sees from his intellect's indication benefit him, given that he who attributes that to the natures that do not understand what is generated from and by them, and likewise the stars, or to a number of makers whose beginning was ignorance and blindness, or to blind imitation of sayings about the eternity of things as they are, which are contradictory and opposites— how can they have reason for these ignorant principles while they are their branches? Or how can they stop at the realities of things so that they claim wisdom or foolishness in something? And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 767

Commentary
This passage is a cumulative argument for the sufficiency of evidence for tawhid and a refutation of those who deny the Creator. Maturidi begins by noting that even if the people of tawhid had only the proofs of the truth of the callers and the clear evidences (the prophets and their signs), that alone would be enough to establish the truth, especially since the deniers have nothing comparable. He then intensifies: in fact, everything in existence testifies by its very essence to its being created and that it was created by a Wise Creator. The obstacle is atheists' obstinacy in claiming eternal entities without any evidence, relying only on blind imitation of people who are not proofs or on their own foolishness—their inability to conceive of something coming from nothing. Maturidi then points out that these deniers are internally inconsistent: each knows himself as ignorant of some things then later learns them, incapable of some things then becomes able, in need of some things then independent. So one with such a nature should not trust his own opinion, nor can his intellectual indications benefit him when his reasoning leads him to attribute everything to unthinking natures, stars, or multiple creators—all starting from ignorance—or to contradictory sayings about eternity. He asks: how can such people, being mere branches of these ignorant principles, claim to have reason or understand the realities of things to judge wisdom or foolishness? The passage ends with the affirmation that all power is from Allah.

Key Terms
أهل التوحيد
أدلة صدق الدعاة
منكرى الصانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Evidence for tawhid is only from revelation and not from reason or creation.
Correction: Maturidi emphasizes that creation itself testifies by its essence, which rational reflection can recognize.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would be sufficient to establish tawhid?
A) Only the testimony of creation.
B) Even only the proofs of truthful callers, along with the testimony of creation.
C) Philosophical arguments alone.
D) The consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that evidence for tawhid is both minimal (prophetic proofs) and universal (creation's testimony).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every single thing around you, by its very existence, declares that it is created and that it has a Creator. Yet you sometimes live as if this reality has no claim on you. What specific habit or attitude would change if you truly heard that declaration right now?
?
The callers brought clear proofs that deniers cannot refute. You have access to these proofs, but have you honestly examined them to the point that they compel your heart? What would it take for you to stop procrastinating on this investigation?
?
Maturidi says the evidence is sufficient. Your soul knows this, but your daily choices often betray a search for more certainty before obeying. What is the one area of obedience you have delayed, waiting for more evidence, that you can begin today based on what you already know?

The Refutation of Deniers: Inconsistency and Blind Imitation
Maturidi refutes deniers by exposing their lack of proof, internal inconsistency, and reliance on blind imitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes deniers of the Creator by exposing their internal contradictions and their reliance on blind imitation rather than reason.

Core Idea
Deniers are inconsistent and rely on blind imitation, not true reason.

After establishing the sufficiency of evidence, Maturidi turns to refute the atheists and deniers. He attributes their rejection to obstinacy and claims of eternal entities without proof. He then points out that each person experiences ignorance then knowledge, incapacity then power, need then independence—showing they should not trust their own opinion. Their alternative explanations (natures, stars, multiple creators, blind imitation) are contradictory and stem from ignorance. This lesson exposes the irrationality of denying the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation section
Read the passage carefully. Identify the deniers' claim of eternity and Maturidi's critique of their lack of proof.
Note the phrase 'لا سبيل لهم' — they have no way to support their claim.
2
Analyze the internal inconsistency argument
Maturidi points out that every person knows himself to be ignorant then knowledgeable, incapable then capable, needy then independent. This should lead to humility, not pride in one's opinion.
This is a powerful psychological argument.
3
Examine the alternative explanations
Maturidi lists four false alternatives: natures, stars, multiple creators, and blind imitation. He dismisses them as ignorant and contradictory.
Each alternative fails to provide a coherent explanation.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical conclusion
Maturidi asks how such people can claim reason when they follow ignorant principles. This emphasizes their unreasonableness.
The final phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' affirms reliance on Allah.

أحد على اتقاء ما يضره إذا لم يعلمه ؛ فعلى ذلك خلق الأمرين في الحكمة أوجب مع ما في ذلك من دلالة غنى الفاعل وتمام قوته وعلمه بما وأتم من خلق أحدهما ولا قوة إلا بالله ولو لم يكن لما عليه أهل التوحيد سوى أدلة صدق الدعاة إليه ، والبراهين النيرة معهم معهم، وهم الوسل ، مما لا يوجد شيء من ذلك لأحد من منكرى الصانع الواحد لكان ذلك كافيا ، فكيف وما من شيء إلا وهو بجوهره يشهد بحدثه ، وأنه حدث المحدث حكيم . لولا تعنت الملحدين بما ادعوا من قدم الأعيان مما لا سبيل لهم فى الرجوع إليه إلا إلى تقليد من ليس دليل ، أو جعله سفهه ، وهو عجزه عن الوقوف على كون شيء لا عن شيء دليلا له . ولا ريب أن كلا منهم يعلم من نفسه جهلا بأشياء نفسه جهلا بأشياء ، ثم العلم بها ، وعجزا عن أشياء ، ثم قدرته عليها ، وضرورة إلى أشياء ، ثم غنى عنها ، فحق من هذا وصفه أن لا يثق برأيه ، ولا ينفع ما يرى انه من اشارة عقله مع ما لا يخلو أن من رد ذلك إلى الطبائع التي لا تعقل ما يولد منها وبها ، وكذلك النجوم ، أو إلى عدد من الصانعين مما كان بدء أمرهم الجهل والعمى ، أو إلى تقليد أقاويل فى قدم الأشياء على ما [هي] عليه مما يتناقض ويتضاد ، فأنى لهم العقل هذه الأصول المتجاهلة الذين هم فروعها ، أو الوقوف على حقائق الأشياء حتى يدعون فى شيء حكمة أو سفها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
One is to avoid what harms him if he does not know it; accordingly, the creation of the two matters in wisdom is more obligatory, along with what indicates the Self-sufficiency of the Agent and the perfection of His power and His knowledge of what [?] and more perfect than creating one of them. And if there were not for the people of tawhid except the proofs of the truth of the callers to it, and the luminous evidences with them—they are the means—while none of that exists for any denier of the sole Maker, that would be sufficient. How so, and there is nothing except that it testifies by its essence to its createdness, and that it was created by a Wise Creator? Were it not for the obstinacy of the atheists by what they claimed of the eternity of entities, with no way for them to refer back except to blind imitation of one who is not proof, or to make his foolishness—and it is his inability to understand that a thing comes from nothing—as proof for him. And there is no doubt that each of them knows from himself ignorance of some things, then knowledge of them, and incapacity for some things, then power over them, and necessity for some things, then independence from them. So it is fitting for one described thus that he should not trust his opinion, nor does what he sees from his intellect's indication benefit him, given that he who attributes that to the natures that do not understand what is generated from and by them, and likewise the stars, or to a number of makers whose beginning was ignorance and blindness, or to blind imitation of sayings about the eternity of things as they are, which are contradictory and opposites— how can they have reason for these ignorant principles while they are their branches? Or how can they stop at the realities of things so that they claim wisdom or foolishness in something? And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 767

Commentary
This passage is a cumulative argument for the sufficiency of evidence for tawhid and a refutation of those who deny the Creator. Maturidi begins by noting that even if the people of tawhid had only the proofs of the truth of the callers and the clear evidences (the prophets and their signs), that alone would be enough to establish the truth, especially since the deniers have nothing comparable. He then intensifies: in fact, everything in existence testifies by its very essence to its being created and that it was created by a Wise Creator. The obstacle is atheists' obstinacy in claiming eternal entities without any evidence, relying only on blind imitation of people who are not proofs or on their own foolishness—their inability to conceive of something coming from nothing. Maturidi then points out that these deniers are internally inconsistent: each knows himself as ignorant of some things then later learns them, incapable of some things then becomes able, in need of some things then independent. So one with such a nature should not trust his own opinion, nor can his intellectual indications benefit him when his reasoning leads him to attribute everything to unthinking natures, stars, or multiple creators—all starting from ignorance—or to contradictory sayings about eternity. He asks: how can such people, being mere branches of these ignorant principles, claim to have reason or understand the realities of things to judge wisdom or foolishness? The passage ends with the affirmation that all power is from Allah.

Key Terms
منكرى الصانع
قدم الأعيان
تقليد
طبائع
نجوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Deniers have rational arguments for their position.
Correction: Maturidi shows they lack proof and rely on blind imitation or internal contradictions.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do deniers rely on for their claim of eternal entities?
A) Rational proof.
B) Blind imitation and their own inability.
C) Empirical observation.
D) Scriptural evidence.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi exposes the irrationality of denying the Creator by showing deniers rely on blind imitation, internal inconsistency, and poorly conceived alternatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief without evidence, simply because you inherited it or because it was easier than investigating? In what area of your life do you rely on blind imitation (taqlid) rather than genuine understanding, and what will you do to change that starting today?
?
Maturidi points out that deniers are inconsistent because they experience change—ignorance to knowledge, incapacity to power, need to independence—yet deny the Creator. Where in your own life do you see this same inconsistency: knowing that you depend on Allah for every breath, yet acting as if you are self-sufficient? What specific habit or attitude must you repent of this week?
?
The deniers' alternatives—natures, stars, or blind traditions—are described as 'ignorant principles.' Have you ever settled for a shallow explanation of the world because it was comfortable or convenient? What truth from this lesson will you hold onto when you are tempted to avoid the full weight of recognizing your Creator? Resolve now: I will not be like those who trust their own opinion without seeking Allah's guidance.

Food, Harms, and Medicine as Divine Instruction
Maturidi uses food and medicine as first examples of divinely taught knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hidden harms in food and the complexity of medicine point to a divine teacher beyond human reason.

Core Idea
The intricate knowledge of food and medicine cannot be self-discovered; it must be taught.

Maturidi begins his inductive argument by drawing attention to the most basic human need: food. He notes that food contains hidden harms and requires precise knowledge of limits and remedies, which humans could not have deduced on their own. The medical sciences, with their understanding of diverse temperaments and deadly poisons, further demonstrate that these fields originate from divine teaching, not human intellect.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario: food preparation
Read how food has both benefits and hidden harms. Maturidi points out that one must know the limit of consumption and the remedy if harmed.
2
Examine the medical sciences
Notice the mention of diverse natures and deadly poisons. Humans need to know the beneficial dose—something reason alone could not achieve.
This builds on the food example.
3
Reflect on why this indicates teaching
Ask yourself: could a human have figured out these complex relations without prior instruction? The commentary will clarify the logical conclusion.
The passage sets up the proof for later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصلح للاغتذاء ، على اختلاف ما جعل لصلاح ذلك . ثم جعل في الطعام أنواع الأذى مما يدفع إليه المنتفع إذا لم يحفظ حده ؛ لأنه ممن يعلم حد ذلك ، ثم دواه إن ضره بالقدر الذى به يدفع ضرره ثم علوم الطب مع تفاوت الطبائع واستعمال السموم القاتلة ؛ ليعرفوا قدر النافع مما يقوم معه البدن . ثم في أنواع الحرف التي بها قوام سترهم وكنتهم والوقاية لهم من الحر والبرد . ثم فيما خلقت لهم من الدواب الصعبة مما ليس يعلم المتأهل فيها أنها لأى منفعة خلقت ، ولا أنها لمنافعه خلقت أو لا ، ولا كيف يروضها ؛ إذ طبع كل منها النفار عما شي له ، حتى تنقاد وتخضع . ثم فى أنواع التجارات التي لا يقوم لهم دين ولا دنيا إلا بها . ثم ما فرق حوائجهم في البلدان ، الذي ليس في طبعهم ولا في عقولهم ما يدلهم أو يبين لهم في كل حاجة أنها أين تطلب ، ثم فى معرفة طرقها ؛ إذ ليس في العقل ما يدل على مكانها ولا على طرقها . ثم فى تعرف الألسن التي بها قام النفس للمعاش وفهم المعاد ، ثم تعرف الأسماء التى لولا هى ما فهمت حاجة ولا أمكن أحدا معرفة موضعها . ثم في وجوه أسباب التناسل ، وفى معرفة تربية الصغار . ثم فى العلم بتدبير أغذية ما ليس يتطرق ، ثم بظهور - بعلم الخلق بعضهم من بعض – جميع ما ذكرت من الألسن والأسماء والحرف والطب والصناعات كلها وطرق البلدان ورياضة الدواب وكينية استعمالها وجميع ما ذكرت هو الدليل البين أن أصولها تعليم وإشارة لا استخراج العقول ، والله الموفق
... [food] suitable for nourishment, according to the different things made for its suitability. Then He placed in food types of harm that drive the one who benefits to [avoid] if he does not preserve its limit, because he is from those who know its limit, then its remedy if it harms him by the amount by which its harm is repelled. Then the medical sciences with the diversity of natures and the use of deadly poisons, so that they may know the extent of benefit with which the body thrives. Then in the types of crafts by which their covering and protection from heat and cold are sustained. Then in what was created for them of unruly beasts, which the rational person does not know whether they were created for some benefit, or whether they were created for their benefit or not, nor how to tame them, since each of them is innately averse to what is intended for it, until they submit and obey. Then in the types of trades without which neither their religion nor their worldly life would be established. Then how their needs are distributed in the lands, such that their nature and minds do not guide them or show them in every need where it is sought, then in knowing the paths to them, since there is nothing in the intellect that indicates their location or their paths. Then in learning the languages by which the self is sustained for livelihood and understanding of the hereafter, then learning the names without which no need could be understood nor could anyone know its place. Then in the aspects of the causes of reproduction, and in knowing the upbringing of children. Then in the knowledge of managing foods that are not normally accessible, then by the appearance—through the knowledge of some creatures from others—of all that I mentioned of languages, names, crafts, medicine, all industries, paths to lands, training of beasts, and the manner of their use, all that I have mentioned is the clear proof that their origins are teaching and indication, not extraction from intellects. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 779

Commentary
This passage is an inductive proof from the diversity of human needs and acquired skills to the conclusion that their ultimate origin is divine teaching, not human reason alone. Maturidi lists a series of domains: food preparation (with its hidden harms and remedies), medicine (with its understanding of beneficial doses and deadly poisons despite differing temperaments), crafts (for clothing and shelter), taming of wild animals (which are naturally averse to humans), trade (essential for religious and worldly life), distribution of needs across lands (which reason cannot locate), knowledge of roads, languages (without which communication and commerce fail), names (necessary for conveying needs), reproduction and child-rearing, and management of unusual foods. In each case, humans could not have discovered these by their own intellect; rather, they had to be taught. Therefore, Maturidi concludes, all these sciences are evidence that their foundations come from teaching and divine indication, not from intellectual extraction. He ends with 'And God is the grantor of success.'

Key Terms
الطعام
الطب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi denies human reasoning in all matters.
Correction: He only denies that humans could have discovered these complex practical sciences by intellect alone, not that reason is useless.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the knowledge of food harms and medicine demonstrate?
A) Humans are naturally wise
B) It is obtained through trial and error
C) Its origin is divine teaching, not reason alone
D) It is unnecessary for life

Key Takeaway
The knowledge of food and medicine is too precise for human reason to have originated it alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you ate something without considering its potential harm. How does this lesson challenge your assumption that you fully understand what is beneficial for you? Will you now pause to reflect on the hidden knowledge behind everyday things?
?
If the intricate knowledge of food and medicine must be taught by Allah, what does that imply about your responsibility to seek that knowledge from His revelation? How often do you turn to the Quran and Sunnah for guidance on physical well-being?

Crafts, Animals, Trade, and Roads: Signs of Teaching
Maturidi lists crafts, animal taming, trade, and geography as further examples of divinely taught knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of crafts, tamed animals, trade, and knowledge of roads all point to divine teaching.

Core Idea
Human skills in crafts, animal taming, and navigation are taught, not reasoned.

Maturidi continues his enumeration by turning to the practical arts that sustain human life: crafts for clothing and shelter, taming of wild beasts, trade for religion and worldly life, and knowledge of how needs are distributed across lands and how to travel. In each case, the necessary knowledge exceeds what unaided reason could produce.

Guided Path
1
List the examples in this passage
Identify: crafts, taming beasts, trade, distribution of needs, and road knowledge.
2
Notice the common thread
Each example involves knowledge that is not instinctively known or deducible by reason alone.
E.g., no one would naturally know how to tame a wild animal.
3
Connect to the larger argument
Realize that these are presented as evidence that their origin is teaching, not intellect.
The commentary explains the logical force.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You possess a skill today—whether typing, driving, cooking, or trading—that you treat as ordinary. But before it was taught, you were utterly incapable. When you exercise that skill, do you consciously thank the One who taught it, or do you absorb the credit? This lesson asks: what would your life be if every human had to reinvent the wheel? Write down one skill you use daily and pray: 'Allah, You taught me this; keep me grateful and humble.'
?
Maturidi says that even taming a horse requires a knowledge that the horse is innately averse to. Have you ever considered that your ability to work with animals, or even to coexist peacefully with people of different temperaments, is a gift of divine teaching? Think of a difficult person in your life. Can you see that the patience or wisdom to deal with them is not from your own nature but from Allah's instruction? What will you ask Him to teach you today?
?
The passage notes that trade routes and the distribution of needs are known only through teaching, not intellect. When you plan your future—your career, your finances, your family—do you rely on your own maps, or do you seek directions from the One who laid the paths? This week, commit one major decision to prayer and consciously acknowledge that without His teaching, you would be lost.

Languages, Names, and the Final Proof of Divine Teaching
Maturidi lists languages, names, and other domains, then declares they prove divine teaching.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how languages, names, reproduction, child-rearing, and the summation of all examples prove that these sciences are from teaching, not reason.

Core Idea
Languages and names are foundational; their origin must be divine teaching, as reason cannot generate them.

Maturidi culminates his inductive argument by mentioning languages, names, reproduction, child-rearing, and managing unusual foods. He then declares that all the examples listed—from food to medicine to languages—collectively prove that their origins are teaching and indication, not intellectual extraction. This lesson presents the concluding demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Read the final examples
Note the mention of languages, names, reproduction, and child-rearing.
2
Observe the concluding statement
Maturidi explicitly states that all these together are 'the clear proof' that their origins are teaching and indication.
This is the culmination of the entire passage.
3
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why the diversity and specificity of these fields make it impossible that they are from unaided reason.

Then how their needs are distributed in the lands, such that their nature and minds do not guide them or show them in every need where it is sought, then in knowing the paths to them, since there is nothing in the intellect that indicates their location or their paths. Then in learning the languages by which the self is sustained for livelihood and understanding of the hereafter, then learning the names without which no need could be understood nor could anyone know its place. Then in the aspects of the causes of reproduction, and in knowing the upbringing of children. Then in the knowledge of managing foods that are not normally accessible, then by the appearance—through the knowledge of some creatures from others—of all that I mentioned of languages, names, crafts, medicine, all industries, paths to lands, training of beasts, and the manner of their use, all that I have mentioned is the clear proof that their origins are teaching and indication, not extraction from intellects. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 779

Commentary
This passage is an inductive proof from the diversity of human needs and acquired skills to the conclusion that their ultimate origin is divine teaching, not human reason alone. Maturidi lists a series of domains: food preparation (with its hidden harms and remedies), medicine (with its understanding of beneficial doses and deadly poisons despite differing temperaments), crafts (for clothing and shelter), taming of wild animals (which are naturally averse to humans), trade (essential for religious and worldly life), distribution of needs across lands (which reason cannot locate), knowledge of roads, languages (without which communication and commerce fail), names (necessary for conveying needs), reproduction and child-rearing, and management of unusual foods. In each case, humans could not have discovered these by their own intellect; rather, they had to be taught. Therefore, Maturidi concludes, all these sciences are evidence that their foundations come from teaching and divine indication, not from intellectual extraction. He ends with 'And God is the grantor of success.'

Key Terms
الألسن
الأسماء
تعليم
إشارة
استخراج العقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Maturidi's argument is disproved by modern discoveries made through reason.
Correction: Maturidi's point is about the origin of foundational knowledge, not that reason cannot later develop it; the first origin is divine teaching.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude about all the examples he listed?
A) They are from human experience
B) They are from trial and error
C) Their origins are teaching and indication, not extraction from intellects
D) They are from nature

Key Takeaway
The cumulative weight of diverse human needs and sciences proves their origin is divine teaching.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on language every day. Have you ever truly considered that the very ability to speak and name things is a gift from Allah, not something you invented? How does this change your gratitude?
?
The passage lists many complex human activities like medicine and child-rearing. Can you honestly claim that you could have figured out how to raise a child or cure a disease purely by your own reasoning? What does this say about your dependence on Allah's teaching?
?
Maturidi says 'all that I have mentioned is the clear proof that their origins are teaching and indication, not extraction from intellects.' This means every time you use a language, a skill, or knowledge, you are seeing a sign of Allah. Will you start recognizing these signs in your daily life, and how will that transform your awareness of Him?

From Mutual Reliance to Divine Guidance
Maturidi argues from human mutual reliance to the necessity of a truthful divine guide.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the universal human practice of seeking help from others demonstrates the rational necessity of a truthful adviser in religion, ultimately pointing to divine teaching.

Core Idea
Human dependence on others proves we need a truthful guide from God.

Maturidi begins by pointing to an obvious fact of human life: when faced with difficulties, people naturally turn to others for advice and learning. This common practice reveals a deep truth about human nature—that our intellects alone are insufficient for all our needs. In this lesson, we explore how this observation leads to a rational argument for the necessity of a truthful adviser (nāsih sādiq) in matters of religion, and how all instruction ultimately traces back to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repetitive structure emphasizing reliance on others.
2
Identify the core observation
Look for the phrase about people turning to each other during calamities. What does this reveal about human self-sufficiency?
3
Follow the logical inference
From the observation, Maturidi concludes it is rationally necessary to turn to a truthful adviser. How does he justify this step?
He reasons: if for worldly matters we need help, then for religion even more so.
4
Notice the generalization
Maturidi then states that all knowledge—including religion, magic, and warfare—is derived from oral instruction. Why is this important for his argument?
It shows that all instruction ultimately comes from a source, which must be divine.
5
Grasp the final point
The passage ends by stating that the first teaching is from the All-Knowing, All-Wise. How does this complete the argument for prophethood?
It establishes that reliable knowledge of religion must come from God through truthful messengers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا مع المعروف الموجود من فزع بعض إلى بعض عند النوائب وما يحزنهم من الأمور المهمة للاستعانة برأيهم والصدور عن مشورتهم بما عندهم فضل في العالم . ثم تعليم فنون الآداب، ثم قيام كل بما يستفيد أنواع العلم من درس الكتب والاستماع إلى الحكماء، فدل ذلك أنهم لم يروا بعقولهم كفاية عن الاستعانة وآداء حاجاتهم جميعا ، لزم فى العقل الفزع إلى ناصح صدوق ، وذلك ظن الخلق بأولئك ، إنه وصل إليهم العلوم على ألسن هؤلاء . فعلى ذلك أمر الدين والدنيا ، وعلى ذلك علم السحر يعتبر جواهر الأشياء بأنواع المعالجات وعلوم محاربة أعداء الدين والأموال كلها مستفادة فى الأمر الظاهر من الألسن وما عنها يوجد ، فأول ذلك تعليم يكون من العليم الحكيم
This, along with the well-known reality of people turning to one another during calamities and distressing important matters, to seek help from their opinions and defer to their counsel, due to what they possess of excellence in the world. Then the teaching of the arts of refinement, then each person engaging in learning types of knowledge from studying books and listening to the wise. That indicates that they did not consider their intellects sufficient without seeking help to fulfill all their needs. Therefore, it is rationally necessary to turn to a truthful adviser. And that is the assumption of people concerning those [teachers], that knowledge reached them on the tongues of these. So likewise are the matters of religion and this world, and likewise the science of magic considers the essences of things through various treatments and the sciences of combating enemies of religion and wealth—all are derived, in the apparent matter, from tongues and from what is found through them. So the first of that is a teaching from the All-Knowing, All-Wise.
Source pages p. 779

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous argument by observing a universal human practice: people turn to others for help with worldly difficulties, because they do not find their own intellects sufficient. This need for counsel and learning (through books, listening to the wise) shows that humans naturally rely on external sources. Thus, for the most important matter—religion—it is even more rational to seek a truthful adviser (ناصح صدوق). Maturidi then points out that all fields, even magic and warfare, are derived from oral instruction. Therefore, all guidance ultimately originates from a teaching by the All-Knowing, All-Wise (God). The structure is: observed human behavior → rational necessity → religious parallel → divine origin.

Key Terms
فزع
ناصح صدوق
تعليم
عليم حكيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi's argument encourages blind imitation (taqlid) of any teacher.
Correction: Maturidi specifically calls for a truthful adviser (nāsih sādiq), implying the need for a reliable and truthful source, not blind acceptance of anyone.

Checkpoint
What does the universal practice of people turning to others for worldly needs imply, according to Maturidi?
A) That people are naturally independent
B) That a truthful adviser is rationally necessary for religion
C) That wisdom comes from solitude
D) That intellect alone is sufficient for all needs

Key Takeaway
Human reliance on others for guidance proves the necessity of a truthful divine messenger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a major life decision, do you first turn to God's guidance (through His Book and His Messenger) or to people's advice? What does your instinct reveal about your reliance on divine guidance?
?
This lesson shows that even learning worldly skills requires teachers. But do you actively seek knowledge of religion from trustworthy sources, or do you rely on your own opinions? What step can you take today to ensure your faith is built on truthful teaching?
?
Maturidi argues that human intellect is insufficient. In which area of your deen have you relied too much on your own reasoning instead of turning to the Qur'an and Sunnah? What will you do to correct that?

The Necessity of Reason to Avoid Circularity
Maturidi argues that reason is necessary to avoid circularity in proving origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reason is necessary as a starting point for proving the origination of bodies, and how rejecting reason leads to circular reasoning.

Core Idea
Revelation cannot prove its own truthfulness without reason.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi engages with a hypothetical opponent who claims to rely solely on revelation to prove that bodies are created. Maturidi exposes the circularity in this approach and demonstrates that reason must be the foundation for such proofs. This lesson walks through his logical dismantling of that position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recognize that Maturidi is addressing an opponent (likely a Christian) who might use revelation as the sole proof for the origination of bodies.
This is a dialectical setting typical of kalam.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent says: 'I rely on revelation (al-samʿ).' Note that this is the starting point of the argument.
3
Follow Maturidi's counter-question
Maturidi asks: 'What proves the truthfulness of that revelation?' Pause before reading the answer.
This question breaks the self-referential chain.
4
See the circularity
If the opponent answers 'the origination of bodies,' then both statements depend on each other: origination from revelation, and revelation from origination. This cuts off knowledge.
The Arabic uses 'fānqaṭaʿa sabīlu maʿrifatin' – the path of knowledge is cut off.
5
Conclude with reason
The only way out is to affirm reason (al-ʿaql) as the foundation. Once reason is accepted, it applies also to Jesus, showing he is created.
This argument establishes the priority of rational proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نتكلم فى دليل حدث الأجسام، فإن صيره العقل لزمه في ذلك. الأشياء فإن قال: السمع، قيل: ودليل صدق المسموع ما هو؟ فإن قال: حدوث الأشياء، يصير حدوث الأشياء لا يعلم إلا بالسمع، وصدق لا يعلم إلا بحدوث الأشياء، فانقطع سبيل معرفة فيها إلا أن يقر بالعقل فيلزمه ذلك في المسيح.
Then we speak about the proof of the origination of bodies. If he makes reason (al-ʿaql) the determinant, it becomes necessary for him in that matter. As for things, if he says: [I rely on] revelation (al-samʿ), it is said: And what is the proof of the truthfulness of what is heard? If he says: the origination of things, then the origination of things is not known except through revelation, and [the truthfulness of] revelation is not known except through the origination of things, so the path of knowledge in these matters is cut off unless he affirms reason, and then that becomes necessary for him concerning the Messiah.
Source pages p. 812

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument about epistemological foundations. Maturidi presents a hypothetical objection from a Christian interlocutor who claims to rely on revelation (al-samʿ) to prove the origination of bodies. The author then asks what proves the truthfulness of that revelation. If the answer is 'the origination of bodies', a circularity results: origination is known only through revelation, and revelation is known only through origination. This cuts off all knowledge unless one concedes the necessity of reason (al-ʿaql). Once reason is admitted, the same rational proof applies to Jesus, showing he is originated. The argument is classic in kalam for establishing the priority of rational proof over transmitted proof.

Key Terms
دليل حدث الأجسام
السمع
العقل
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage argues against the validity of revelation itself.
Correction: It argues against using revelation as a self-referential proof without rational grounding, not against revelation per se.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude if someone tries to prove the origination of bodies solely through revelation?
A) Revelation is sufficient and no further proof is needed.
B) Reason must be used to avoid circular reasoning.
C) The Messiah is not subject to origination.
D) The proof fails and we must rely on faith alone.

Key Takeaway
Reason is the necessary foundation for proving the origination of bodies; without it, arguments become circular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accept a religious teaching, do you ever stop to ask what makes that teaching trustworthy? Or do you rely on it simply because it comes from a source you've been taught to trust? How would you justify your belief if someone pressed you on the foundation?
?
This passage reveals that without reason, our reliance on revelation can become circular. In your own faith journey, have you ever encountered a belief that seemed to stand on nothing but itself? What would it take for you to examine that belief rationally rather than defensively?
?
Maturidi insists that reason must be affirmed to break the circle. But affirming reason means being willing to think critically about even your most cherished convictions. Are you ready to let reason test your faith, even if it makes you uncomfortable? What is one belief you are afraid to examine too closely?
