The Divine Test Through Hardship and Ease
Maturidi explains the divine test through hardship and ease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God tested humans with hardship and ease, and how this test engages both intellect and action.

Core Idea
The divine test uses difficulty and ease to call forth human effort.

Maturidi begins by explaining the divine test. He states that God tested humans because their intellects were unwilling to accept certain things and they naturally inclined to good. The test consists of two pairs: difficult and easy, hard and soft. Without this test, humans would not strive. This passage grounds the necessity of effort in the path of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Let the words sink in before analysis.
Notice the parallel structure of 'العسير واليسير' and 'السهل والصعب'.
2
Identify the reason for the test
The passage says: 'إذ أبت عقولهم احتمال أمثالهم' – because their intellects refused to bear the like of those things.
What are 'those things'? Likely the moral burdens of free will.
3
Understand the two matters of the test
The test is made of two pairs: difficult/easy, hard/soft. This shows divine wisdom in balancing challenge and relief.
4
Ponder the consequence without trial
Without trial, humans would undertake both equally – meaning no differentiation of effort. The test distinguishes the sincere from the lazy.
This connects to the purpose of prophethood and revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم امتحنهم، إذ أبت عقولهم احتمال أمثالهم، ورغبت في محاسن ما حُسن من الأعمال واجتناب ما قبح من ذلك. ثم جعل ما فيه محنتهم أمرين: العسير واليسير، والسهل والصعب؛ بلا محنة يتعاطون الأمرين جميعا؛ لما إليه مرجع ما أقدموا عليه وامتنعوا. وعلى ذلك جعل الأسباب التي بها التوصل لهم إلى الأصل الذي به يرتقى إلى كل درجة وينال كل فضيلة، وهو العلم على وجهين: على الظاهر البين والخفى المستور ليتفاضل بذلك أوائل العقلي على قدر تفاضلهم في الاجتهاد واحتمال ما كرهته الطباع ونفرت عنه النفس. وعلى ذلك جعل سبيله قسمين: أحدهما العيان الذي هو أخص الأسباب، وهو الذي ليس معه جهل، ليكون أصلا لما خفى منه، والثاني السمع الذي عن دلالة الأعيان يعرف صدقه وكذبه. ثم جعل السمع قسمين: محكم ومتشابه ومفسر ومبهم، ليبين منتهى المعارف من الكف فيما يجب ذلك والإقدام فيما يلزمه، ومن حمل المبهم على المفسر، لزم المحكم وعرض المتشابه عليه ما أمكن أن يكون ما فيه مما يلزم تعرفه ومما إليه حاجة بأهل المحنة، أو ترك الخوض في ذلك فيما أمكن الغنا عن تعرف حقيقة ما فيه، فيكون محنة الوقوف.
Then He tested them, when their intellects refused to bear the like of those things, and they desired the beauties of what was good of deeds and avoided what was ugly of that. Then He made what is in their trial into two matters: the difficult and the easy, and the hard and the soft; without trial they would undertake both matters equally; because to Him is the return of what they advanced upon and refrained from. And upon that He made the means by which they attain to the principle by which one rises to every degree and attains every virtue, and that is knowledge, in two aspects: manifest and clear, and hidden and concealed, so that the first ones of intellect may excel according to their degree of striving and enduring what natures dislike and the soul disinclines from. And upon that He made its path two divisions: one is direct observation (al-‘iyān), which is the most specific of means, and with which there is no ignorance, to be a foundation for what is hidden from it; and the second is hearing (al-sam‘), by which its truth and falsehood are known through the indication of observable things. Then He made hearing two divisions: unambiguous (muḥkam) and ambiguous (mutashābih), and explained (mufassar) and obscure (mubham), to show the ultimate limits of knowledge—to refrain where it is necessary and to proceed where it is required. And whoever carries the obscure onto the explained commits to the unambiguous and presents the ambiguous to it as much as possible, that what is in it may be from what it is necessary to know and from what the people of the trial need, or to refrain from delving into that when it is possible to dispense with knowledge of its reality, so that it becomes a trial of stopping.
Source pages p. 821

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the divine test and the means of knowledge. Maturidi begins by stating that God tested humans because their intellects were unwilling to bear certain things, and they naturally inclined to good deeds and avoided evil. He then explains that God made the test consist of two pairs: difficult and easy, hard and soft, so that humans would engage both with effort, not passively. The ultimate goal is to attain virtue through knowledge (al-‘ilm). God made knowledge two types: manifest (ẓāhir) and hidden (khafī), so that people of intellect (awā’il al-‘aqli) could excel according to their striving and endurance against natural aversion. The path to knowledge is also two: direct observation (al-‘iyān) which is infallible and serves as the foundation for hidden knowledge, and hearing (al-sam‘) which requires verification through observable signs. Hearing is further divided into muḥkam (clear) and mutashābih (ambiguous), and mufassar (explained) and mubham (obscure). This division is meant to guide action: when the obscure is interpreted in light of the explained, one adheres to the unambiguous and submits the ambiguous to it as far as possible, either pursuing necessary knowledge or abstaining when the reality is not needed. This abstention itself becomes a trial of stopping (mihnat al-wuqūf).

Key Terms
محنة (miḥna)
العسير (al-‘asīr)
اليسير (al-yasīr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The divine test is only about hardship and suffering.
Correction: The test includes both difficulty and ease, making it a balanced trial to bring out human effort.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did God test humans?
A) Because they were disobedient
B) Because their intellects refused to bear certain things and they desired good deeds
C) To punish them for their sins
D) To make life difficult for them

Key Takeaway
The divine test is designed to bring out human effort through a mix of difficulty and ease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have experienced both ease and hardship. How does knowing that both are part of Allah's test change the way you approach your current difficulty? What effort are you withholding because you think only ease matters?
?
Without the trial, you would treat both matters equally. In what area of your life are you treating ease and hardship as if they don't require different responses from you? What would it mean to intentionally choose the harder path for the sake of Allah today?
?
Your intellect naturally inclines toward good deeds and avoids evil, yet you still face trials. What does this tell you about your own responsibility to act on that inclination even when it is hard? What one step will you take this week to advance toward a good deed you have been avoiding?

Two Aspects of Knowledge: Manifest and Hidden
Maturidi explains the two aspects of knowledge: manifest and hidden.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that knowledge comes in two aspects – manifest and hidden – and that excellence is achieved through striving and enduring discomfort.

Core Idea
Knowledge is both manifest and hidden, requiring effort to attain the hidden.

After discussing the test, Maturidi turns to the means of attaining virtue: knowledge. He explains that God made knowledge two-fold: manifest and hidden. This division allows people of intellect to excel according to their striving and their ability to endure what nature dislikes. This passage sets the stage for the two paths to knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Carefully read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the two aspects of knowledge.
This passage is about the nature of knowledge itself.
2
Identify the two aspects
The passage says 'العلم على وجهين: على الظاهر البين والخفى المستور' – knowledge is on two aspects: manifest/clear and hidden/concealed.
3
Understand the purpose of this division
The purpose is so that 'أوائل العقلي' (the first ones of intellect) may excel according to their degree of striving and enduring what natures dislike.
This implies that hidden knowledge requires more effort.
4
Connect to the divine test
This division is part of the test: effort in seeking hidden knowledge is rewarded.
This ties back to the test of difficulty and ease.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الظاهر البين (al-ẓāhir al-bayyin)
الخفى المستور (al-khafī al-mastūr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All knowledge is equally accessible without effort.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes manifest (easy) and hidden (requires effort) knowledge, making effort a key factor.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did God make knowledge into two aspects?
A) To confuse people
B) So that people of intellect may excel according to their striving and endurance
C) To hide truth from the ignorant
D) To make knowledge impossible

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is divided into manifest and hidden to reward those who strive and endure discomfort in seeking it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a hidden truth in your faith you have avoided because it demanded too much mental or emotional effort from you? What would it cost you to truly pursue it today?
?
When did you last deliberately endure something your soul dislikes in order to gain deeper understanding of Allah? If never, what is holding you back?
?
Which 'manifest knowledge' have you been satisfied with, while secretly knowing that the hidden depths require you to step out of your comfort zone? Will you take one concrete step this week to seek it?

Method of Learning
beginner
4 min
Two Paths to Knowledge: Direct Observation and Hearing
Maturidi enumerates the two paths to knowledge: direct observation and hearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two paths to knowledge according to Maturidi: direct observation (al-‘iyān) which is infallible, and hearing (al-sam‘) which requires verification through observable signs.

Core Idea
Direct observation is infallible; hearing needs verification by observable signs.

Maturidi now specifies the two paths to knowledge: direct observation and hearing. Direct observation is the most specific means, free from ignorance, and serves as the foundation for hidden knowledge. Hearing, on the other hand, derives its truth from the indication of observable things. This establishes a hierarchy where senses are primary and revelation is secondary but valid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the two divisions: 'العيان' and 'السمع'.
This is a key classification in Maturidi's epistemology.
2
Understand 'al-‘iyān' (direct observation)
Maturidi says it is 'أخص الأسباب' (the most specific means) and 'ليس معه جهل' (without ignorance). It is the foundation for hidden knowledge.
This refers to empirical observation, which is certain.
3
Understand 'al-sam‘' (hearing)
Hearing is the second path; its truth and falsehood are known through the indication of observable things ('دلالة الأعيان').
This means revelation must be consistent with observable reality.
4
Reflect on the hierarchy
Observation is primary; hearing depends on it for verification. This aligns with the Islamic principle that reason and revelation are harmonious.
This prevents blind acceptance of transmitted reports.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم امتحنهم، إذ أبت عقولهم احتمال أمثالهم، ورغبت في محاسن ما حُسن من الأعمال واجتناب ما قبح من ذلك. ثم جعل ما فيه محنتهم أمرين: العسير واليسير، والسهل والصعب؛ بلا محنة يتعاطون الأمرين جميعا؛ لما إليه مرجع ما أقدموا عليه وامتنعوا. وعلى ذلك جعل الأسباب التي بها التوصل لهم إلى الأصل الذي به يرتقى إلى كل درجة وينال كل فضيلة، وهو العلم على وجهين: على الظاهر البين والخفى المستور ليتفاضل بذلك أوائل العقلي على قدر تفاضلهم في الاجتهاد واحتمال ما كرهته الطباع ونفرت عنه النفس. وعلى ذلك جعل سبيله قسمين: أحدهما العيان الذي هو أخص الأسباب، وهو الذي ليس معه جهل، ليكون أصلا لما خفى منه، والثاني السمع الذي عن دلالة الأعيان يعرف صدقه وكذبه. ثم جعل السمع قسمين: محكم ومتشابه ومفسر ومبهم، ليبين منتهى المعارف من الكف فيما يجب ذلك والإقدام فيما يلزمه، ومن حمل المبهم على المفسر، لزم المحكم وعرض المتشابه عليه ما أمكن أن يكون ما فيه مما يلزم تعرفه ومما إليه حاجة بأهل المحنة، أو ترك الخوض في ذلك فيما أمكن الغنا عن تعرف حقيقة ما فيه، فيكون محنة الوقوف.
Then He tested them, when their intellects refused to bear the like of those things, and they desired the beauties of what was good of deeds and avoided what was ugly of that. Then He made what is in their trial into two matters: the difficult and the easy, and the hard and the soft; without trial they would undertake both matters equally; because to Him is the return of what they advanced upon and refrained from. And upon that He made the means by which they attain to the principle by which one rises to every degree and attains every virtue, and that is knowledge, in two aspects: manifest and clear, and hidden and concealed, so that the first ones of intellect may excel according to their degree of striving and enduring what natures dislike and the soul disinclines from. And upon that He made its path two divisions: one is direct observation (al-‘iyān), which is the most specific of means, and with which there is no ignorance, to be a foundation for what is hidden from it; and the second is hearing (al-sam‘), by which its truth and falsehood are known through the indication of observable things. Then He made hearing two divisions: unambiguous (muḥkam) and ambiguous (mutashābih), and explained (mufassar) and obscure (mubham), to show the ultimate limits of knowledge—to refrain where it is necessary and to proceed where it is required. And whoever carries the obscure onto the explained commits to the unambiguous and presents the ambiguous to it as much as possible, that what is in it may be from what it is necessary to know and from what the people of the trial need, or to refrain from delving into that when it is possible to dispense with knowledge of its reality, so that it becomes a trial of stopping.
Source pages p. 821

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the divine test and the means of knowledge. Maturidi begins by stating that God tested humans because their intellects were unwilling to bear certain things, and they naturally inclined to good deeds and avoided evil. He then explains that God made the test consist of two pairs: difficult and easy, hard and soft, so that humans would engage both with effort, not passively. The ultimate goal is to attain virtue through knowledge (al-‘ilm). God made knowledge two types: manifest (ẓāhir) and hidden (khafī), so that people of intellect (awā’il al-‘aqli) could excel according to their striving and endurance against natural aversion. The path to knowledge is also two: direct observation (al-‘iyān) which is infallible and serves as the foundation for hidden knowledge, and hearing (al-sam‘) which requires verification through observable signs. Hearing is further divided into muḥkam (clear) and mutashābih (ambiguous), and mufassar (explained) and mubham (obscure). This division is meant to guide action: when the obscure is interpreted in light of the explained, one adheres to the unambiguous and submits the ambiguous to it as far as possible, either pursuing necessary knowledge or abstaining when the reality is not needed. This abstention itself becomes a trial of stopping (mihnat al-wuqūf).

Key Terms
العيان (al-‘iyān)
السمع (al-sam‘)
دلالة الأعيان (dalālat al-a‘yān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Revelation (sam‘) can contradict observable reality and must be accepted blindly.
Correction: Maturidi states that the truth of hearing is known through observable things, meaning revelation must align with reason and empirical evidence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how is the truth of 'hearing' (al-sam‘) known?
A) Through blind faith
B) Through the indication of observable things (dalālat al-a‘yān)
C) Through consensus of scholars
D) Through personal intuition

Key Takeaway
Direct observation is the foundation of certainty; hearing is validated by its consistency with observable reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely more on what you have heard from others than on what you can observe in your own life? Have you ever accepted a religious claim without checking if it aligns with the observable reality Allah has placed before you? Will you commit to making direct observation—your own senses and reason—a foundation for your faith starting today?
?
Maturidi says hearing (revelation) is known to be true through observable things. Can you name one observable sign in your daily life that confirms the truth of a specific Islamic teaching? If not, what might that say about the strength of your certainty?
?
If direct observation is the foundation for knowledge of the hidden, then your knowledge of Allah begins with what you see in creation. When did you last consciously reflect on an observable part of the universe—a sunset, a cell, a moral law—and let it increase your certainty in the unseen? Commit to doing that this week and see if it changes your heart.
